“ The Argonaut is the most readable American news-
paper.”—The Bulletin.
The Argonaut publishes bright weekly letters from
New York, Paris, and London, from skilled correspondents
who are in touch with the intense life of those great cities.
It publishes frequent letters from other points in the
old world, and from ihe older world of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
describe their peculiar tinge, 'out an idea may be gathered
from this, our standing injunction to our contributors:
“ Whatever you are, never he commonplace.” The re-
sult is that the Argonaut short stories have an individu-
ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly re\ tew of the literary movement. Brief notes on
books, rather than lengthy reviews, together with notes
upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader well
up with the literary gossip of the day.
For sale at The Bulletin Office after the arrival of the
American mail ; price, 6d. ; posted, 7d. ; mailed to any
Australasian add cess for £1 per annum and postage.

THE RED PAGE.
I.
As one who, in a desert wandering
Alone and faint beneath a pitiless sky,
And doubting in his heart if he shall bring
His bones back to his kindred or there die,
Finds at his feet' a .treasure suddenly
Such as would make him for all time a king,
And so forgets his fears and with keen eye
Falls to a-counting each new precious thing,
—So was I when you told me yesterday
The tale of your dear love. Awhile I stood
Astonished and enraptured, and my heart
Began to count its treasures. Now dismay
Steals back my joy, and terror chills my blood,
And I remember only “ we must part.”
.. . ■ . ii.
Why practise, love, this small economy
Of your heart’s favours ?* Can you keep a kiss
To be enjoyed in age ? and would the free
Expense of pleasure leave you penniless ?
Nay, nay. Be wise. Believe me, pleasure is
A gambler’s token, only gold to-day.
The day of love is short, and every bliss
Untasted now is a bliss thrown away.
’Twere pitiful, in truth, such treasures should
Lie by like miser’s crusts till mouldy grown.
Think you the hand of age will be less rude
In touching your sweet bosom than my own ?
Alas, what matter, when our heads are grey,
Whether you loved or did not love to-day ?
hi.
I ask for love who famished am in plenty,
Not scorning the dear manna of your tears,
But being vexed with that too froward twenty
Which heads the sum of my reb dlious years.
My soul is fallen “ in lust of cucumbers,
Of fish, of melons,” through its long abstaining.
Unworthy Egypt yet enslaves my fears.
Ah, love, I thirst-, but not for heaven’s raining.
Why speak to me, alas, of heavenly joys
Who ask for joys of earth these cannot cheat ?
What are these clouds, these pillars of fire to me ?
The wilderness is long. Youth cannot be
For ever fed-on these unnatural toys,
And needs must murmur if it have not meat.
IV.
What is this prate of friendship? Kings dis-
crowned
Go forth, not citizens but outlawed men.
If love has ceased to give a loyal sound,
Let there at least be silence. Once again
I go, proscribed, exiled, dominionless
Out of your coasts, yet scorning to complain.
I grudge not your allegiance nor my bliss,
I yield the pleasure as I keep the pain.
Rebellion’s rights are limited though strong.
The right to take gives not the right to give.
Mine were the sole right and prerogative
To give a title or forgive a wrong.
This gift of friendship was not yours to bring.
As I have lived in love I still will live
Or die, if needs fhust, and without reprieve,
Your lover yet and kingdomless a king.
v.
I love not thy perfections. When I hear
Thy beauty blazoned, and the common tongue
Cheapening with vulgar praise a lip, an ear,
A cheek that I have prayed to ; when among
The loud world’s gods my god is noised and sung,
Her wit applauded, even her taste, her dress,
Her each dear hidden marvel lightly flung
At the world’s feet and stripped to nakedness—
Then I despise thy beauty utterly,
Crying, “Be these your gods, O Israel! ”
And I remember that on such a day
I found thee with eyes bleared and cheeks all pale,
And lips that trembled to a voiceless cry,
And that thy bosom in my bosom lay.
VI.
We might, if you had willed, have conquered
heaven.
Once only in our lives before the gate
Of Paradise we stood, one fortunate even,
And gazed in sudden rapture through the grate.
And, while you stood astonished, I, our fate
Venturing, pushed the latch and found it free.
There stood the tree of knowledge fair and great
Beside the tree of life. One instant we
Stood in that happy garden, guardianless.
My hands already turned towards the tree,
And in another moment we had known
The taste of joy and immortality
And been ourselves as gods. But in distress
You thrust me back with supplicating arms
And eyes of terror, till the impatient sun
Had time to set and till the heavenly host
Rushed forth on.us with clarions and alarms
And cast us out for ever, blind and lost.
VII.
How loud the storm blew all that bitter night 1
The loosened ivy tapping on the pane
Woke me and woke, again and yet again,
Till I was full awake and sat upright.
I listened to the noises of the night,
And presently I heard, disguised yet plain,
A footstep on the stair which mounted light
Towards me, and my heart outbeat the rain.
I knew that it was you. I knew it even
Before the door, which by design ajar
Waited your coming, had disclosed my fate.
I felt a wind upon my face from heaven,
i felt the presence of a life. My hair
Was touched as by a spirit. Insensate
I drew you to liiy bosom. Ah, too late !
I clutched the darkness. There was nothing there.
VIII.
We may not meet, I could not for pride’s sake
Dissemble further, and I suffer pain,
A palpable distinct and physical ache,
When our eyes meet by accident, and when
I hear you talk in your pathetic strain
Which always moved me. Only yesterday,
As I was standing with a crowd of men
In the long corridor, you came my way
And chanced to stop, and thus by chance I heard
A score of phrases uttered in that sad
Half-suppliant voice which once my spirit stirred
To its foundations. Yet your theme was glad—
Strangers your hearers. What was in these spells
To move me still ? A trick and nothing else ?
IX.
You are not false, perhaps, as lovers say !
Meaning the act—Alas, that guilt was mine !
Nor, maybe, have you bowed at other shrine
Than the true god’s where first you learned tp
pray.
I know the idols round you. They are clay—
Mere Dagons to the courage half divine
Which bears you scat hless still thro’ sap and mine
And breach and storm upon your virgin way ?
Alas, I know your virtue ! But your heart,
How have you treated it ? I sometimes see,
When nights ai'e long, a vision chaste and true
Of pale pathetic eyes, which gaze on me
In loye and grief eternal. Then I start,
Crying aloud, and reach my arms to you.
x.
Farewell, then. It is finished. I forego
With this all right in you, even that of tears.
If I have spoken hardly, it will show
How much I loved you. With you disappears
A glory, a romance of many years.
What you may be henceforth I will not know.
The phantom of your presence on my fears
Is impotent at length for weal or woe.
Your past,.your present, all alike must fade
In a new land of dreams, where love is not.
Then kiss me and farewell. The choice is made
And we shall live to see the past forgot,
If not forgiven. See, I came to curse,
Yet stay to bless. I know not which is worse.
I %
These are some of the sonnets of Wilfrid Scawen
Blunt, who has reached the dignity, of a selected
edition with W. E. Henley and George Wyndham
for sponsors. ’the collection is welcome.; for
minor poets in five booklets are trying alike to
patience and to pocket.
* * * *
Blunt (lie looks it, shorn of the wilful, scornful
lames which seem subtly to index and to have in-
iuenced his character) has had definite status
since his “Love Sonnets of Proteus ” were issued
in ’81; and his subsequent work has only con-
firmed, not heightened, that status. His poetry
is unusually personal and peculiarly interesting ;
you gain from it in iiroportion as you bring.
Blunt is characteristically timid and melancholy ;
he has forced himself to'be courageous and cheer-
ful, conquering a body which ill seconded his
brain and desires. For all men of his tempera-
ment or near it, his verse has the force of a private
history, a self-revelation. It recites Blunt’s ex-
perience and criticism of life; it embalms his
emotions recollected in tranquillity. And there-
fore (partly) it is not Poetry, or not the highest
kind of Poetry.
• * • *
In excusing Blunt in the preface, Henley ac-
cuses him. He sees clearly that Blunt is no
artist; that he has small sense of words and no
imagination ; that his pictures are photographs,
accurate and strong and attractive, but never
lifted (to use meaningless words from which a
meaning may be gathered) through the mere
human to the pure divine. To justify a defence
which, to his own mind, seems not thoroughly
justified, Henley makes a division between the
Poetry of Life and the Poetry of Art; and, rang-
ing (quite properly) Blunt’s verses in the first
class, he seeks to establish their claim to be
as high or as valuable as some verses in the second
class.
* * * »
It is always difficult to speak truth of a living
author at the beginning- of his own book, and
Henley has felt the hamper. So his best opinion
must be read beneath his liues; and it is substan-
tially just. Blunt has Energy—the energy of a
hale, strong man ; and the virile force of him heats
his verse to dull-red, but it never glows to white.
Hi s work is Nature in himself, or plus himself,
not multiplied by himself. He lives poetically on
the plane of his actual life, neither higher nor
lower; and since any actual life is a poor thing
beside the life conceivable, Blunt’s verses are poor
things beside the high. conceits of Poetry. It.is
better to resign their high-poetical claim, and to con-
sider them as a human document; for the man is
better than his work, and has lived better.
That is Blunt’s own point of view. He writes—
I would not, if I could, be called a poet.
I have no natural love of the “ chaste muse.”
If aught be worth the doing I would do it;
And others, if they will, may tell the news.
I care not for their laurels but would choose
On the world’s field to fight or fall or run.
My soul’s ambition will not take excuse
To play the dial rather than the sun.
Tlie faith I held I hold, as when a boy
I left my books for cricket-bat and gtjn.
The tales of poets are but scholars’ themes.
In my hot youth I held it that a man .
With heart to dare and stomach to enjoy
Had better work to his hand in any plan
Of any folly, so the thing were done,
Than m the noblest dreaming of mere dreams.
He may have chosen the better part; but he can-
not have both parts. Art is a mistress who bears
no rivalry : she exacts that Life must be her hand-
maid, and will not yield her best if forced to serve
as Life’s.
* * * *
In the youth which Blunt might have spent in
verses he fought bulls and wandered with
Bedouins ; and the Muse was coy when he called
her in his thirties. He persevered, and half-turned
the averted face, yet never saw it wholly. Had
his early bent been otherwise, Blunt might haye
done very fine poetic work. There is nearly the
force of Shakespeare in him, had he only Shake-
speare’s fancy. The pith of the following, one of
the best of Blunt’s “sonnets” (they are Catholic
as he, and include their 15, or 10, or even more
lines, without a noticeable murmur), is very Shake-
spearean. He has been talking of the origin and
youth of man, and proceeds—
And then fate strikes us. (First our joys decay.
Youth, with its pleasures, is a tale soon told.
We grow a little poorer day by day.
Old friendships falter. Loves grow strangely cold.
In vain we shift our hearts to a new hold
And: barter joy for joy, the less for less.
We doubt our strength, our wisdom, and our gold.
We stand alone, as in the wilderness
Of doubts and terrors. Then, if we be wise,
We make our terms with fate and; while we may,
Sell our life’s last sad remnant for a hope.
And it is wisdom thus to close our eyes.
But for the foolish, those who cannot pray,
What else remains of their dark horoscope
But a tall tree, and courage, and a rope ?
* * * *
The effect of this is very like the effect, of Shake-
speare’s 129th—“ The expense of spirit in a waste
of shame —-but even in this hard, strong, com-
paratively stiff and cold sonnet of Shakespeare’s
there is an imaginative flavour,, a melody in the
words, which are Blunt’s essential lacks. And
when you take one of Shakespeare’s softer,
warmer, more poetical sonnets, the difference is at
once apparent—
Thy bosom is endeared with all hearts,
Which I by lacking have supposed dead,
And there reigns love and all love’s loving parts,
And all those friends which I thought buried.
How many a holy and obsequious fear.
Hath dear religious love stol’n from mine eye
As interest of the dead, which, now appear
But things removed that-hidden in thee lie !
Thou art the grave where buried love doth live,
Hung with the trophies of my lovers gone,
Who all their parts of me to thee did give;
That due of many now is thine alone :
Their images 1 loved I view in thee,
And thou, all they, hast all the all of me.
Here the atmosphere is different altogether from
Blunt’s : it is not a clear, dry air, but one moist
and mellowed with feeling.: it is the air of Poetry
suffusing the country of Romance. Blunt never
gets nearer to Romance than passionate reality.
Or take an example from sensuous Rossetti, of in-
tellectual mettle inferior to Blunt’s.
Eat thou and drink ; to-morrow thou shalt die.
Surely the earth, that’s wise being very old,
Needs not our help. Then loose me, love, and hold
Thy sultry hair up from my face ; that I
May pour for thee this golden wine, brim-high,
Till round the glass thy fingers glow like gold.
We’ll drown all hours : thy song, while hours are
toll’d,
Shall leap, as fountains veil the changing sky.
Now kiss, and think that there are really those,
My own liigh-bosomed beauty, who increase
Yain gold, vain lore, and yet might choose our way !
Through many years they toil; then on a day
They die not, —for their life was death, —but cease;
And round their narrow lips the mould falls close.
* * * *
This has no particular thought-value; but it is
Poetry : Rossetti was born to inherit beauty, and
he makes the wilderness of words a holy ground
with phrase and cadence. Blunt lost the power
over words (if lie ever possessed its potency), when
he failed to cultivate it sedulously in youth. So
in later years, when he talked of a dead passion,
he could say that “our love is dead.” He might
even have risen to “ our troth is dead, ” heighten-
ing the poetry by the change of a word ; but the
change, and cadence, and heightening in “the
noblest troth dies here to dust ” were beyond
him ; and lines like—
The sunrise blooms and withers on the hill
Like any billflower ; and the noblest troth
Dies here to dust.
were far above him in the ether.
* * * *
This is meant as explanation, not as deprecia-
tion : in some moods Blunt’s verses are better to
read than Rossetti’s. His best and longest piece,
“Griselda,” is a very fine and suggestive woman-
study (and incidentally man-study), and Blunt,
like Burns, is better than his verses. His sym-
pathy for Arabi and Ireland sprang from a heart
bigger than his big brain. Even at 58 men call
him one of the finest and finest-looking fellows in
England, or in Egypt.

Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your facil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued ?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy ?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary?
Does your head swim?

“ Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a bad attack

shall recommend your Medicine to all my friends.—Yours
truly, E. SMITH, Halpin Park, Nhill, Victoria.”

JUBILEE YEAR.
Established 1819.
ProVidcpt Society.
With the close of the year 1898, the Australian Mutual Provident Society will complete FIFTY YEARS of its
existence. Members are invited to make this a RECORD YEAR by taking- out additional policies themselves, and by
inducing their friends who are not already assured to become members. ALL POLICIES ISSUED before
the end of the year will share in the division of profits to be made as at 31st December next. Amount of Cash Surplus
divided amongst the members during the 49 years ending 1897 was £7,726,170. The Cash Bonus distributed amongst
the Policyholders for the single year 1897 was £115,235. EVERY YEAR A BONUS YEAR.
Accumulated Funds, £11,179,578. Annual Income, £2,091,706.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Cockatoo Digger.
He ain’t a reg’lar digger, the same as me and you;
He uses square-nose shovels, and he digs his holes
askew,
Big at top, and small at bottom ’nough to give a
man the cramp;
And he brings a coil of fencin’ wire for fixin’ up
the camp.
He’s a hunkin’, hulkin’ wapstraw
He’s a talkin’ cockatoo,
Full o’ rotten theories,
And he fellers me and you.
He makes a crazy cradle, tries to copy me and you,
But his slides is all uneven, so o’ course the gold
goes through ;
He talks o’ washin’ mullock, and the mullock he
calls loam ;
Brings his farmin’ on the diggin’s, ’stead o’ leavin’
it at home.
He’s a loutin’, spoutin’ yokel,
Talks o’ reefs that break and spue—
Thinks he’s talkin’ minin’
The same as me and you.
He never finds no diggin’s—that’s left to me and
you;
While he’s harrowin’ and scratchin’, we got all
that to do ;
When we’ve done the prospectin’, and found
what is to find,
He comes and puts ’is pegs in—and he does his
peggin’ blind.
For he doesn’t know the bizness ;
He’s a cocky through and through,
Never learned the diggin’
The same as me and yom
He works his claim peculiar, and not like me and
you * "
That sinks and drives straightfor’ard till we clean
her up and through.
He mullocks-up his surface, and he drives at
chance and guess,
For he don’t know wash from headin’s and his
cleanin’-up’s a mess.
For he ain’t a reg’lar digger,
He’s a scratching cockatoo
With cheek enough to come
Advisin’ me and yon.
He don’t keep decent workin’ hours, the same as
me and you ;
He brings his farmin’ day hours, which leaves
night with very few ;
He loses in his tailin’s, doesn’t save what gold he
could—
So we’re glad to see him goin’ home to say the
field’s no good.
He’s a gruntin’, grumblin’ cocky,
And we’re sorry, but it’s true—
He calls himself a digger,
The same as me and you. ,
W.A.
PROSPECT GOOD.
Shoots.
When the green is on the furrow,
And the fresh wheat comes away,
’Twixt the “ put-in ” and the harvest
Comes the cookie’s holiday ;
And it kind o’ knocks his back in
To find when ploughing’s through,
That his shoots want cutting badly,
And there’s miles of them to do.
Oh, the shoots! cutting shoots ! everlasting
shoots !
From crow-cough in the morning when the frost
is lying white;
There’s one long month behind me,
In another two you’ll find me
Still a-cut-cut-cutting till the cows come home at
night.
A foot high at the most they were
When we were ploughing here,
So I reckoned it was good enough
To let them stand this year;
But the old chap surely grows them
(With a “ culture ” most “ intense ”),
For they’ve flourished like his own—and now
They’re level with the fence.
It’s as bad as oakum-picking,
Just as deadly-dull, and slow ;
But they must be down by stripping,
So you hit out all you know ;
You slog in like a madman
Till you’re blistered, stiff, and sore,
Then you look at what’s behind you,
And you curse at what’s before.
Cutting shoots, mallee shoots, everlasting shoots,
From crow-cough in the morning when the
frbst is lying white ;
There’s one long month behind me,
In another two you’ll find me
Still a-cut-cut-cutting till the cows come home at
night.
E. R. P.
Nex’ Seed’n’.
The time Eliza V me set up
On this’ere section
(The year Tregeagle won the Oup,
By Flash—lnspection)
We uster say, w’en things went wrong,
“ Well, never mind ; it ain’t fer long:
We’ll put it right nex’ seed’n’.”
Nex’ seed’n’ ! Nex’ seed’n’!
We’ll do that bit o’ weed’n’,
An’ stop them cracks,
An’ patch them sacks,
An’ lots o' things nex’ seed’n’.
W’en Summer went an’ Autumn come,
An’ w’eat were rise’n’,
We’d talk o’ savin’ up a “ plum,”
As sure as p’is’n.
Or p’r’aps we’d look at Thompson’s lot,
An’ say we’d buy it w’en we got
Things redded up, nex’ seed’n’.
Nex’ seed’n’! Nex’ seed’n’!
We felt like runnin’ Eden
Without the snake,
An’ no mistake,
An’ things like that, nex’ seed’n’.
But seed’n’s come an’ seed’n’s go
More quicker ’n’ quicker,
An’ money trickles in so slow
An’ debts get thicker !
An’ Bill come first, then Siss, then Sue,
Then Jack—an’ there’s another doo—
Or maybe twins —nex’ seed’n’!
Nex’ seed’n’! Nex’ seed’n’!
There’s frocks an’ pants a-need’n’.
Our Jack’s bin ill,
The doctor’s bill
Must wait until nex’ seed’n’.
S.A.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 29, 1898.
Prick, 6d
The PressjCen and tub Newsbots.— Preaching
at the Pil.t-street Congregational Church last
night, the Rev. E. -T. Duustan said he felt he
should be neglecting his duty if he did not make
a protest against the'action of certain represen-
tatives of the press last Sunday. Had he listened
to the dictates of expediency, or obeyed the voice of
inclination, he would have remained silent, but for
the second time certain "pressmen had outraged
the sentiment of the community by organising a
picnio for newsboys cn the Lord’s Day. The law
stood upon the Statute Books, and should, there-
fore, be obasrved. The pre&s exercised a power-
ful, moral, social, and political influence, and the
community had a right to look to it for carefu 1
guidance.

Yesterday bad been proclaimed by the Govern-
ment as a day of humiliation and prayer for rain,
but happily it was converted into a day of fhanks-
piving instead.
I Archbishop Smith had pi omulgated a form of prayer
' which was in consonance with the proclamation.
By the prayer it was contemplated that worsbip-
l pers should hntnble themselves before God
in this tboir “ time of special distress and
anxiety.” This really related to the condition of
things prior to the general fall of rain, and as it
I was considered that it did not correctly describe the
j condition of affairs now prevailing, some clergy-
men manifested a reluctance to use the form, and
relied upon the special prayer and thanksgiving
embraced in the ‘‘Book of Common Prayer.”
Some ministers used the special form of prayer ,
for rain issued by the Archbishop, others used
the ordinary form, and others again used
the thanksgiving. There were yet other I
ministers who, in acknowledgment of the
general rain, read the thanksgiving, and who,
having regard to the wants of those districts still in
need of rain, recited the ordinary prayer for rain.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, oi
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence" column all contributions submitted •
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 29, 1898.
The Yellow Whisky Advt.
Some day, when The Bulletin has grown
unspeakably rich by denouncing the bloated
capitalist and whooping up the cause of the
poor, it has a vague idea that it will build an
Ideal City with a part of its pile. As a
matter of preference it will buy up Sydney, and
level that dreadful metropolis to the ground,
barring, perhaps, two streets, and build its
Ideal City on the site, for the place where
Sydney stands is one where an Ideal City
would show to advantage. Then a grateful
country will remember this paper and raise
a 100-ton marble monument above its bones
when it is dead, and that ponderous tomb
will be a good thing for the unemployed to
get on the lee side of on rainy nights. If
Sydney is unprocurable it might content it-
self to purchase Melbourne and build
its city there ; though a small part of
the centre of Victoria’s capital is a (com-
parative) dream of loveliness there are
few places in this world which need re-
generation worse than Port Melbourne
and Williamstown and Richmond and Col-
lingwood and Footscray. But it would
prefer Sydney. And when it makes that
incredible pile by denouncing the capitalist
and strongly advocating the eight-hour
movement, its Ideal City will be like no
other city that ever was on the face of this
unkempt and hair-tangled earth. There will
tie no bye-street or lane in it less than 70ft.
wide and no main street less than 132 ft.
George-street will take a bee-line from
Circular Quay to Parramatta—or perish in
the attempt. Every street will go as straight
as an arrow, and take all obstacles as they
come. Every building will be detached with
at least a few feet of green, or of an apology
therefor, between it and the next building.
If it isn’t green enough the owner will be
fined. A rigid Building Act will be enforced
throughout the city and its furthest suburbs.
The whole of that area will be stone or brick,
and not even a wooden stable or outhouse or
fence or dog-kennel will be allowed to totter
in its gaunt, dry-rotted, unpainted, sun-
blistered, combustible unsightliness in the
whole metropolitan area. The Bulletin’s
own mattoid will draw up the plans for that
Ideal City ; it will be run by a benevolent
despotism in the interests of Art and Beauty ;
and whatever is ugly or smellful or glaring
or offensive therein will get three months
without the option.
* * * *
In The Bulletin’s new city no one will
be allowed to paint his building blue or
green or red with yellow stripes, for, after
all, no one has any more right to strike his
fellow man in the eye with a shock of fearful
colors than to do the same outrage with a
brick. No one will be allowed, either, to
leave his building dirty and unpainted. A
certain degree of uniformity will be insisted
on, and the man who wants to pile up a
gaunt ten-storey tenement, which looks, in
its height and its thinness, as if it didn’t get
enough to eat, will be treated as a public
enemy. The man whose steps or front rail-
ings are broken or unsightly will be fined ;
and the man whose property is decayed will
be fined also ; and the man who hoists a
glaring calico-sign anywhere will get one
month’s hard labor; and the person who
disfigures the pavements with orange and
banana peel will get six months ; and the
boy who yells horribly at the back of an
omnibus, or makes a deafening row in any-
body’s ear with an evening paper, will be
sent to a reformatory. But the pity of it
all—when one thinks of the Ideal City which
might exist on the lovely site where Sydney
now moulders in its waste of crooked alleys—
is that that city might just as well be there
now. It could have been built for very
little more than the present one cost if there
had been some few shreds of intellect among
the early fathers of New South Wales. But,
unluckily, there weren’t. These early fathers
had the grandest opportunity which ever
came to any set of fathers in these last two
centuries, and about the way they misused it
the less said the better.
* * * *
There is time, even now, to undo their
work if only the authorities will set about it.
The Bulletin suggests, as a beginning,
which will cost nothing and which will
remove probably the most widespread offence
in the modern city, that the bill-poster
should be prohibited and utterly abolished.
Sydney, like other Australian and British
cities, is disfigured from end to end with
staring advertisements, painted or plastered
on fences, dead walls, rocks, and every other
vantage-ground. Of course, to compel their
removal would be an interference with the
“ liberty of the subject.” But theirexistence
is a still worse interference. In theory,
every man has a right to stick any ad-
vertising disfigurement on his own fence
that he pleases. But it is an equally
sound theory that every man should be at
liberty to wander through his own city with-
out being shocked at every turn by the red-
pill advertisement, or stunned by the green
hair-restorer, or deafened by the loud hues
of the cheap-boot notice. The law, which
has its lucid moments, prohibits objection-
able smells, when they become very objec-
tionable and strike the community with a
violent thud in the nose ; and the eye wants
equal protection. If a citizen has the very
best guano or blood-manure or bone-dust
for sale, he isn’t allowed to advertise it by
depositing the substance where its effluvium
will drift along the streets of Sydney or Mel-
bourne ; and the law which permits him to
advertise it or anything else in red letters
eight feet high in these same localities is a
law which has a nose but no eye. The
people who advertise are comparatively few
at the best; the people who have to look at
the disfiguring advertisement, whether they
will or not, are many. The Bulletin pleads
for the liberty of the subject—the liberty of
the great majority, which should be allowed
to traverse the streets in peace undisturbed
by the unpicturesque placard of the minority,
* • * *
The abolition of the street-advertisement
would do no harm to the trading-community
on the whole. It might injure the indi-
vidual trader here and there the loudest
advertisers of the crowd—but, to the mass,
it would be a clear saving of money. Nor
would it be necessary for them to spend
more money on newspaper advertising to
make up the loss of publicity, and thus inci-
dentally swell The Bulletin’s profits. An
enormous proportion of advertising is pure
waste of money. There are probably a
hundred different brands of whisky placarded
all over Sydney—yet the community doesn’t
get any the drunker in the aggregate. Of
course whiskies 1 to 50 can’t leave off
disgracing the city with their pictorial night-
mares, because, if they did, whiskies 50 to
100 would carry off'all the trade. But if the
law, with a kindly regard for the appearance
of a neglected capital, swept them all away
in one act, then they would all stand on the
same footing. It might be a trifle rough on
whisky No. 47, if it had gut the best adver-
tising spaces, and was thus somewhat ahead
of its rivals, but the community would drink
just as much as before, and get just as
drunk as before, and the advertising
money would be saved, and the whisky
business, in the aggregate, would boom
just the same.' The same rule applies to
other cases, or at least to most of them.
But the abolition must be effected by
law. No mutual agreement can do it;
the only result would be that whisky firm
No. 8 or No. 96 would break the agreement
as soon as it had bought up all the advertising
spaces, and thus “dish” its rivals hopelessly.
«. * * *
Yet the permanent advertisement isn’t the
worst offender. It is, at least, kept in toler-
able repair. But the transient advertisement
—the special Sunday service or lecture or
bun-struggle notice, the election placard, the
public meeting and charity bazaar announce-
ment —are many degrees worse. There is no
law to compel the person who puts these on
the fence to come round and wash them off
again when they are done with. They are
simply left to decay, and fall to rags, and
flap themselves to death in the wind. The
last New South Wales election is very
ancient history, but the bills calling on the
public to vote for Bile Wilks and Spruson
and Neild and Samuel Somebody , the
rest of the name had fallen off and blown
away up the street when the writer took his
observations—still hang out wretchedly on
fences and'posts and condemned buildings.
Even the placards about the Federal refer-
endum are not quite gone yet. All this is
just as reasonable as it would be to allow the
horse which dies unexpectedly in George-
street, Sydney, to remain there until it
decayed and blew away in the form of dust.
* • * *
The street advertiser was bad enough
when he stuck to the streets, but he isn’t
content with that now. He invades the
beauty-spots around the city, and hangs his
pill advt. on the waterfall, and the notice
about his sixpenny hat on the cliffs overlook-
ing the harbor. Boats with patent-medi-
cine information on their colored sails roam
around Neutral Bay and Lane Cove. The
towering rocks beside the road to the Spit
have somebody’s infallible-something at 6|d.
profusely stencilled upon them. Manly, and
the Bulli Pass, and the Hawkesbury, and
the peaceful reaches of Middle Harbour are
all being invaded. Only the Goth and vandal
would paint their outrages on places like
these, but, then, the world is full of adver-
tising. Goths. The harbor and the mountain
views and the waterfalls are—or ought to be
—public property. They should be places,
of rest where the citizen can go and be at
peace, and leave the world behind him.
When he is called upon even there to
pause along with his wife and his three
daughters, to consider the “ inn’ards ” of
the whole party, and to ask himself and
them if their systems don’t require to be
thoroughly cleansed with somebody’s patent
horse-ball at eighteenpence, then it is time to
revolt. It is by way of revolting thoroughly
and effectively that The Bulletin advocates
the abolition of the whole system, and a
general wash-down of every Australian town
and city and their surroundings.
* * * *
And just here this paper wakes up sud-
denly from its dream of an Ideal City, and
finds pasted on its own office wall down in
Chinatown, Sydney, a handbill stating that
there will be a bun-struggle somewhere to-
morrow evening at eight.
The Rivers.
The decision of N.S.W. Assembly that “the
use of inland rivers for purposes' of water con-
servation and irrigation should be more clearly
safeguarded,” practically finished the debate
on the Federal resolutions so far as that House
was concerned. The decision was arrived at
by 58 votes to 13—which was not wonderful
considering its complete and unobtrusive harm
lessness. For all rivers are “inland”—at
least, the river that is out in the ocean is in
no way connected with the case. And every-
body is willing to “safeguard the use” of
rivers ; South Australia, in particular, has been
most anxious all along to safeguard the use of
the Murray where it flows through the Crow-
eaters’ territory, and Victoria wants to safe-
guard the same river on its border because
N.S.W. claims sole ownership of it ; so Premier
Reid’s hazy and mixed expression suits every-
body well. It is the interpretation of it later
on which will make trouble, because the words
themselves have no definite meaning whatever,
and give no definite authority of any kind.
N.S.W. will doubtless insist on reading them
that N.S.W. should safeguard the whole Murray
system as its own property; and Victoria
and S.A. and Queensland will read them that
all three provinces should safeguard the rivers
jointly as joint property ; and as the clause is
equally intelligible either way things are no
further advanced.
* * * »
Of course everyone knows what use the
Reid Government proposes to make of the
resolution. All along it has insisted on view-
ing the river question—like the railway question
and the boundaries question—in a provincial
rather than an Australian aspect. Its first
assumption—that navigation should be con-
sidered a matter of secondary importance as
compared with irrigation—is undoubtedly cor-
rect. Australia hasn’t enough water for both
purposes —in fact, it isn’t quite certain that it
has absolutely sufficient for either purpose, and
even now, when there is practically no irrigaiion,
the supply of water for navigation purposes
often gives out. Irrigation is vastly the more
important of the two, and when it is scientifi-
cally carried out the Murray River system, in
ordinary seasons, will be utterly dried up before
it reaches the sea, and the Murray mouth will
be a mere dusty excavation. Australia must
needs depend on railways for its in-
ternal communication. Possibly the real
reason why the Reid Government desires
to sacrifice navigation to irrigation is that the
Darling and Murray are water highways which
carry the produce of western N.S.W. cheaply to
S.A. ports, instead of it being dragged at three
times the cost to Sydney ; but, whatever its
motive, there is no doubt about the correctness
of its conclusions. Some day western N.S.W.
will have to do without its river outlet to S.A.,
for the water will all be wanted to moisten the
soil and grow huge crops thereon. And then,
if the provincial railway policy is still adhered
to, and the west can’t get cheap railway com-
munication with its nearest port, there will he
no payable outlet for these crops, and a very
interesting situation will ensue.
* * » •
But it is on the question of the ownership of
the waters for irrigation purposes that the
great struggle is to be fought out. With skil-
ful conservation there may be enough water in
the Murray system to irrigate all the lands to
which that water can be profitably carried,
right down to the Murray mouth. The only
question is, whether the precious fluid should
be divided among the four provinces (Queens-
land, N.S. Wales, Victoria, and S.A.) through
which the Murray system flows, or whether
N.S. W. shall be allowed to enforce its ridicul-
ous claim to own all these waters, and shall
have the right to intercept them all as they
pass through its territory. In other words, it
is a question whether they shall be regarded
as the property of Australia, provided for the
enrichment of Australia in general, or whether
each province shall be allowed to use its little
bit of river for its own separate interests and
without any regard for Australia as a whole.
This is the brutally selfish and unpatriotic
attitude which the Reid Government has taken
up. In the words of Premier Reid, ‘'any
attempt on the part of the Convention or any
other power under Heaven to get N.S. W. to
give up her absolute ownership of the Darling
and Murrumbidgee Rivers must fail. We will
not hand over the control of the Darling and
Murrumbidgee Rivers, because they both abso-
lutely belong to us.” The statement that the
Darling, whose waters have their beginnings in
Central Queensland, absolutely belongs to
N.S. W. is, of course, either plain ignorance or
else plain Rougemont. But, even apart from
that, it is interesting to see where the parochial
idea of river ownership leads.
* * * *
As already mentioned, the waters of the
Darling begin in Queensland. That province
supplies more than one-third of the river, and
if the theory is to hold good that each pro-
vince is to be the absolute owner of as much of
the river system as lies within its borders, un-
doubtedly Queensland can divert all these
upper waters for irrigation purposes. As irri-
gation has hardly started yet in Queensland
this trouble may be a long way off, but it will
be a very real trouble when it comes. If
N.S.W. carries its point on the river problem,
and goes in for large irrigation works all along
the Darling to fertilise its western country,"
Queensland may wake up any day and entirely
cut off the supply' as regards the ten north-
western counties, and leave them desert.
Similarly, Victoria supplies practically all the
waters of the Murray for the first 1300 miles
or so of its course, and if the Reid Govern-
ment’s theory prevails, that every province is
to be at full liberty to grab all the waters
w'ithin its reach without regard to anybody
else, then Victoria’s right to divert its own
tributaries, if it can do so, for irrigation pur-
poses, is beyond question. In that case there
would be practically no Murray at all —only the
Darling and the Murrumbidgee.
* * * *
South Australia supplies practically no water
to the Murray system ; it merely furnishes an
outlet. But even that is at times a matter of
some importance. Australia, desperately dry
as it is a great part of the time, has its flood
years now and then. If N.S.W. is going to
enforce its claim to the sole ownership of as
much of the Murray and Darling river system
as it can reach, and if it makes full use ofits
claim by using up the waters for local irrigation,
regardless of the interests of S.A., then it un-
doubtedly can, in dry years, mop up the w'hole
of the wretched rivers before they reach S.A.
at all. Then S.A. would receive no waters
except in wet years when the local rain supply
made them practically superfluous, and when it
didn’t want them. In that case, S.A. would
probably give N.S.W. notice to remove its
river altogether. If the Darling and Murray
and Murrumbidgee are wholly N.S.W. pro-
perty, then they have no right to go into
S.A. territory. So long as N.S.W. is wholly
dependent on S.A. for an outlet in wet years to
prevent its Darling and Murray and Murrum-
bidgee drowning the country, these three
rivers can’t be exclusively N.S.W. rivers.
If each province is to be absolute owner
of its own river, instead of there being
a joint ownership, then Queensland will
be liable to N.S. W. for damages when it lets
the flood waters of its exclusive rivers, the
Culgoa and Upper Macintyre and Warregoand
other branches of the Darling, come down and
do damage and drown cattle in N.S.W. And
N.S.W. will be liable to S.A. when its rivers
make similar trouble in that country. And,
seeing that N.S.W. claims all of the Murray, it
will also be liable when its river swells up and
overflows the arable flats of Victoria. It can
get level, no doubt, by shutting up the Mildura
settlement, which is irrigated by its waters,
but there will be no profit and little satisfac-
tion on either side.
* * * •
Even this, however, is only a fraction of the
difficulty. All the part of western N.S.W.
which most wants irrigation—a good one-third
of the province, comprising the counties north
of the Lachlan and west of the Bogan—contri-
butes not one permanent running stream to
the river system. It is wholly dependent on
the water which comes down from Queensland,
eastern N.S.W., and Victoria. It is in these
arid regions that the greatest irrigation system
of the future will be constructed. Some day,
however, the unwieldy province of N.S.W.
will be cut up like Queensland and Westralia;
when it will be done and how it will be done
no one can say, but no political arrangement in
this world and no artificial boundaries in this
world hold good for ever. Then a great part
of the irrigated region may be cut off front its
water-supply, and if the Reid Government’s
unpatriotic and purely parochial idea is adopted
the northern province, as sole owner of the rivers
within its territory, can shut off the supply,
and use it for its own purposes, and turn the
garden into a wilderness again, and make all tlur


millions spent on irrigation a mere bad debt.
That part of N.S.W. which contributes the bulk
of the purely N.S.W. water-supply lies closer to
Brisbane than to Sydney, and when border
duties are removed and other provincial bar-
riers are shifted, there is always a possibility
that it may break off from the distant metro-
polis and gravitate to the one which is nearer.
In such a case it would take both branches of
the Darling River along with it. In similar
fashion the Murray is liable to change hands
some day because the Riverina has its natural
political and commercial centre in Melbourne.
To a politician who looks far ahead all these
chances make the purely selfish provincial
theory inadvisable even on purely selfish
grounds.
• • • •
The Bulletin’s conviction is that every
river which flows through more than one pro-
vince should be placed, with all its affluents,
under Federal control. These scanty streams
were sent here by Providence— if there is a
Providence —before there were any provincial
boundaries. They were sent to water Aus-
tralia; not to water only N.S.W. There is,
probably (with economy), water enough to go
round ; but, if there isn’t, every acre
of irrigable land from the head of the
Culgoa in Central Queensland, down to the
Murray mouth, has an equal claim to a share
of what is going; and when irrigation starts on
a large scale and there is danger—as there cer-
tainly will be some day—of the supply being
inadequate the central authority should share
it out, so far as possible, according to the
irrigable area in each province. Or rather,
Australia should, for irrigation purposes, be
considered a single province, without the least
reference to boundaries, and the water supplied
for all-Australian benefit should be spent like
the money borrowed on all-Australian credit —
where it is most deserved and most needed. This
paper speaks on behalf of Australia in general in
this matter, and its interest is in the welfare of
Australia—not merely in the welfare of that
part of it which lies this side of a streak on a
map. The tendency to be an enthusiastic
patriot up to a certain streak on a map, and a
conscienceless brigand beyond it, is the great
obstacle in the way of union. Every Govern-
ment in Australia has, unfortunately, got the
streak disease in a bad form, and the GoD-fear-
ing Ministry of N.S.W. has it worst of all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Potato Young Commission.
Justice Owen, the Commissioner appointed by
the Reid-Young Government to enquire into
the charges made against Young (and inciden-
tally against Reid) re the Hastings-Macleay
election, met all by himself last week and
asked for “evidence.” Nobody turned up to
offer any evidence against Young —in fact,
there was no prosecutor at all. Therefore the
Telegraph, the servile organ and mendacious
door-mat of the Reid Ministry, rose to ask
why the low characters who made all these
“ charges ” against Potato Young didn’t come
forward like men with evidence to prove them,
and plainly intimated that if they didn’t come
forward, they should for very shame conceal
themselves in a hollow log for the rest of their
days. In the Assembly Premier Reid took up
a similar lofty attitude :
Mr. Reid said that, in view of the large number of posi-
tive statements made before the Commission was ap-
pointed as to the corrupt actions of Mr. Young, he could
not conceive of there being any difficulty in collecting
evidence. (“ Hear, hear.”) The slanderers would surely
bring forward the evidence on which they had based
their slanders, if they had any. (Cheers.) There could
be no excuse on the ground of ignorance, for Mr. Justice
Owen had directed advertisements to be inserted in the
press asking those who had evidence to give to notify the
Court of the fact. Surely, as hundreds of persons were
alleged to have seen these corrupt overtures, some of
them would come forward. (“ Hear, hear.”)
Mr. Perry : Isn’t it the duty of those who demanded
the inquiry to produce the evidence ?
Mr. Reid : No. (Cheers.) The Commission would
never have been appointed but for the charges referred
to, which had not come from the Government, but from
other people. Now they wanted these other people to
have the patriotism and the pluck to come forward.
The Royal Commission was called for by the
Reid party only, and appointed by the Reid
Government to whitewash the Reid Govern-
ment, and no one but the Reid Government
has anything on earth to do with it. There is
no question of “ evidence ” in the case. All
the evidence consisted of the printed reports of
Potato Young’s speeches, in which he cried to
the people to elect Pumpkin Smith because he
(Smith) would get them public works (from
Young’s department), and Barton wouldn’t.
Which was so very nearly the same as saying
that Young’s department would give the
Macleay people public works if they applied
through their member, Smith, and wouldn’t do
so if they applied through Barton, that few
people can see the difference. These appeals
to the people to elect Smith, and thereby get
lots of public works, are not denied. It
is hardly possible to say that all these news-
papers made the same mistake, and all heard
Potato Young say the thing he didn’t say—
although Brunker did exactly that thing
in regard to his famous yarn about the
27 per cent, duties. The Barton party
laid Young’s statements before the House,
and asked if the House regarded, or did not
regard, Potato Young as a disgraceful object,
and the House, by a very small party majority
indeed, said it didn’t. That ended the matter.
There is no “ evidence ” to bring forward ex-
cept the reports of Young’s speeches, and they
have been already brought forward. There is
nothing to prove except that Potato Young
was reported to have said a large number of
very disgraceful-looking things to help Pump-
kin Smith into Parliament, and the reports
prove themselves and are uncontradicted.
As regards witnesses, nobody professed to
have any witnesses except those numer-
ous reports of Young’s own words. The
Reid party chose to appoint a Commis-
sioner to hear further evidence (which nobody
had offered) and to examine personal witnesses
(which no one had offered either). If Potato
Young will rise up and declare that all the
ugly statements ascribed to him by the news-
papers which followed him through his
campaign are either conspiracy or unani-
mous hallucination then there will be some-
thing to inquire into. The Commission can at
least start inquiring which party is accurate
and which is Ananias. But at present the
Opposition says that Young said certain things,
and that these things were utterly disgraceful.
Young doesn’t deny saying these things ; he
simply says they weren’t at all disgraceful and
his party majority backs him up. When Young
won’t circumstantially deny that he said the
things he is accused of saying, what is the use
of proving he said them, especially when his
party majority has decided that it doesn’t
matter whether he said them or not ?

One Rich Landlord.
The sequestration of the estate of Charles
Sargeant, a member of Victoria’s House of 48
Rich Landlords, is a very remarkable proceed-
ing. The chief qualification insisted upon for
Vic. M’sL.C. is that they must be men with
property. Each fossil must own, and have
owned for at least a year before his election,
“ lands and tenements in Victoria to an annual
value of £lOO above all charges and encum-
brances affecting the same.” Sargeant goes
broke on account of a moderate-sized pro-
missory-note, which he gave to the Commercial
Bank of Australia in 1891, and which he has
never since contrived to meet. He was last re-
elected in 1895 as an unencumbered Rich Land-
lord, possessing “ lands and tenements ” in
Victoria worth some £2500 or £3OOO of capital
value over and above all “charges and en-
cumbrances affecting the same,” according
to the touching definition of the Act. Now,
was Sargeant, in ’95, legally possessed of
such unencumbered property, considering that
he has just gone broke over a debt of £3BOO,
which was in existence at the time of that
election ? Also, did any public official attempt
to find out if Sargeant was qualified ? Is it
anybody’s duty to find out if any M.L.C. is
qualified, or does the country simply take the
Fat Man’s word for it ? Is he even called upon
to swear that he has the necessary unencum-
bered property, and, if he is, who audits his
swear ? And how many of the other 47 alleged
Rich Landlords are minus the legal qualifica-
tion and only governing Victoria in the interests
of Fat upon a stupendous bluff ! The Bulle-
tin can’t remember a single case when the
qualification of any Vic. M.L.C. has been
inspected, so it presumes that the country
just takes the busted Tory’s word for it. Cer-
tainly there is something incongruous in the
idea of a politician whose main qualifica-
tion is supposed to be the ownership of
much unencumbered property having his
estate sequestrated. The p.n. which Sargeant
gave in ’9l was for £4985, and as he now goes
under on a judgment for £3844 it seems that,
in the last seven years during which he has been
bossing the country as a rich property-holder
with £2500 or £3OOO worth of unencumbered
“lands and tenements,” he has been unable to
pay more than £ll4l and the interest. That
certainly looks as if he had been for some time
an unqualified legislator helping to squash all
democratic progress by an absolutely illegal
vote. He may not have been, but the matter
is suspicious enough to call for enquiry. (What
is the penalty, by the way, for being an un-
qualified M.L.C. in Victoria?) The Bulletin
suggests, in view of the haze surrounding Sar-
geant’s case, that there should be a vigorous
enquiry into the qualification of all present and
future Victorian Ms.L.C. It is bad enough for
the democracy to be blocked at every turn by
its 48 Rich Landlords. If, by way of insult
added to injury, the people are being scorned
and flouted by illegal sham Landlords, it is too
much.
A Curious Committee.
The N.S.W. Elections and Qualifications Com-
mittee will probably burst up very shortly, and
the work of deciding disputed elections will be
referred to the Supreme Court. The charge
most commonly laid against the present com-
mittee is that most of its members simply de-
cide that the fellow who belongs to their own
party is in the right. The appeal of Bavister
(Govt, nominee) against Wise’s election at
Ashfield is the latest trouble. Bavister lodged
his appeal and the needful £5O deposit within
the prescribed eight weeks after the re-
turn of the writ—-but the petition was
unsigned. Lodged another duly signed,
but with no £5O deposit, reckoning that the
first deposit would apply automatically.
Pleaded, on behalf of Wise, that the first de-
posit should have been withdrawn and re-lodged
with the second petition, and that the latter,
having no deposit attached to it, is informal
like the first one. Also, Wise alleges that
the second appeal came too late, arriving more
than eight weeks after August 2, the day
when the writ was returned. Bavister alleges
that it was in time, as it arrived less than
eight weeks after August 13 —the last day
when the writ might legally have been re-
turned. The Committee, which is mostly made
up of Government supporters, struggled over
this matter with closed doors, and the
Government members of the Committee
were of opinion that Bavister’s first (un-
signed) appeal was informal and wanted to
go on with the second one. Then they found
that the second was quite possibly informal
also, and that their first opinion might mean
that their opponent, Wise, would retain
his seat. So the Government majority
on the Committee decided that the un-
signed petition of their supporter, Bavis-
ter, wasn’t informal, after all—whereupon
an indignant Crick rushed for the door
and left. The Elections and Qualifications
Committee smells so badly of partisanship
now that its abolition has become absolutely
necessary.
The Coming Vic. Nigger.
In Victoria it is reckoned that Indian tea
merchants employ about 1200 of their own
coffee-coloured race hawking tea, and when the
Immigration Restriction Bill came before Vic.
Leg. Council last week that body was much
bruised in spirit lest the “rights” of these
persons should be infringed. So it got over the
difficulty by enacting that any employer may
introduce five niggers per annum under a bond
of £2OO that they will leave the province again
at the end of five years. The “ bond ” is a
beautiful idea, anyhow. At the end of five
years it will be very hard to identify the five
niggers; and it will be nobody’s business to
enquire about them ; and if they are by any
chance enquired about and their owner can’t
find five niggers who are leaving in any case,
and induce them to personate his men—well,
he will probably say they are dead. If there is
trouble it is 50 to one that nobody will attempt
to enforce the bond, and it is also 500 to one
that there will be no money to be got because
Ram Chunder who gave the bond has gone out
of the business, and Ram Lal, who is running
it in his stead, knows nothing about it and isn’t
responsible. And, as likely as not, it will prove
that Ram Chunder, the nominal importer and
bondsman, was really only a dummy who never
owned a cent of his own. Also, Ram
Chunder generally disappears within five
years in any case. And if Ram Lal wants
to import more than five niggers a year his
way is easy and plain : he can ready up 20
niggers to pose as employers, and import five
other niggers each, and then he will have 100
of them all in one consignment. Under this
beautiful law it seems that 100,000 or more
Asiatics can swarm into Victoria yearly. All
that is wanted is an alleged “employer” to
pose, for a small commission, as the importer
of each five, and to give his valueless “ bond ”
for £2OO. The same “employer” can be used
several times a year if he changes his name and
address and dyes his hair. And, later on, when
he can’t be found, and his £2OO can’t be found,
and the niggers he imported can’t be sorted out
from the great mass of brown and yellowish
humanity, it will be nobody’s business in
particular. In addition to all this, any
nigger who has served in the British army or
navy and been discharged with a good charac-
ter is to be admitted free ; there are some
hundreds of thousands of them, and their
good characters will be bought by intending
emigrants to Victoria, and a new supply of
good characters will be manufactured to meet
the demand, so this opens up a magnificent
prospect. The Victorian House of Rich Land-
lords struck out 18 clauses—practically the
whole—of the original Immigration Restriction
Bill as passed by the Assembly, and put in the
above great idea instead. Then “ a number of
members expressed extreme regret that the
populace of the colony should be so wanting in
liberality and Christian spirit as to wish to ex-
clude their yellow and brown brothers,” which
is a beautiful piece of hypocrisy on the part of a
House which is itself so lacking in liberality
and Christian spirit that it won’t let its own
white brother sit along with it unless he owns
“ lands and tenements to the annual value of
£100.” Also, F. Sargood moved, unsuccess-
fully, that the present Chinese exclusion regu-
lations should be altogether abolished. If the
Victorian Assembly stands this latest outrage
by its House of Landlords, that province had
better shut up both Houses and go in bald-
headed for Anarchy.
The antiquated and fallacious insanity-test
upon which N.S.W. Judge Owen considered
himself bound to rely in the Lisson case, was
intended, Dr. Springthorpe, of Melbourne,
points out, to “ apply to persons who labor
under partial delusions only, and are not in
other respects insane,” or, in other words, to
partly-insane persons. But modern science
denies the existence of “ partial insanity,” and
affirms that the fact of a man being able intel-
lectually to appreciate the nature of an act is
no proof whatever that he is not maniacally
compelled to it. The “M‘Naghten test,”
in a word, assumes the possibility of
ascertaining exactly how far the crack
in a man’s intellect extends, and at what
point his mental structure becomes sound
enough to render him legally responsible. This
notion, besides being vicious in theory and
scientificably untenable, is so unreliable in
practical application that the law of most
civilised nations has discarded it. Also, the
leading English Judges have at least con-
sistently ignored it, so Judge Owen would not
have been without precedent for bringing what
he considered strict law more into line with
modern scientific opinion, to say nothing of
common sense or humanity.
Melbourne Trades Hall Council being invited
to assist the Anti-Sweating League in a deputa-
tion to the Chief Secretary, got huffy, and
several of its delegatesobjectedtobeingled atthe
tail of the League in this fashion. Which is en-
tirely their own fault. The Anti-Sweating
people are now trying to get the provisions of
the Factories Act extended to include many
other trades, and to control more directly the
hours of work and the minimum rates of pay.
Where gross cases of sweating occur in Mel-
bourne now the unfortunate victims know well
that it is practically useless to trouble the
Trades Hall in the matter, but the Anti-
Sweating League will take any case up, and, in
many instances, working quietly and without
blataut advertisement, has obtained relief. It
is late in the day for the Unions’ delegates to
complain of being dragged at the heels of those
who are doing the work that should have been
taken up by themselves long ago. The Trades
Hall for years past has done nothing—has
even made no attempt to do anything—either
socially or politically; but for a few empty
blatherskites the whole organization might be
non-existent. They started well, on a sound
foundation, like their eight-hours’ monument,
and, like it, they remain unfinished, incomplete,
a mere suggestion of what they might have
been.
*,
The consolidation of the Statute Law of
N.S.W. goes on apace, and there is every pros-
pect of the work being completed before the
end of the year. The work has been well done ;
but, as might have been expected, some errors
have probably crept in. It is rumored that the
Commissioner lately had an interview with the
Judges of the Supreme Court, who wanted him
to explain why he had, in the Consolidated
Evidence Act, taken away the right of an
accused to give evidence. The section dealing
with this question is sec. 5, which reads : “ No
person offered as a witness shall lie incompetent
to give evidence by reason of incapacity from
crime or interest,” and it appears that their
Honors do not think this covers the case of an
accused person. The point really turns on the
meaning of “ incapacity from crime or interest,”
and this involves a consideration of the history
of the changes in the law on this subject. It is
actually proposed to bring in an Act to rectify
the alleged mistake.

At North Melbourne Police-court, the other
day, when the police opposed the granting of a
collector’s license, on the ground that the
applicant had been convicted of larceny, the
man pleaded that for the four- years since
elapsed his conduct had been unexceptionable.
He also said that, as he was now in great pecuni-
ary straits, he hoped the bench would not pre-
vent him earning an honest living. The appli-
cation, however, was promptly refused ; which
makes it evident that the Law has taken to
heart Elton’s “ Babes in the Wood ” advice—
“ Don’t hit a man when he’s down—kick him !”
Any attempt to interfere with the vested
right of the mortgagee to fix his claws into
any piece of land gets short shrift in Vic-
toria. Noticeably so in connection with
Homestead Protection Reform, which can
claim almost as many abortive Bills to its
credit as Plural-Voting-Abolition itself. In
’B3 Coppin passed a Bill through the Assembly
which “never returned” from the Council. In
’B4 it was negatived on the second reading in
the Assembly. In ’95-6 M‘Coll tried unsuc-
cessfully to get it through the Assembly. In
’96 his second attempt lapsed in committee,
and so in the first session of ’97. In the second
session of ’97 he got leave to introduce it again,
but it was not printed or circulated. In ’9B it
appeared in all the pomp and panoply of a
Government measure in the Land Bill, but,
yielding to pressure, the Minister has promised
to drop it. Yet the principle is in full opera-
tion in many American States and with the
most beneficial results.
Editor Bulletin. —Great noise is made over
the occasional placing on the Sydney market
of a slightly cancerous or tuberculous beast, but
it is doubtful if people in the country eat any-
thing in the shape of meat that is not more or
less afflicted by either or both of these diseases.
Those who send in cattle to Sydney know the
strict supervision maintained there, and if a
beast goes down in which the official eye de-
tects disease it is only because the sender
could not observe it. Had the diseased condi-
tion been obvious, the animal would have
promptly gone into country consumption.
There is practically no inspection in the country.
Butchers are supposed to notify the police
when they are going to slaughter, and some-
times a constable happens round to theslaughter-
yard, to what purpose it’s hard to say, as he
probably wouldn’t know disease if he saw it.
When he does turn up they run in fairly-sound
cattle, otherwise they kill the diseased. The
evil is a crying one, and if by accident an intel-
ligent man should get into the position of
Minister for Agriculture, here is an imperative
duty ready to his hand.— Sarcoma.
N.S. Wales came very near reforming its Leg.
Council 36 years ago—nearer than it has ever
got since. Hargrave, then Attorney-General,
carried a Bill through the Council providing
for the election of members of that body. In
the Assembly it was taken up by Cowper, was
read a second time by a majority of 24 to 20,
and set down for consideration in committee for
the following Wednesday. W r hen Wednesday
came, however, Forster raised a cabal, and
the Bill was discharged from the paper by 34
to 15. That Bill, among other things, provided
a small property qualification for electors, 30
years as minimum age for candidates, and the
Hare system of voting ; but, considering the
state of N. S. W. society 36 years ago, it was by
no means an illiberal measure. It made the
Council franchise just about as democratic as
that of the Assembly, which was all that could
be expected. And considering that the Council
passed it without any difficulty—well, it is just
possible that it would also pass Premier Reid’s
long-waited-for Reform Bill if Premier Reid
would only send it along, which, so far, he
hasn’t done.

“ Tanna Tommy ” re N.S.W. black-labor
trouble :
Colored labor still continues to arrive promiscuously in
batches on the N.S.W. Northern rivers. Premier Reid’s
proposal to make the exclusion of black labor a sine qua
non for the retention of the £3 duty on sugar will not
lessen the evil, only intensify it, as it will prevent the
aliens from obtaining employment in avocation for which
they are peculiarly adapted, and drive them into competi-
tion with the white in other branches of employment,
with a consequent result of bringing down wages. We
would also have a further development of the abominable
Syrian-hawker curse, while the Kanaka would graduate
in dairying and other branches of farm work. Already
it is stated by a Tweed paper that one dairyman in that
district employs Kanakas as milkmen, and this is but the
beginning. What is wanted is Premier Reid (or, better
still, a federated Australia) to sit at the gates of Aus-
tralia and promptly fire out the black man and the
yellow man, and the little brown man from Japan, who
tries to enter, whether they be British subjects or not.
Those “silken bonds” don’t need any mixture of black
cotton in them. The colored trash already here could be
left to die out or walk out.
“ Mirracrinya,” to The Bulletin :
Why doesn’t. Mr. Meston visit S.W. Queensland?
Plenty of aboriginal abuses there for him to correct.
Only t’other day some blacks left a station near Thargo-
mindah to go kangarooing about 50 miles away, but were
promptly followed and brought back by the stockman in
charge of the station. If this is not slavery, what is ?
And right under the nose of the local police, too. Mr.
Meston, in report on working of Aboriginals’ Protection
Act, says : “ it requires very judicious administration, so
as not to cause any exasperating situation to employers."
Does this mean that squatters are not to be interfered
within their treatment of the blacks? Surely M. must
know that any station-owner who has a lot of blacks
working for nothing will feel irritated if he has in future
to pay them. Doubtless the American planters were
“ irritated ” when Lincoln abolished slavery. Mr.
Meston also says (idem): “No longer in any part of
Queensland is wrong to the aboriginais allowed to go un-
punished.” This, in face of fact that, on majority of large
S.W. cattle-stations, if a blackfellow leaves he is promplly
followed, brought back, and, in some cases, severely
punished. Also, re case (already mentioned in Bulletin)
wherein a man was fined £lO for taking a half-caste boy
from tribe, to educate—why, if one man is thus harassed,
are not those prosecuted who are openly employing
aboriginals ad lib. without authority ? There is no escape
from the conclusion that while the law is enforced against
the poor man it is relaxed in favor of the station-owner

business in Australasia. From establishment in 1843 to December 31st, 1897, this Company has paid to its Policy
Holders or their representatives over Ninety-five Million Pounds Sterling. This sum is double the amount paid to its
Policy-Holders by any other Company in the world, and demonstrates beyond cavil or doubt that the best Company
to insure in is not the Company that accumulates the largest surplus beyond its needs—for the benefit nerhans of it's
stock-holders—but the Company that does the MOST GOOD FOR ITS POLICY-HOLDERS. ’ 1 1 ’
