Pattie Browne’s season of two weeks at
Sydney Royal is getting along so far in a state of
moderate prosperity. “The Dovecote ”is only a
reasonably good comedy, and even that statement
puts a certain strain on the truth, though the
truth can stand it well enough. It was evidently
selected, like the unfortunate “Nancy,” not
because it is a brilliant burst of mirthfulness, but
because the cantankerous heroine gives Miss
Pattie a show to occupy the biggest part of the
stage. The heroine in question is a cat-like little
personage, who drives her young husband wild
with causeless jealousy, and at last bursts up the
home owing to a golden hair on his coat. The
hair arrived there under circumstances which can
bear the closest inspection, but this slap-dash
young person has no rational inspection about her.
Her loving and spotless parents, to whom the
matter is referred, agree to quarrel furiously be-
tween themselves, so as to show their Pattie the
ugliness of domestic strife, and they call each other
names in a labored, perfunctory fashion, and break
a dish unconvincingly now and then. They are
two nice old people, pleasantly delineated by
Frank Mac vicars and Mrs. Gr. B. W. Lewis, and
they deserve better luck than to be driven to
wreck their own cups in their old age.
* * * ♦
Their painful bangings-about impose on Pattie;
that masterful and interfering girl promptly decides
in her own mind that a Spanish lady living in the
next paddock is the siren who led the old man
astray, and she all but spanks the senora in a brief
interview. In fact, she takes up the matter
with such ability that she drives her miserable
parents into a real disagreement. She also teaches
the only other married lady in the play to search
her husband’s pockets for incriminating evidence
—in fact, her existence is a ceaseless search in
somebody’s pocket for evidence on the unpleasant
subject of adultery. The hair on the husband’s
coat is fully explained at last, and everybody be-
comes reconciled, and it appears that the Spanish
lady didn’t know enough English to fully under-
stand the abuse that had been heaped upon her
and wasn’t seriously offended after all, yet, by the
time the show is oyer, Miss Pattie has made her-
self so thoroughly disagreeable that the audience’s
sympathy is off. In fact, it would gladly see the
curtain fall on the sound shaking of the heroine.
♦ * * *
Miss Browne herself is in no way respon-
sible for this state of things. She does her
best with a very unsatisfactory part, arid
her best in this case is very good indeed.
But a character whose cantankerousness never
rests grows very heavy. And that rageful little
woman completely dominates the play. The com-
£any is an excellent one most of the way round.
iesly Thomson, who was unfortunately crippled
in the early stages of the season, is a quaint,
boyish little man who doesn’t take well at first,
probably because he is so unlike most other men ;
but he improves greatly on acquaintance. Eloise
Juno, in a small part as a Scotch servant, holds up
the reputation she gained in the “ Little Minister ”
as the best Scotch comedy-lady the writer can re-
member ; in fact, her casual Maggie is probably
the best thing in the production. And even the
very small parts of the cast are filled in by artists
of such dramatic tonnage as Misses Edith Barrow,
Inez Bensusan, and Noni Rickards, also Oily
Deering, Sweeney, Hawthorne, and Charles Field.
But the “ Dovecote, ” with all these advantages,
isn’t far removed from failure. If there was a
little more incident it might get on better, but,
apart from the real rows of the young people and
the perfunctory sham rows of the old ones, there
js hardly any incident at all.
** * *
Alfred Dampier opened his season at
Sydney Criterion with something which staggered
under the heavy compound name of “Every-Day
London.” In a good many respects it was like its
title. London is one of the ruts of the British
melodrama, and the dramatist pegs along in the
said rut like a small dog hurrying along a gutter.
Nineteen out of 20 modern British melodramas
are located in London, and include a view of the
Thames by night, and a glimpse of a street in the
East End where Bill sells baked ’taturs on a bar-
row, and Sam deals in pigs’ feet and winkles.
London is one of the traditions which has fallen
on the drama like a club on a belltopper, and flat-
tened it. Dampier gives less of it than usual,
however, though that necessary evil, the Thames
by night, is there in Act IV. Three or four
splendid patches of scenery adorn the Criterion
production, and it includes two ideas that are not
quite worn to death. The first is a woman who
receives an offer of =£3oo for a child (the mys-
terious gentleman in the cab wants it because his
own has died and he can’t acknowledge the death
owing to complications about a will), and in her
haste she sells him a muffled infant which is really
her own. Then she wants to gird up her skirts,
and run madly after that cab to the ends of the
earth, and tear the vehicle to pieces, and slaughter
the horse if need be, to recover her infant, but the
tramp concealed behind the fence murders her just
then and takes the £300; and her husband, re-
turning at that moment from a long voyage, is
found beside the body and gaoled for a very long
period.
Fifteen years later the same tramp, no older,
but now a well-dressed society person, hires
the father, not knowing he is the father, to pre-
tend, from base motives, to be the parent of his
own daughter—he himself not knowing she is his
daughter. Which is rather a good idea and would
be well carried out if it were not for the old stage
foolishness about the birth-certificate. A shabby
stranger calls and lays a birth-certificate on the
table, said document certifying that somebody,
who was the daughter of somebody else, was born
somewhere on some date or other, in the presence
of nobody in particular. A distracted old
gentleman looks at it and says with a shriek that
it proves the shabby man to be beyond question
the father of his girl. And if 18 persons arrived
in a row with the certificate it would prove,
according to the same idiotic stage law, that they
were all the girl’s father, even though eight of
them were women, and the rest were dogs and
Chinamen, and the oldest was only 19, and the
girl was 43. The stage birth-certificate is fast
making this paper feel angry.
* * * «
There are bits of good dialogue strewn
here and there in ‘ ‘ Every-Day London, ” though
it lags wearily in places, and wants more action,
and also a rather better company. Dampier, the
immortal, grows no older, and his splendid voice
loses none of its vigor, and his rendering of the
gentleman who gets 15 years and is afterwards
hired to personate himself is a piece of work which
could not easily be improved upon. George
Buller does a small, mean, casual ruffian in a par-
ticularly excellent fashion; in fact, there are few
men who can play a small person of loathsome
character better than George Buller; and Johnson
Weir is developing into a good, useful, all-round
actor. Master Dampier starts his stage career in
this drama as a tattered small boy, and seems a
bright youth of considerable promise. But Miss
Katherine Russell’s red-headed Susan is woe-
fully commonplace, and The Bulletin sorrowfully
concludes that the pretty Miss Rose Dampier
hasn’t inherited a spark of her father’s histrionic
talent up to date, and ought to decide right here
that her metier isn’t the stage. It says this regret-
fully, for old-man Dampier is such a general success
that his ability ought to spread all over the
family; but it doesn’t. And there is no other
lady in the company who has any spare capacity
to make up for these alarming shortages.
** * *
The Lyceum has revived Frank Harvey’s
“ Ring of Iron ” for a brief season. It is a great
many years—l 2, if The Bulletin remembers
aright—since that large piece of ironmongery was
last produced in Sydney, with Essie Jenyns,
Billolloway, Brother Charles, Albert Norman and
Alice Deorwyn in the cast. That was at the
Opera House—which dingy house up above the
pub in lower King-street was actually called the
New Opera House in those days. The “ Ring ”is
one of the best of Frank Harvey’s many dramas,
and is a good way above his usual washy standard.
Its loudest feature is the harrowing baronet who
shunts his wife into a lunatic asylum in order that
he may marry another lady. John Cosgrove puts
his extensive brawn and muscle and his big
masterful head into this part, and his size makes
the baronet a broad, convincing aggressive person
who could break all the ten commandments at
once if he fell on them. Diver, after a long course
of wickedness, takes a turn as the good man who
repairs the commandments again after Cosgrove
has broken them, and three pretty girls, of whom
Miss Helene Burdett takes The Bulletin’s fancy
most, are there mainly for the purpose of being
trodden on by Cosgrove in his career of wicked-
ness. It is growing more and more difficult to
keep pace with the Lyceum, which usually puts
up a big new shock every Saturday, and this paper
only gets out with its notice of the last shock by
the time the next shock is about due. “Lured to
Ruin ” comes next.
*
Her Majesty’s, which closed its massive
front gate for the first five days of the week, re-
opens on Saturday with “Under the Red Robe.”
Whether the opening will do justice to that fine
work of fiction remains to be seen, but this
paper has it on the best authority—that of the
advertisement—that it will do all the justice that
a big stage and a large cash expenditure admit of.
The good points about the “Red Robe” are the
story itself and the gorgeous millinery of the
period. The bad one, from a dramatic point of
view, is that it is an active tale where some of the
best incidents extend over 50 miles of bad road.
As it must necessarily be bottled into a much
smaller space than the author intended, it merely
remains for Sydney to form an opinion about the
judiciousness or otherwise of the bottling.

Dante’s exhibition of the unholy art of
magic booms, perhaps, even more this week than it
did last. The managerial statement that hun-
dreds were turned away from the doors may be
taken literally or not, just as the public pleases—
perhaps they turned away at 11 o'clock because the
performance was over. But even with all deduc-
tions the young man with the big wide smile is a
highly payable speculation.

The Sydney Tivoli bill is only in a
moderate state of glory this week, the exit of
various people having left considerable gaps in the
magnificence of things. And the projected depar-
ture of Bellman and Lottie Moore —principally
the latter—throws a certain gloom over the house,
for the agile Lottie is a general favorite, and can
invert herself in a way pleasing to the gods. Miss
Cora Caselli, a shapely high-kickist of tremendous
elevation in the high-kick line, is the most notable
. person of the present co. who will remain when
• Miss Lottie departs, but Florrie West, described
as “The Dazzler,” is due on Saturday.
** 0 *
Bristol’s circus is still in the condition
vaguely alluded to last week, and its address is,
as heretofore, on the neglected bit of waste ground
adjacent to Redfern station, Sydney. The
Alhambra also keeps up a reasonably deserving
variety entertainment, and these long-abandoned
premises begin to feel almost alive again.
** * *
“ The Lady of Lyons” revival at Melb.
Princess’s proved to be a rather rough and un-
ready show, with sundry noticeable features in it.
The most humorous was a military person who,
having a dozen lines or so to speak, made nearly
the same number of abortive attempts to remem-
ber them. Miss Meta Pelham’s mincing version
of Widow Melnotte raised an occasional laugh,
also Mrs. Bryer’s appalling appearance in several
green and red costumes gave pleasure to all. Fur-
thermore, W. F. Hawtrey, in the fine “ fat ”
character of Colonel Dumas, achieved an artistic
failure with considerable success. Some people
seemed to like Hawtrey’s rasping voice and hard,
inflexible style of walking through his part, but
to anyone who has got the aforesaid voice
“ upon his nerves,” Mr. Hawtrey begins to be very
trying. It may almost be said that there is only
one note in the voice. It would make a proposal of
marriage, or offer up a prayer for rain as though it
were reiterating curses on Black Michael, or again
advising Marcus Superbus to “ send for the
woman. ” When will Albert Whelan add Haw-
trey’s voice to his list of imitations ? George
Majeroni, in the small part of a rampageous re-
publican, made himself felt as well as heard, and
the two principals put some of their neatest work
into the contract. Claude Melnotte and Pauline
are showy characters, but they need a lot of earnest
acting. Miss Ada Ferrar did very well, whilst
Julius Knight went one better. Julius was a
bright young hero, with not too many heroics
about him, and although his delivery of the old
familiar turgid speeches became mechanical at
times, he was mostly real and always likeable.
Three nights of Bulwer Lytton’s play brought the
season, presumably, to a close on Tuesday.
** * *
“La Poupee,” with Miss Yane and Chas.
Kenningham as new arrivals in Melbourne, was
announced to open a comic-opera campaign at
Melb. Princess’s on Wednesday. Melbourne will
be glad to get some more opera.
** * *
The almighty half-dollar, supported by the
festive florin and the shilling that is nimble, come
clinking into the Rickards treasury, and Peggy
Pryde sings “He’s out upon the fuddle” (which
her mother sang of yore) to a crowded house.
Ajax, the Flexible Marvel, is the latest “ turn ” at
the Opera House. He turns himself in a large
assortment of inhuman directions, and sits upon
his own chest with an air of having been born that
way. In fact, Ajax is so very remarkable as a
contortionist that The Bulletin can hardly make
itself believe he is not identical with one of the
Kclairs (the alligator one) whom Fitz Gerald Bros,
brought to Australia three years ago.
* * * * gy
The Yic. Operatic Society performance of
“Bohemian Girl,” at Melb. Bijou, seemed to im-
prove every night during a week’s run. The best
singer of the whole tuneful co. was the baritone,
Horace Stevens, who figured as a boy soprano a
few years ago, and grew into a youthful baritone
without pausing. Count Arnheim is an absurd
character, even in old professional hands,
but young Stevens played it in a tall,
straight, deportmental style which didn’t lessen
the effect of his rich voice. J. Goudy, the
Devilshoof, came out quite strong as an
actor ; and certainly no amateur prima-donna has
knocked a Melbourne audience harder at her
first attempt than Miss (or Signorina) Cesca
Vollugi. This shapely daughter of Cecchi’s old
friend is built for the stage. A trifle taller, per-
haps, than Nellie Stewart, graceful, winning, with
gloriously-ruddy golden hair, and intelligence to
match. When she came out as a concert-singer
last year, the Vollugi’s voice seemed to have lost
some of its natural sweetness in training. As the
“Bohemian Girl” she warbled sweetly, but the
voice production was not so free and bold as be-
fore. Anyhow, Sig. Vollugi is a very welcome
angel in all respects.
Mr. and Mrs. Brough are back again—The
Bulletin is sure that it may offer them Aus-
tralia’s cordial welcome—after a successful tour in
India and around. As may be seen by accom-
panying photographs, they look none the worse
for their travel. Mrs. Brough remains as
gracious and charming as ever ; and the perennial
Robert even seems to have lost some of the
wrinkles with which misanthropic Time has
flecked his brow.
** * *
On Saturday next (29th) the reconstructed
Brough Comedy co. makes a first appearance at
Melbourne Bijou, and the best wishes of Aus-
tralia’s playgoing public will be with the manage-
ment. Of the nine newcomers in the ca two or
three have brought high reputations with them.
All of them have been selected by the critical
actor who runs the show, which is equivalent to
saying that everyone is up to the Brough
standard. Old friends, Mr. and Mrs. Brough,
Miss Temple, Mario Majeroni, and MTntyre, are
good wine, and need no bush. The season starts
with “ The Liars,” by Henry Arthur Jones.
** * *
Bland Holt returns to Melb. Royal 6n
Saturday. The previous tenants, Maurice and
Bateman, gave up the managerial ghost last week,
and rumors of strange lawsuits and things are in
the early summer air. Bland Holt will go
straight to the soul of the racing carnival with his
“Sporting Life, ” a drama that hummed in Sydney,
and is calculated to hum even louder in the city
where Bobadil amounts to a religion, and every
second man has got a dead bird concealed about
his person.
** * *
Also, the much-forgotten Alexandra is to
throw open its gates to the populace on Saturday.
Lrskine Scott, who last year produced some intel-
ligent dramas in a worthy manner at the Haleck,
has arranged to try his luck again on similar lines.
He will produce “A Miner’s Secret,” by Mark
Melford. The co. looks pretty promising, and the
name of John Wallace, as stage-manager, is a
guarantee that tli ■ scenery will hang together and
the supers, will simulate excitement when Johnnie
gives the signal for their angry passions to be let
loose.

company of vocalists to fill the building on Satur-

At Poverty Point.
“T.D. Such a pity to hear Newbury’s contralto—
Maud Dairympie —wastin'.'' her powerful voice in unskilful
singing. She is just as Crossley was when fair Ada first
came to Sydney, and a year or two of hard study would
probably enable her to pack up traps and go where the
good singers go.
“T.D. An entertaining stranger from Philistia sat
behind me last night at Newbury's pop. He joined
heartily in the disgraceful “guying” of a poor girl who
had inexperience enough to do 20min. of Grieg’s pianistic
gymnastics. When Newbury apologised for somebody’s
absence, and sang “In Native Worth” himself, the
Philistine listened for a few bars of recitative, and then
remarked that the fat little tenor must be a makeshift,
but ’e didn't sing so bad. He grew very enthusiastic,
however, when Phil let himself out on the eternal “Holy
City,” and even aired his musical knowledge by humming
the Jerusalem refrain until someone exhorted him to
shut up. But one of the adult prodigies (where does
Phillip get ’em all?) took his alien heart by storm,
and I heard the shilling-popper sum up that “ the feller
with the voice like a female was best of all—and ’e never
put on no crimson frills, neither.’’
Cabled that Florence Esdaile helped to allay the panic
at London Tivoli, the other night, when the biograph
lantern went off with a bang, setting Are to the adjacent
woodwork and filling the house with smoke. If the ex-
Melbourne soprano gave them many top-notes amidst
the smoke she deserved a substantial cheque for her
trouble.

Anson (writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent) is
on tour as the real Mackay in “ Too Much Johnson,” and
later gets a good chance at the London Garrick. He is
newspaperly-termed “ one of the finest actors on the
stage,” so it looks as if George’s turn were cominn-.
Nellie Stewart jibbed out of the Chevalier engagement.”
The great, tired portion of London (writes Tiie Bulle-
tin’s correspondent) is daily “ bussing” it to the Aqua-
rium to see the Australian fat family (the two females
and the male weigh 79 stone in the aggregate). All the
credit of the development of these elephantine infants is
put down to the fact that they were born of Devonshire
parents who had been weaned on their native cream be-
fore emigrating. The Vic. Agent-General is most remiss
in not advertising Victorian butter in this connection.
The husband of the “ Georgia Magnet” met Bouge-
mont-Grein in Wellington 12 months ago, and addressing
him by his old name, the cannibal chief produced a card
with the name “ Eougemont,” and explained—" Ssh,
I’ve got a game on.”
The properties used in the production of the
‘Bed Bobe” are a picturesque feature of that dressy
drama ; and the old oak setting of the second act is a
thing to be borne in mind by any democratic Australian
the next time he furnishes a boom mansion on ancestral
lines. Hassan, the builder of this effective spectacle, is
remembered as the eccentric donkey who pranced to the
energetic smile of panto. Lonnen. Opinions were divided
at the time as to the merits of the performers, but this
paper gave its vote for the donkey without a moment’s
hesitation. Hassan is a clever fellow.
Arthur Bateman, who found the money for Melb. T.E.,
shows, “ Bound the World in Eighty Days,” etc., had a
decent lump left him by his father, who long part-owned
and vigorously edited Bathurst Courier. During a snow-
fall in Ballarat 16 years ago editor Bateman received such
a vigorous snow-balling that next day came a dignified
leader upon the irreverence of the rising generation. He
always regretted parting with the Courier, which he con-
sidered his life’s mascotte.
Prof. Bristol s counting horse was answering simple
questions in arithmetic at a Sydney circus, the other
night, by stamping the correct number of times with one
forefoot. The gods kept him going, too. Quick the
questions came, “ 4 from 8?” “7 from 15?” and so on.
At last a small, squeaky voice was heard saying, “ 3 from
2. ” There was a general laugh as the Professor told the
clever youngster to come and do it himself.
Fremantle loses few chances of stressing the fact that
it’s “ the port of Westralia. ” Lately its municipal coun-
cil invited “the Firm’s” Sign of the Cross co. thither;
but the municipal enterprise was rewarded only with the
reply that the local town-hall stage wasn’t good enough.
Not to be beaten, the council at once resolved to make
the stage accommodation better. So the municipal
theatre gets close to the horizon.
That eccentric solicitor P. A. C. O’Far-
rell, who died last week in Melb. hospital, recently
sent a characteristically abusive letter to Chief
Justice Madden, giving, among other things, the
writer’s opinion in plain terms of the learned one’s
legal equipment. His Waxcellencty replied, cour-
teously excusing the letter under the circum-
stances, and enclosing a fiver. The eccentric one
was flabbergasted with the very welcome coals of
fire, and what is more, wasn’t too proud to feel
touched.
Henry Barkly, whose death at 83 is re-
ported from London, will be best remembered as
Governor of Victoria. He also governed Tas-
mania, the Cape, Mauritius and other places, but
he spent seven years in Victoria, and was one of
the two Governors whom that loyal territory
salaried at >£15,000. He was Governor there when
the Constitution was proclaimed. His first wife
was buried in Melbourne. He retired from the
Imperial service over 20 years ago on a substantial
pension.

The Great Boulder scandal is not dying out, as
many another W.A. scandal before it has done, and the
ignominious retreat, or dismissal, of the directors for their
■conduct in this unpardonable business seems now only a
matter of time. The shareholders’ committee has sent
over the world a complete text of the agreement with the
Milling Co., a circular indicating its next move, and a
pamphlet giving 50 extracts from the English and Aus-
tralian press on the subject. The well-greased, and,
therefore, very greasy, rags that live on the game, and
die when it’s all over, continue to barrack for the direc-
tors. “Much satisfa''t ; on ” is expressed at the “great
success of the process,” and it has been predicted that if
the board could only stave off the outcry till October, all
would be well by reason of the results then obtained.
Here, at the end of that month, nothing satisfactory is
forthcoming. On the other hand, there is room for antici-
pationsof comparative failure. Thefeelingamongstmining
managers of the field is almost unanimous that Konemann
will never be able to make his estimated extractions.
Hamilton, the Boulder manager, while rendering every
assistance in its preparation, expresses no opinion. What-
ever the result—very good or very bad—the directorscan-
not escape punishment for making an outrageous contract
for the use of an unknown process.

* * * *
George Brookrnan told a deputation of local
shareholders in Adelaide last week it was not true the
Bake View South Co. was £30,000 in debt, but it had a
credit balance of £6000 ; nor was it true the mine would
soon shut down. He would try to get an additional local
director appointed, but held out little hope of that being
agreed to. Wait till the balance-sheet comes out. The
mine has been a disappointment. It’s only hope is depth,
and should it be found that one of the Lake View ore
chutes dip into it, Whittaker Wright—now that Kaufman
has retired—will have his eagle eye fixed upon it. The
policy is to wait and watch, more particularly watch.
Understood that the mine will soon be opening up at the
500ft. level to see what it is stuffed with at that depth.
Better to go down another 200ft. and then open out.
Another disappointment at 500ft. may cause the mine to
be sold too cheap.

* * • •
Many variegated yarns are told of the richness
of the lode in the Lake View Consols mine, W.A., at the
300ft. south level. Six weeks ago it was known that, for
eight days, the average value for 8ft. wide was 32oz.
varying from 19oz. to UOoz. per ton. Test holes put in the
sides, ’tis said, prove this richness is not confined to the
drift, and it is also of considerable length—in fact, said that
the face has been walled-up, pending instructions from
manager Callahan, who, ’tis now said, has gone to consult
with Whittaker-Wright at Cairo, and will return to the
mine immediately afterwards.
* * * *
The new battery of 40 stamps lately started at
the Galleymont gold-mines is one of the finest gold mill-
plants at work in N.S.W., and is well worth inspection by
parties contemplating erection of similar machinery. The
ore is fed automatically through rockbreakers and feeders
without shovelling, and the whole is most substantially
built. The engines are a fine pair of horizontal compound
type, driving the battery with ropes. Mr. M. Eissler,
the managing engineer, speaks in the highest terms of
the construction and design of the plant, which was built
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne.*
* * • *
“Flabby Paul,” to The Bulletin, from “Ta-
mata,” Mambare River, New Guinea, 6/10/’98 There are
about 150 miners, of whom some do fairly well. Two men
are getting an ounce a day each. Flour is 14s. a fifty at
Tamata, but it costs 20s. to have it carried to the field—a
distance of 25 miles. Carriers are scarce ; some miners
are unable to procure any. Carriers’ wages are 10s. per
month and rations. They can each carry about 351b., and
it takes them three days to go and three days to return,
so you see each 351b. costs 2s. 6d. and rations for the boy,
besides the price of the goods. One miner died of fever
about three weeks back, and four had to go away by last
boat. Two carriers died last week of dvsentery. There
is not much danger from the natives between ‘here and
the field, although two prospectors had one of their boys
speared t’other side of the field. This place (Tamata) is
about 60 miles up the river. The Govt, surveyor is
laying-out a township here, and is going to survey
another at the field. The last two natives concerned in
the murder case have been captured.
F. A. Rocbfort to The Bulletin, from Gira
goldfield, B. New Guinea (10/9/’98)Misleading state-
ments are being printed in the Australian press about the
great finds of gold on the Gira River, in British Papua.
It would be well for men to think twice ere coming here.
Fair gold was got in three short creeks running into the
Gira, but these have been mostly worked out, and since
their opening nothing new has been found. The nearest
creek to Tamata, the Govt, station on the Mambare, is
about 30 miles over a very rough track ; closely adjoining
are the other two. The number of men at present work-
mg on the so-called goldfield is about 80, half of whom
are not making tucker. The Ivanhoe has also brought
63 men from Cooktown and Woodlark, all of whom intend
coming here. Packing by carriers is expensive, 6d. per
pound being charged by storekeepers ; so food is a costly
item to men getting little. Carriers are few, and it will
take them all their time to keep going the present popu-
lation with, the addition of the Ivanhoe crowd, and when
their time is up they will be hard to replace, as the mor-
tality among them of late has been excessive. Useless
therefore, men coming here without “ bovs,” as they can-
not carry their own swag and rations. "There is plenty
country to prospect, but it must be borne in mind that it
is only prospecting they are coming for. Fever, as usual
is prevalent; and dysentery has appeared in a bad form.

.The National Bank (Melbourne) shows an im-
provement for the half-year to 30th Sept., 1898, the net
profits being £17,602. (The National has a ridiculous
nabit of putting down “ IVet profit, £20,526,” and then
aeciuoting note and income tax, £2924, wherefore a lot of
nnancial editors, even in papers of the standing of the
Age, describe the £80,526 as net profit.) This is nothing
' el .' gieat, still it is above the National Bank’s recent
averages, as the following statement of profits shows :
March, 1894 .. £31,312 Sept., 1896 .. £11,706
hept 1894 .. 25,231 March, 1897 .. 14,712
March 1895 .. 15,126 Sept., 1897 .. 18,275
Sept., 1895 .. 13,763 March, 1898 .. 15,471
March, 1896 .. _ 10,523 Sept., 1898 .. 17,602
The National pays its usual div. of 5 per cent, to prefer-
ence shareholders, £7645, and the remainder, added to
previous balance, leaves £47,438 at credit of profit and
loss account. The ordinary shareholders stand out
as usual ; after the preference crowd has got its
per cent., the balance would only pay the drdi-
? ar ' .people 1 per cent, and leave a fraction over,
respite the improvement, the National’s profit are
only equal to £1 14s. 9d. per cent, per annum on the
shareholders funds, which is not a particularly good re-
turn. There will probably be an improvement through
saving of interest when the last of the 4) per cent, recon-
structed deposits are paid off ; but the amount of these —
though not stated separately in the balance-sheet—is not
no "G The relief in this direction may increase the
profits sufficiently to make them equal to a 2 per cent,
div. to ordinary shareholders, but even that is none too
certain. To pay a 5 per cent. div. to preference and a
very humble 3 per cent, to ordinary shareholders requires
a net profit of £32,692, which is nearly twice the present
earnings, and if it is proposed to make any serious
attempt at building up a reserve as well the earnings need
to be still larger.
Tbe National has a very large and solid busi-
reserve of cash and liquid securities
(£-.,49/,230) is very good, equalling about 37 per
cent, of all liabilities to the public. Why, with this
massive reserve, a greater effort is not made to pay off
the remainder of the 4j per cent, deposits is something of
a. mystery. There would be at least a saving of some
thousands per annum by the process, and the cash reserve
se.ems needlessly large. It is difficult to form an opinion
as to whether the small earnings are only owing to
depression, or whether the National has still some depre-
ciation to write off, but probably the depression is mainly
responsible. At all events, the situation is none too
bright . , r , , e ordinary shareholders—especially those
who paid £5 per share premium on the new issue of £4
shares in 1887. The National’s present troubles are
mainly owing to the boom policy of its directors in earlier
days. In the 35 years previous to ’93 it paid £2,686,329 in
dividends, and only put £199,424 of its profits to reserve
fund—the other £470,576 of the reserve being made up by
the sale of shares at a premium. A little less 12 per cent,
fi"’- ln the old days would have done a great deal to
lighten the present strain.
* * * *
The Colonial Bank (Melbourne) has now so far
improved that the interest on reconstructed deposits has
been raised from 2£ to 3 per cent. The Colonial re-
arranged its disastrous affairs in 1893 at 4| per cent. In
1895 it re-arranged some more, and reduced the interest
on deposits to 2 .1- per cent, for five years, to be increased
to 3 per cent, when the earnings allowed. After the five
years, which expires in 1900, the reconstructed funds
are to bear interest at the current rate for 12 months’ de-
posits. The present is the first half-year when the Colo-
mal has contrived to pay the full 3 per cent., and its
profits compare with previous results as follows :
September, 1835 .. £7BO September, 1897 .. £3312
March, 1896 .. .. 2564 March, 1898 .. 3398
September, 1896 .. 2004 September, 1898 ~ 853
March, 1897 .. .. 3043
The present half-year’s profits would appear as £4164 but
for the extra, $ per cent, paid on deposits, so there is
really a considerable improvement. During 1 the half-year
the Colonial has paid off £128,988 of its reconstructed
debts, which are now down to £1,267,393, and this is
another evidence of progress. The cash and liquid assets
(£400,331) have declined £150,000, and now equal 17 per
cent, on all liabilities to the public, which is strong enough
for all purposes, seeing that the great bulk of said liabili-
ties are fixed for long periods. Still, it is about the
smallest percentage of cash reserve in Victoria, and with
so little of its money lying idle the Colonial ought to
show better results than it does.
The profit, after paying 3 per cent, to depositors,
which is about the same rate as paid by other banks,
equals a shade over 4s. 4d. per cent, per annum on the
paid-up capital of £788,167. Of this, however, it is
admitted that £431,166 is lost and has to be written
off, so the profits equal 9s. 6d. per cent, per annum
on the capital (£357,000) which is alleged to be
good and intact. If the earnings (as compared
with those of even the worst of other banks) are any fair
criterion, then it certainly looks as if not more than
£150,000 of that capital at the very outside was intact if
the assets were written down to their present bed-rock
value. The Colonial is doing a very slow recovery, and
another year or two may tell a different story, but this is
certainly how things appear just now. The formal
writing-down of capital is still postponed owing to these
mysterious “difficulties” so often alluded to, but by a
steady process of forfeiture and by applying profits in
reduction of the old debit balance the said balance is
steadily diminishing. Apparently the Colonial has a very
weak crowd of shareholders, and calls are hard to collect
During the half-year another 6020 shares have been for-
feited for non-payment, which makes 44,578 forfeited in
all, or more than one-third of all the ordinary shares in
the bank. The whole ordinary paid-up capital is now
£484,123, so if the admitted losses were written off instead
of being carried forward there would remain only £53,000
ordinary capital and £304,044 preference capital in the
bank—or £357,000 altogether. This is certainly £06,000
better than the situation 18 months ago, for then the ad-
mitted losses were £43,000 more than the ordinary capital,
and it seems probable enough that the improvement will
continue. Still, it is a very bad situation even now, and
requires a great deal of improvement.

The South British Insurance Co. (Maoriland)
for the year ended 31st August, shows a profit of only
£6371, which is reduced by the writing of £441 off furni-
ture, &c., to £5930. The underwriting business shows an
actual loss of £4896 by reason of the great Melbourne
fire, but this is more than made good by interest on in-
vestments. The usual 17£ per cent, dividend absorbs
£11,310, so the co. ends the year with £5380 less cash than
it had at the beginning. This sort of thing doesn’t hap-
pen frequently to the South British, and it is a solid con-
cern with £64,000 paid-up capital and £176,000 of reserves
the kind of insurance co. which seems to grow in none
of the Seven Provinces save Maoriland—and can stand an
occasional shock of the kind. Still the 17£ per cent,
dividend looks rather too magnificent in-such a bad year.
* * * *
A casual remark in the winding-up report of the
Southern Insurance Co. (Melbourne), which lately sold its
business to the British and Foreign, is to the effect that
there is probably. £lO,OOO of depreciation to be allowed
for in the securities. This is equal to one-tenth of the
entire investments. To put the matter in the most un-
°l!?* irus ’ ve way it would seem to imply that a consider-
able part of the alleged assets'are quite well known to be
lost, and therefore the figures put- in the balance-sheet
are more or less misleading ; that the board is aware of
the situation, and isn’t at all ashamed of it ; that it went
on paj'ing big dividends so long as it could make even a
pretence of earning the money to pay them with, and
made no attempt to adequately provide for losses ; and
that its balance-sheet was not an exact and complete
statement of the co.’s affairs by any means. And that’s
just what is the matter with a whole lot of companies
whose directorates haven’t the courage to tell the whole
truth about them, or are too tired to face the situation.

vious half-year’s balance) making it £85,000. In 1892, the

years by £50,000. The Tooth Co. is now ten years old,
and so far its career has been very prosperous.

Three years ago the Union Mortgage and Agency Co.
handed over a lot of its properties to a subsidiary co.—the
Australian Estates and Mortgage Co. Of the Union’s
assets £400,000 consists of 5J per cent, debentures in its
own “ pup,” and £1,229,930 consists of shares therein.
The total paid capital of the new co. is only £1,250,000, so
the old co. practically owns the lot. Therefore all the
Union’s paid-up capital and nearly £300,000 more is
represented by shares in its mysterious relative, the other
concern, which other concern has only paid one dividend
in three years, and that was 1 per cent. This year the
pup pays no div. and shows a loss of £933, so it would be
interesting to know what these £1,229,930 of shares are
really worth. And on top of all the Union Mortgage and
Agency writes off £lOO,OOO of its alleged reserve of
£175,000 to cover losses.
There are seven large pastoral co.’s doing busi-
ness in Australasia. Last year three showed a loss and
one showed a small profit only by leaving a good propor-
tion of its debenture-interest unpaid. Four paid no
dividends. Three have no reserve funds whatever, and
one has even to square its accounts by putting the accu-
mulated loss among the assets. Altogether the pastoral
business has fallen on evil days. Dalgety and Co., with
its 8 per cent, dividend, and the Australian Mortgage Land
and Finance, which pays 7J, are the only large concerns
which seem to be feeling reasonably good in this time of
woe.

B.H. Block 14 Co., having got out of its diffi-
culty with the slag-wheelers, &c., at the Port Adelaide
smeiters, relieves itself of responsibility by letting the
work, over which the trouble arose, to contractors. And,
curiously, the contractors are the president and vice-
president of the new workmen’s union. The riled mem-
bers, who were looking forward to making another appeal
to the co. shortly, as the manager had invited them to,
promptly expelled the two officers and elected others.
But that doesn’t end the contract.

Cobar-Chesney. Main shaft, the manager re-
ports, going down to 560 ft. before opening out. Sulphide
veins in shaft at 500 ft. show that iron is increasing—all
that is wanted to make the ore a self-fluxing proposition.
North of the shaft at 360 ft. the lode is already proved
100 ft. wide of good value. In this as well as in the
southern ore body winzes are going down in excellent
ore.
* * * *
Acting-Judge Sly heard at Broken Hill a civil
case which has put wicked thoughts into the minds of
White Cliffs opal miners. The representatives, respec-
tively of the biggest Sydney and Melbourne buyers (one
of them was Aaron something and the other Mark some-
thing to the same effect) had a row; and in the end one
of them called the other a robber—which is a thing, of
course, that no gem-merchant could possibly stand. The
£5OOO he asked for dwindled, however, to a farthing by
the time the jury had finished with it. Meantime,
witnesses had stated the profit on opal-buying variously
at from 25 to some odd thousands per cent. For one par-
cel, worth over a thousand, the price paid was £'Bo, ol
which the share of I he lucky getters (they nearly all work
on tribute) was just £4O. It isn’t surprising that the
manager for the English co., which monopolises much of
the pick of the field, has lately thrown up his billet and.
gone opal-buying himself; and if they hadn’t taken re-
turn tickets his Honor, and barristers Wise and Gannon,,
and all the rest of the court would probably have giveni
Sydney and the law best with the same object in view.
Anyhow, the exposures have done good, and White Cliffs;
tributors and other sellers are for the moment complain-
•ng less than usual. The population of the field was never
before so great; and, though big finds are not common,
the output of average gem was never so consistent, nor-
was the product ever more marketable—in the seven-year-
history of this field, that is.
* « # *
If, as alleged, the air is impure at less that 500 ft.
in the big shaft going down on the Balmain side of the-
Harbor for the Sydney Colleries Co., what is it likely to-
be at five and six times that depth? A gigantic under-
taking this, the result of which is very problematical.
Also alleged that the hour’s delay that takes place when
a blast misses fire, as must often occur in such a shaft, is
docked from the miners’ wages. If so, it is the “ nearest”'
thing writer has ever heard of in mining work. After an
hour every man going back takes his life in his hand, and
the fact that he has lost an hour’s pay must be a lovely
consolation while he is engaged in the horribly- dangerous;
work of picking out a missed hole. Perhaps if he went
back in 10 minutes and got blown to atoms occasionally,,
tiie management would not deduct it! Holes will miss-
fire in face of every precaution.

« * * *
A White Cliffs man to The Bulletin : — Enough,
copper has quite recently been found about 100 miles N.E..
of Broken Hill to make the hair of any Mt. Lyell or Chil-
lagoe man stand on end ; but nothing is said about it,
because it is “ premature.” In this case premature means
quite a lot. Since the removal of the Broken Hill smelters
to Port Pirie, the Tarrawingee flux-quarries have been
shut down, and the railway from Broken Hill to Tarra-
wingee is offered for sale to Govt, for 115,000 (length, 40-odd
miles). If Govt, does not buy it, the rails are to be taken
up and exported to Tasmania (Emu Bay). The culverts,
bridges, cuttings, embankments, ballast, etc., will not be
exported to Tas., but will be in situ, and will, one would
think, be silent witnesses of the busy hum of the good old
days of Tarrawingee. No fear ! N.S.W. Parliament will
be asked to sanction the construction of a line by private
enterprise to connect the Hill with the new copper pro-
vince, and the aforesaid ownerless culverts, bridges, cut-
tings, ballast, etc., will come handy for the new line,
which will only be 60 or 70 miles longer, and which
will traverse country offering but small engineering
difficulties. While negotiations are pending, ample time
is gained to allow the copper-industry to be taken in hand
by the “ proper crowd,” and the stage of “prematurity’*
will have passed.

Considering the ease with which mining scrip
may be forged and got out of hand, it is surprising that
more manufactured paper of the New Perseverance and
Landy’s Dream character is not marketed. There are
thousands of buyers and sellers of shares who could not
tell genuine scrip from the simplest colorable imitation.

Some Q. miming items. Both Gympie and
Towers again flattened out, the former actually shivering,
most of the prematurely-born pups doing a financial gasp.
S. Glanmire and Monkland latest addition to Gympie mal-
contents with 2310z5. from 291 tons. Gt. Eastern and
Orient, 6820z5. from 604 tons, paying 6d. div. On the
Towers one of the biggest and best mines contemplates
doubling its share-list; why, Heaven only knows. “ Water-
ing ” is a besetting sin of the Towers, and in the past did
more harm than, the cat. Vic. and Cal. Block, after weary
years, pays a modest 3d. div. The Brilliant pays its
hundredth div. ; total distribution to date, £507,000.
Victoria and Queen slumped owing to poor results from
last crushing. Brilliant and St. George, 1238 tons for
16250z5. Band of Hope, 650 tons, 5640z5. Victoria G.M.
co., 280 tons, 4940z5. (she was reported to be raked out 3
years ago but has been paying monthly divs. ever since).
Kelly’s Blocks, 252 tons, 5570z5, Brilliant Extended, 412
tons, 213ozs.—still low. Queen’s Cross down to 3s. 3d.,
owing to non-div. crushings. Papuans, ditto, ditto, 6s.
* • » *
Since the beginning of the year about 2000 acres
have been taken up in mining leases on Gympie, implying
an extra expenditure of £9OOO per month. As a result of
the boom, calls have gone up to £14,169 for one month
(Sept.) The calls for ’97 averaged only £7OOO per
month. Since Jan. ’9B nearly 3,000,000 new shares have
been created and 34 new co.’s registered with a nominal
capital of £388,000. New and powerful winding plants
are being erected—particularly on what may be called
New Gympie—away to the east of the old workings.
Value of new plant erected this year is put down at
£20,000.
* » * *
At Ch. Towers recently two miles of creek sands,
with three cyanide plants, were offered for sale and with-
drawn when the bidding reached £34,000. 18 months
ago the land was open to the first appropriator. Com-
panies have been recently formed for production of lime
necessary for cyaniding. ... At a gully near Mt. Daven-
port (Cape River) alluvial gold has been recently found in
small quantities and some 50 men are employed.
* * * *
Vesuvius, Charters Towers—the representative
in London reports that he is confident of finding money
there to keep this mine going. Well, the Victorians in-
terested have had a mighty hard time with it for six
years. A reef has been cut, and it would be Bull’s
luck if he came in and reaped the benefit after about 100
calls had been paid to find a reef.

Croydon items. Sept, returns : Mills, 5421 tons,
68360 z.—an increase over preceding month of 2866 tons
and 43910 z. For Sept, quarter results were : Mills, 10,281
tons for 11,3460 z. ; cyanide, 3673 tons, 28070 z. Stone of
magnificent quality—estimated of record richness for
Croydon—was being raised at No. 5 and 6 Golden Gate
when mail left. No. 7 Golden Gate crushed something
over 400 tons for a cake weighing 1800oz. Bull will see
the slug at the Exhibition. Croydon business men and
miners have set themselves to disprove Geologist Jack’s
contention that the famous Iguana reef is lost beyond re-
covery. Local co. now sinking on a very promising indi-
cation that reef will be picked up again as good as new.
When mail leaving Croydon (Oct. 10) a lot of mystery
surrounded the alleged purchase by a Finch-Hatton syn-
dicate of Rogers’ No. 1 Golden Gate mine. Ex-newspaper
man Morgan went ’Ome to float the No. 1 for as much as
be could get over £60,000. He got considerably under
that figure; at least an alleged offer was “got” for the
purchase of the mine at £10,000, and a fifth interest in
paid-ups. This was a big drop, but the owner accepted—
and at latest he hadn’t even got the £10,000. It was a
big deal for Morgan, and he got several columns of a
slobber interview on the strength of his successful efforts
to sell. Of course the money and the mine are all right,
but Bull appeared to be very chary about closing.

Lock washday
Last Sunday was appointed in N.S.W. as
a day of prayer and humiliation for rain, but some-
how the rain came before the prayer, so there are
no definite results to show. The question is left
just where it was, which is nowhere in particular
and not very much of that. The situation goes
into these three propositions. (1) Rain was
bound to come after prayer because rain comes
after everything, including murder, hash and
measles—if you only wait long enough. (2) When
rain comes after prayer, even a very little rain and
a very long way after, it may be the result of
prayer or it may not. It is safe to bet that the
clergy will say it is the result, anyhow. (3) If
rain comes before prayer the clergy may claim
that it came because they were going to pray;
they have enough assurance to do that also. And
that about sums up the situation.
* * * *
Notable that, whereas the N.S.W. Pro-
testant Churches changed their programmes as
soon as rain came, and offered prayers of thanks-
giving, the R.C.’s were not so easily satisfied, and
in most of their churches pleaded for more rain in
the forsaken districts which will probably be
again bewailing the drought ere many weeks are
past.
* * * *
Waxellency Madden, retiring from the
Vic. Executive Council at the end of his six-
months’ dramatic season, said it was a great thing
for the province to have “ a man of Lord Brassey’s
experience as its Governor.” This w 7 eek Brassey
will be back upon the job, and one of his first
duties will be to congratulate the province on
having a Madden to represent our Gracious Queen
A DEGENERATE.
SHE: "How strangely low, sweet, dreamy music like that affects me!"
HE: "And me. I never hear it without regretting those sins I have-not committed."
whilst Brassey is droning away elsewhere. It is a
matter of utter indifference to the Vic. public
what these potentates say about one another.
Their measures have been taken long ago. One
man is awfully gloomy, and the other fantastically
spectacular. On their respective “owns” they
are about equally unsatisfactory, but they would
go very well mixed—like square gin and ginger-
beer.
• * * *
All Saints’ Church, Sutton Forest, the fane
which N.S.W. ruralising Governors patronise,
has been breaking a solemn church law for years
—in fact, since its erection—without taking any
account of the transgression. The Ten Command-
ments which should be carved or painted up in
sight of all were never displayed there, the reason
now given being that there was no room for them.
This plainly is a very insufficient reason and Gov.
Hampden takes a hand in remodelling the church
and setting the Ten Commandments in their
proper place.
“ Porthos ”: Hear the other side ! Money
spent in sending female missionaries to China pro-
vides amusement for that large section of the
population that must take its pleasure sadly or
not at all. And, as for wasted lives. The girls’
lives would be just as truly wasted in typing
at 7s. a week. Also, the Christians are as fully
justified in risking the lives of a few girls (for the
possible benefit of Chinkies) as the larger Austral-
asian public is in risking the lives of boy-riders
for the problematical improvement of horses.
* ♦ * *
A few- “good young men” and saintly
virgins hired a certain hall wherein to convert
the local Chow. John “ welly muchee likee,”
and there were so few backsliders that the young
men got suspicious. Dropped in late one night
and found young lady sitting beside John guiding
one' yellow claw carelessly through the alphabet
while the other arm was squeezing her waist. The
blackboard was plentifully decorated with loving
phrases. “Gland fun ” for John, but the young
men retired from the “ propagation of the Gospel ”
under such circumstances.
* ♦ * *
Re relics of pagan superstition amongst
1 Clistian ” Chinkies. Present writer visited a
wealthy Chinese at his elegantly-furnished villa
where he lives with his white wife, and is waited
on by a full staff of white domestics. In the fire-
place of the sumptuously-furnished drawing-room
was to be seen a little box of wax from which
sprouted a chevaux de frise of tandstickor-like
joss-sticks, which the host hesitatingly explained
were a protection against the powers of darkness.
Every ventilator and fanlight was similarly
guarded.
* * m •
In Broken Hill the Church of England
doesn’t own a stick of property. Years back the
mortgagee came down and sold up manse (poor
old Parkos laid the foundation-stone) and churches
and schools—and the parson bought them all on
his little own. And now, being anxious to leave
the city of dust and drought, he is offering them
at a sacrifice. This is a rare opportunity to secure
a job-lot in churches which ought not to be missed
—by those who have not heard of the amazing
liberality of church-collections.
* * » *
Not the least amongst the absurdities of
the recent “Reformation” fandango at Sydney
Town Hall was one rabid parson’s motion expres-
sive of “great thankfulness for the Reformation
of th o fifteenth century.” Nor was any man found
to say him chronologically nay, because, probably,
there wasn’t one among the sainted barrackers for
Luther who wouldn’t have made the same vulgar
blunder. And, after the Reformation ramp had
been ramped, the Ritualistic-rave was raved—
about bobbings and bendings and smoke-pots and
saintly smock-frocks, and things generally of no
more practical importance than the clerical no-
bodies who should never have had the citizens’hall
wherein to air their paltry fanaticism.

When Judge Docker, recently trying, at
Forbes, a larceny case, wanted to explain to the
jury that they must acquit unless they found that
THEBE’D BEEN A PREVIOUS AFFAIR.
FRIEND OF THE FAMILY : “ Now, tell me, dear, who teas the
first woman?"
DEAR : “ Well — er — ma’s never been able to find out yet; but she’s
going to make it hot for pa if ever she DOES!”
the property was removed “ with intent to steal,”
he simplified this difficult English phrase into the
animo furandi of the law-books with the result
that five minutes after retiring the jurymen were
all yelling “ animo furandi ”at one another. At
last, by promising to shout drinks when they
were discharged, t a juror gptt a hearing.
“Gentlemen, we have to find that the prisoner
removed this property animo furandi or else
acquit him. Well, animo means ‘animals,’ and
furandi ‘wild animals.’ Therefore, the man’s
innocent ; it was only tame sheep he stole.”
Acquitted accordingly.
* * * *
The “Pen and Brush Club,” recently
formed in Sydney, will be an interesting' experi-
ment to watch. Its objects are professedly
Bohemian, and the Philistine will be rigidly ex-
cluded. It is to consist of artists (whose absolute
unanimity amongst themselves is so well-known),
musicians (whose mutual feelings can only be
described as more than brotherly), and journalists
(who will probably be expected to act as the
harmonising salad between the oil and vinegar
of artistic rivalry). Bohemian clubs in Sydney
have so notoriously failed or filed that it would
really seem as if the abhorred business man and
Philistine were indispensable. Perhaps this ex-
periment may be the exception which proves the
rule. At all events, The Bulletin wishes it
success.
Melb. Tocsin charges The Argus with
blasphemy for tipping “ Rock of Ages ” for the
Debutant Stakes on the Bth inst. It refers the
matter to three clergymen, including the reputed
author of the Argus articles against Marshall
Hall, for settlement.

An amusing misprint occurred in S.A.
Advertiser t’other day. The Chief Justice is
reported as urging the Society of Arts to widen its
scope by holding exhibitions of work in black and
white, etchings, wood-carving, and art needle-
work. Just at this point the titled widow of an
English army-surgeon is made to exclaim —“Hear
dear ! ” To make things worse, the little Chief is
no longer a bachelor.
Man is at heart a gara-
uler. In Perth (W.A.), the in-
mates of the local Invalid
Dep&t are in the habit of betting
chews of tobacco on how long a
sick fellow inmate will live.
Some of the chaps plunge heav-
ily in this line. In the estab-
lishment named the remunera-
tion for carrying a corpse to the
dead-house is a glass of grog,
while a fig of tobacco is given
to the one told off to perform
certain duties which, though un-
savory, are so eagerly coveted
that fights often take place as
to who shall have the privilege
of performing them.
* » »
“Hermes”: As the light
of a Sydney sunset streamed
across Hyde Park, I watched a
deadbeat stagger past the rose-
bed to where the steps led down
to the Refuge. He had, despite
his misery, a cultivated and in-
tellectual countenance, with, in
his hollow eyes, the awful ex-
pression of a refined soul that
had lost hope. Suddenly he
swayed, stopped, stretched out
one arm convulsively, and fell
in a huddled heap. As I bent
down to loosen his collar I heard
him quote “ Heloise! Dost
thou remember me a little still?”
Then a policeman took him away
in a cab, and I saw him no
more; but often, as I walk
home through the park when
the evening light is slanting
through the trees, I think of
the poor deadbeat and his sunset
memory of Heloise.
Gov. Buxton told a country agricultural
show crowd at Burra that he would not come
back to Adelaide without his wife. Now, H.E.
has confided to his intimates that Lady Victoria
does not intend to return.
» * * *
Mayor Tucker, of Adelaide, deliberately
postponed the date of his annual ball until after
the departure of Gov. Buxton for England,
because he had determined not again to ask His
Ex., and he did not wish to make the snub too
palpable. Last year the Governor attended with-
out any of the ladies of his entourage, and, as he
deigned no explanation of their absence, the
Mayoress’ feelings were naturally very much
hurt.
* * * *
The' recent committal for trial at Nowra
(N.S.W.) of a 16-year-old lad for improper inti-
macy with his 14-year sister (resultant baby), calls
attention once more to the fact that the frequency
of such horrible cases is due to the prevailing bush
inadequacy of sleeping accommodation for big
families. Boys and girls (at the critical age) bunk
together in the most unblushing fashion ; and so
usual in some provincial parts is this gross disre-
gard for common decency that only occasionally
does one hear a condemnatory comment.
* * *
Apropos recent attempts at legislation on
behalf of the distressed females of Tasmania, an
M.P. relates this story: Two girls (one 30, the
other 23, and both deaf and dumb) had, up to last
year, become possessed of four unauthorised
children between them. The unmarried mothers
dwelt at home with their parents. All sorts of
efforts—official and other—were made to discover
the males responsible, but neither of the girls
being able to hear, speak or write made the dif-
ficulty insurmountable. If the man or men
wanted had come into view while the inquiries
were being made, the girls might have pointed at
the guilty (or innocent) person, but the base
deceivers kept out of the way, and so evaded the
“point.” Eventually the local M.P. got the girls
into a charitable institution. Shortly after ad-
mission, the elder died. The other one, though
carefully guarded in the institution, and never
allowed out even to visit her home, unless the
Earents took her away personally, and brought
er back same day, was recently delivered of
another unauthorised infant. But this time she
pointed at one of the institution officials. The
man indignantly denied the paternity, and, so
far, there is nothing beyond the girl’s very
emphatic point to connect him with the trouble.
Possibly she was pointing with some totally dif-
ferent idea in her mind—there is no evidence to
show one way or the other. There the matter
rests, and the chances are that there will be no
more heard about it. The charitable institution
(a Govt, concern) keeps the baby inmate, and writer
wouldn’t be surprised to hear that it was regis-
tered on the books as “A. Point.” Tas. officials
are prolific of grim humor.
* * ♦ *
A miner who recently died at Wangaratta
Hospital (Vic.) under chloroform, was, according
to autopsy, perfectly sound internally, and had
been chloroformed thrice before without harm.
Verdict, “Syncope.” Again, the want of an
official drug-tester!
MISGIVINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Come here, dear heart, and comfort me
Again, since, led by Fortune blind,
From want and wearing misery
You saved me, husband good and kind.
Strange thought to vex a new-made wife—
If Fate had made us strangers yet,
We might in this grim city’s strife,
(I, helpless and alone) have met !
Would you, who call me now your bride,
With tenderest words and soft caress,
Have coldly turned your eyes aside,
Unpitying of my wretchedness ?
“If so, not mine the fault! ” Ah, true,
But still, while fear in love endures,
My heart will ache, as if I knew
That possible cold glance of yours !
And love still shows me void of mirth,
Like shadows in the waters seen,
Beneath the kindest eyes on earth
The alien eyes that might have been.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
GREY-EYE: A BLEATING.
(Overheard in Rose Bay Tram.)
Grey-eye fritters love away
To hapless ones who woo her;
Grey-eye smiles at what they say,
Content that they pursue her.
Grey eyes smile—and that is all :
Yet could Love wish a sweeter pall ?
Gre.v-eye smiles when each has said
What sense forbade him tell her;
Grey-eye smiles when love is dead,
And pique and hate repel her.
Grey eyes smile—and yet grow old
While the same old tale is told !
Grey-eye, will you come and play
On shining sands with ine-ee?
Grey-eye, smile not when I say
What-1 so long to be-ee !
Grey-eye, in the moonlight stroll
Near the languid waters’ roll. . . .
Will yer? Ah— do!

A Run on Eggs. We can hatch chickens with
incubators, but before you get your chick you must get
the egg. A good hen lays an egg a day. We saw in a
late issue of yours that a poultry-owner was the lucky
possessor of a hen which laid two eggs a day. The ex-
planation, no doubt, is that this poultry-man knows
what to feed them on. He has evidently paid a visit to
our Melbourne or Sydney warehouses, bought one of our
new green hone-cutters, and gets two from one. M'LEAN
BROS. & RIGG LTD. have still a few of these egg-
producing mills for sale. Buy one before too late.*

S.A. citizens unanimously support the
disarmament proposal of the Czar. The S.A.
army is gradually dwindling to vanishing point,
and on top of this Col. Stuart, Gordon’s magnifi-
cent successor, rated the soldiers recently for
s.ovenly. work, laziness and incompetence, which,
considering the way the militia has been starved
• k a Vt '’ * s not surprising. It is a sore point
with S.A. non-coms, that they are commanded by
a crowd of society men who join the army for the
sa, kf f>f the uniform and the opportunity to flash
eold-lace at big functions, whereas the non-coms.
"no form the backbone of the forces—fre-
quently old army men with experience—are never
promoted. Moreover, the average Australian
seems to prefer cricket and football and racing' to
lumping a gun about and looking ferocious on
Saturday afternoons.
SOME MAN’S IDEAL.
GENTLEMAN wishes correspond widow lady, view
Matrimony. Stout pref. J.W., P.O., Eliz-st., S.
Suicide reported of the unhappy young
German girl who didn’t get work to do because
she was “ haughty,” and “ didn’t know her place,”
so she decided that her place was somewhere
beneath the waves at Manly, and chose it with
much deliberation. Which shows, as nine-tenths
of the women suicides do show, that every un-
married woman should have =£so per annum from
the State, also every married woman as soon as
her husband begins to drink. The verdict suicide,
without any ornamental frill to it, comes to us re-
freshingly. How can we tell where the lunacy
comes in? For all we know, the angels may be
passing contrary verdicts on the numerous wretches
who half-suicide and then repent—“ Has Magkin-
son, the over-worked George-street barber’s assist-
ant, come up yet?” “No, yer honor—remained
alive in a fit of temporary insanity.”
• * * *
“ Quiz ” : I know men of means in lodges
who neither use the lodge-doctor nor draw sick-
pay ; yet other members grumble at this, because,
as they say, if the wealthy man draws not his pay
he lowers the status of the man who is obliged to
do so.
The abortionist’s life is not always a happy
one, as this story will show. In a certain town
up North the nephew of a small trader became
enamoured of one of the assistants with a not un-
common result. In her trouble she went to her
inamorato. “The best thing for you to do,” he
advised her, “ is to go and see Dr. X.” (the doctor
having a reputation in such matters). “ But I have
no money,” she replied. Nor had he. At last he
bethought himself that his uncle was away in
Melbourne, and that <£lo might be abstracted from
the cash-box. “ Take this,” he told her, “go to
Dr. X., and say it’s all you’ve got.” At first the
doctor was adamant; his fee was .£25, but at last
her tears and entreaties prevailed, and he con-
sented to do, and did, what was required. Mean-
while the date of the uncle’s return approached ;
how was the £lO to be accounted for ? The nephew
sat down and thought hard. Then he, too, paid a
visit to the doctor. “On such and such a date,
Dr. X., you performed a certain operation on Miss
So and So, for £10.” The doctor denied. “No
use; the girl has confessed that she stole the
money from us for the purpose.” The doctor at
length hesitated. “Well, we intend to prosecute
the girl for theft. It’s an awkward business for us
all; she is bound to confess why she stole the
money. Of course we don’t want any scandal
about our shop ; if you will replace what she stole,
we will try to hush it up.” At last the doctor
agreed to refund not only the ten pounds, but to
give the girl £5 as hushmoney. The young man
went away rejoicing, with £l5 in his pocket ;
whether he gave the girl the fiver history sayeth
not
* * # *
The Melbourne municipal by-law which
forbids loitering was recently put in motion
against the street flower-sellers, but the cases were
dismissed on the ground that, so far from “loiter-
ing,” the boys were doing a business brisk enough
to seriously interfere with that of the flower-shop-
keepers, at whose instance, it is asserted, the
proceedings were taken, and were in rapid
motion all the time. A further attempt to make
the peripatetics amenable to the by-law pro-
hibiting, within certain limits, fruit and vegetable
hawking, fell through on the decision that flowers
are not “ vegetables ” within the statutory mean-
ing.

“ Constance 0.” : It’s a far cry from Dr.
Angel Money’s recent assertion (in the Lisson
case) that every somnambulist is ipso facto insane,
to the jury’s decision that a man with lunacy in
the very shape of his head—to say nothing of his
family history—may causelessly and murderously
attack his own theretofore-cherished offspring,
without being mad enough to escape judicial
slaughter. And, in any case, if Dr. Money’s dic-
tum is to be taken otherwise than “construc-
tively,” the bulk of the community has got straws
in its hair without knowing it.

Constance Clyde on female erotic poets :
A “Red Page” poet sapiently inquires of Sappho for
whom she was waiting when the moon was set, and the
Pleiades were down. The answer is obvious. Sappho was
waiting for nobody. No female writer ever does wait, in
her poems, for any man except the man that never was.
The causes which militate against a woman’s writing good
verse are several. To begin with, her greater morality
and domesticity, so that when she sets out to write a
poem about love she ends up by describing matrimony,
which hasn’t necessarily anything to do with it. She
generally thinks it has. It is not, as many people sup-
pose, that woman is deficient in eroticism. It is not a
case of can’t, but won’t. She loves not man the less, but
marriage more. Take Mrs. Browning, for instance.
Despite her chronic invalidism, she had something of the
right stuff in her, but it never came out. Her Portu-
guese love-sonnets to her husband are all right as poetry,
but, considered as love poetry, Robert might as well have
been her grandfather for all the difference it makes. The
fact is that she wasn’t married to Robert when she wrote
them (nobody ever is), and Robert was a young man and
a real man, and she didn’t like to write things about him,
really ! Now, as far as poetry in general is concerned,
one can write very good stuff from the brain alone, hut
for love-poetry one must draw upon brain and heart both.
A woman eschews the heart for copy, and consequently
has to rely the more upon the brain, and she has so much
less brain, so much less creative and imaginative power,
than man ; so that when she does make an attempt, the
result is something not quite good enough to be called a
failure. The consequence of which is that Sappho wait-
ing when the moon is set and the Pleiades are down, in-
variably waits for nobody.

inspectors. Emphasises The Bulletin’s reiterated
reminder that quantity of supervision without
quality thereof is a mere increase of the danger,
as tending to produce false confidence.

For a new sensation try descending a Ben-
digo deep mining-shaft—one of those in the neigh-
borhood of 3000 ft. But it wants no such accessory
as four miners at the Carlisle United had, the
other day, when, after being dropped 2500 ft. of
the full journey of 2900 ft., the cage crashed upon
bearers which the engine-driver thought had been
removed. In this particular case nothing very
serious happened, though all were stunned—a
much less serious result than when there was a
similar sudden stoppage in a Broken Hill shaft a
few years back.

The humors of the telephone. An artless
letter: —
Dear Bulletin. — l rang up a Macquarie-street dentist
the other day and asked him what he would charge for
putting in two front teeth. He said he must see the
mouth first —that the last time he gave a price through
the telephone he let himself in for the job of putting a
tooth in for the elephant at the Zoo for one guinea. I
have doubted since whether I was talking to the right
dentist. Is there any way of finding out who I was really
talking to ’—Anxious Enquirer.

Among Victorian Anglican clergy there is
an agitation to do away with the use of the title
“incumbent” as applied to parsons, “because it
too much resembles incumbrance.”

A Benalla (Vic.) man was fined £5, with
£3 costs, or 14 days’ gaol, the other day, for kiss-
ing a local.widow in the dark. Accused pleaded
that the widow was willing. The moral:
When the widow’s willing and inviting,
Before the pleasant chance you seize
Get the dear’s consent set out in writing,
And witnessed by a few J.P’s.
* * * *
The recently-successful action against a
’bits-driver for breach of a promise said to have
been made on the box-seat sets this scribe rumi-
nating. In these days of freedom, when a lot of
lady passengers are “roofers,” a certain element of
romance has crept into the Sydney ’busman’s cir-
cumscribed existence. The hitherto arid ’bus-top
has become an unconventional courting-ground,
and quite a number of “ unattached ” drivers have
gone into double-harness since the new stairways
lured femininity into riding outside. One vehicle
is known as the “ Honeymoon ’Bus,” owing to the
faithful and pathrtic proceeding of a newly-made
Mrs. Jehu who, on the day after the wedding,
hubby failing to get “off,” rode with him to and
from town the entire day-long ! One charioteer
(married) supplied writer with a cynical side-light
on the subject of what he called the “ mislaid-fare-
sex.” “ You see a ’bus,” he said, “ with a crowd
of wimmen on top, bet your sweet life the driver’s
a single cove. Look at mine.” (Five men and a
boy. _ Nary a petticoat.) “ Bless yer, they knows.
It’s instinct with ’em.” Later on, he hailed a
companion on a passing car, and afterwards
observed, “ Last driver of that there ’bus was a
pal of mine. Girl used to catch him regularly,
mornin’ and evenin’. He proposed to her while
they was changin’ horses out Glebe way. An’ I’m
blowed if, last week, he didn’t come into money.
They’re goin’ to Hingland soon. Some folks ’as
luck. Gee-up there ! ” The new chariot of
Hymen—the twopenny ’bus !

FUGIT HORA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O Life’s a sad and splendid dream,
A dream of hopes and fears ;
A war for goals that only seem
A smile that’s drowned in tears.
O woman’s Love is quick’ning fire !
A tragedy, a feast;
Man’s Love a rose of hot desire
That droops upon her breast.
So, as we know 7 not w 7 hen or where
We’ll lose this hurrying breath,
We’ll sine’, and take our fullest share
Of Life ; and Love ; then Death.
ALONE.
