A WOMAN’S LETTER.
, Sydney, October 24, 1898.
Mr Dear Myee—
Everyone is off to the Cup, and the usefulness
of entertaining Melbourne people strikes Sydney
harder than ever this month. For it’s one thing
to pay for your board, and go to the Cup bare, as
it were, and another to revel under the plumage of
downy Melbourne drag-owners. So careful So-
ciety persons are moved to write in October to
Dear Lady Blank ” or “ My Dearest Mrs. Dash,”
reminding them of “the pleasant time we had
when you were in Sydney in June.” Somehow, I
tancy that lots of Society people are still awaiting
their hostesses’ replies to their letters of invitation.
Yes, at Cup - time the passing Melbourne
acquaintance becomes a dearest friend. I’ve just
heard of an enterprising Sydney Mamma with five
unsettled daughters, who has written round to
every Melbourne woman she ever drank tea with,
asking airily, “ Can you put May, or Janet, or
Julia up for anight or two? They don’t in the
least mind sleeping on the floor.” Such hide (and
seek) always gets home, even if it’s somebody
else’s home, and she’s fixed every one of those
girls, and herself too, for a fortnight’s free board.
Ihe Cup agitation extends to country centres,
and country drapers are hard pushed by those
farmers’ daughters who don’t order from city
houses. One simple haberdasher was much em-
barrassed by a country belle’s enquiries. He had
just returned from the city and was explaining “In
skirts they are wearing black moire silk. In sailor
hats they are wearing wings in front, and in
blouses they wear checked zephyrs with pearl but-
tons down the front. ” Buxom belle : ‘ ‘ Are you
quite sure ? ” Draper : “Oh yes, I took particular
notice in h’order to be versed in heverything.”
Buxom belle: ‘ ‘ And what are they wearing in
stockings ? ” Draper, very perplexed, endeavours
to explain that he really couldn’t say for certain
on that point. He guessed they were black. But
they might have been white. Belle very indignant ;
thinks he ought to know for certain.
Croquet still seriously threatens the summer
season. The mallets clacked gaily at the Observa-
tory garden-party lately, and students and
librarians and other cultured people were able to
knock their heads against each other without any
of the noise peculiar to empty vessels.
There is nothing else half so popular as the electric
tram at Rose Bay. It is about all the conductor
can do to wrestle with the enormous number of
passengers who crowd the tram, especially in the
evening, and there is no thought of keeping to the
20 minutes time-table. At first, the time spent on
the entire journey was 3| minutes ; but since a con-
ductor was thrown out when shooting round a
curve the speed has been reduced to 7min. Rose
Bay householders are none too pleased at the in-
novation, as, by way of set-off to the convenience
of being able to ride so easily to town, they have
the objectionable determination of the “many-
headed multitude ” to picnic in their private
grounds.
The marriage of Miss Ethel De Lissa, a popular
girl grad., to Mr. Arthur Bensusan, was a pretty
thing in Jewish weddings last week. A large
party of smartly-gowned people, peppered with
black-capped students, scattered themselves about
the grounds and watched the binding of the pic-
turesque Hebraic knot under animpromptu canopy.
Miss Haidee De Lissa in lace frock and chiffon
sash played chief bridesmaid, and various other
lace-clad maidens bearing flowers made an admir-
mirable picture against a background of trees.
The rest of the afternoon resolved itself into a
garden fete, accentuated with fizz and strawberries
and speeches, and the unparalleled, if melancholy
happiness, of gazing at a wonderful display of
bridal gifts. The going-away gown was an Eng-
lish creation of piped tan voile worn with a mauve
hat, and a pretty guest frock was Mrs. Aronson’s
white delaine, the chiffon bodice appliqued in black.
A sheaf of purple irises made a noticeably happy
bouquet for this smart frock.
That decorative little theatre, the Palace, looks
on with surprise every night at its big audiences.
Ever since its birth this theatre was led to believe
it wasn’t going to hum, for the sound of dropped
coin has been chiefly a figure of speech in its
treasury. Yankee Dante long-nosed, flaxen-
haired, with a shrewd, boyish face, rouged cheeks,
and the sort of sudden smile that takes—has
changed all that, and everybody is crowding up to
see the lady burnt alive. One great attraction for
the multitude is the atmosphere of “ wegotism ”
you and me and all the rest of us—that Prof.
Dante creates as he wizardises. Anybody can
have a share in the play. The fat woman eating
oranges is asked for a loan of her handkerchief.
The little boy from the fifth class is allowed to
make four figures on Dante’s slate. The drowsy
individual with the rosy beak is invited to name
his drink from the magic-bottle. And anybody at
all can lend the bank-note whose number is magi-
cally read by a blindfold lady. So the audience
waxes weekly, and everybody raves.
The worst of it is that you don’t want any ordi-
nary happenings with a lot of positive wonders,
and a cold draught attacks the spinal column
when your trickster fails you. The other night he
was doing the bottle trick, giving anyone his
chosen drink from the same bottle. Some took
whisky, some claret. A clergyman with a true
clerical palate asked for champagne (he wasn’t a
bogus clergyman, but I won’t give him away to his
flock). Dante tilted his bottle, but nothing came.
The audience leaned forward. “But I can assure
you this has never happened before, and to-
morrow night ” So the clergyman went to
the other end of the drink-list, and asked for
milk. Dante shook his bootle, thumped it, and
finally had to own up that he couldn’t make that
drink either. The audience was a bifi uneasy.
Then he broke up the bottle and pulled out a white
rat and explained that that was the cause of the
clog, and the audience went home satisfied. All
except Dante, perhaps, and perhaps the dis-
appointed fizzler. Perhaps the moral is—never
let the cloth name its own drink. Or perhaps—
but, anyhow, Dante is worth wondering over.
Unlucky little Pattie Brown, with her bronchitis
and her sprained ankle, and that other unfortunate
invalid, Lesly Thomson, made their bow to a big
house on Saturday night. “The Dovecote,” from
the French, is just the thing for a flighty audi-
ence on the verge of the Cup. Pattie left all her
ailments behind her and sparkled away in various
frocks, the most alluring of which was a thickly-
jetted, shiney-sheeny black, with net sleeves em-
broided with turquoise stones and a corsage frill
to match. The little green frock for the first act
was too suggestive of a melon for my fancy, the
curving lines of black lace lending breadth to the
idea as well as to Pattie. Noni Rickards makes
wonderful sartorial advances from the biting blue
raiment which she used to top with a scarlet hat
m one of her first r6les. The improvement goes
deeper than dress, too. She moves well, and
knows how to sit down, and makes quite a pretty
little bit of stage furniture—she has nothing to do
except dress.
Lesly Thomson surprised everyone by looking
about seventeen. His long illness has probably
worn him down, and he now appears as a thin,
little fellow with no legs to speak of, a pleasant
face, reminiscent of Frank Thornton, and a ten-
dency to droop and stoop like a wilted flower.
According to real life, he is just the sort of
small, peaceable, pale-faced man whom a bouncing
wife wears her life out in suspecting. But, accord-
ing. to stage traditions, he lacks the physique of an
artist husband with an alleged eye for models.
But, in the face of Pattie’s red-gold hair, isn’t it
a little unsatisfactory that those two naughty
hairs on Lucas’ coat should have been golden ?
You see, she finds them on her husband’s coat, and
plays Vesuvius in consequence. As the piece all
hinges on the fact of the hairs being golden, the in-
evitable inference is that Pattie’s hair is red. And
even then there’s a hitch. A carrotty wife who
found a golden hair on her hubby’s shoulder would
be obliged, for the sake of her vanity, to think it
was her own.
The second Newbury Pop went off with a loud
report that hasn’t stopped echoing through this
city yet. A harmless little pianiste, Miss Lily
Juncker, essayed to please the audience with some
40 pages or so of Grieg with variations. Un-
fortunately, the audience wasn’t taking 40 pages
that night, and lashed itself into the wildest
demonstration of musical taste ever witnessed in
our Town Hall. Miss Juncker tinkled away
bravely through it all, pausing where teacher had
said to pause, and steadily maintaining the music-
lesson attitude. But the yells multiplied, until
the. front rows couldn’t hear their shocked
whispers. Wildly musical people, who know a
Jew’s harp when they see it from the Town Hall
organ, shrieked out to the girlish offender, “Go
home ! Stop your row ! Go to bed ! ” and looked
round for the shade of Beethoven to pat them on
the back. Other courageous spirits made feline
noises, and groaned and hissed, but they got their
Grieg allee same, every note of it, and Gouli
rejoices in their punishment. After this Newbury
ran his performers on with a scared promptitude,
not pausing for the interlude and a possible uproar.
Next Saturday will probably see a monster hall at
the third Pop, as a rumor has got about that
strange things are always going to happen at these
concerts. Last Saturday’s other strange thing
was the man-soprano, who sang, lady’s songs till
the audience choked.
Another evening entertainment of the week
was Miss Warburton-Matters’ dramatic recital.
Miss Matters is one of Adelaide’s “amateur
angels,” and her golden hair and bewitching
cheeks went a long way towards the success of
the recital. Flute solos by the only Lemmone
did the rest of the business. The recitations were
the ordinary things, the same dying London arab,
the same kind lady with flowers, a race poiyim, a
drawing-room sketch, all rendered with a bright,
but superficial, manner. The young reciter de-
pended rather too much on her smile, but still it
was nice to look at anything so dimpled and youth-
ful. Many good people think that the only
woman reciter who has lifted her voice success-
fully in these parts for a length of years was that
dark lady with dewy eyes who used to roll out
sad and funny things in a rich voice until you
were almost persuaded to be a blackie.
The, reigning attraction in the social world of
Brisbane just now is Walter Bentley, of Shakes-
perian fame. Society butterflies are fluttering in
crowds to his University Extension lectures on
oratory.. Bentley has also organised a dramatic
club, which is to perform Hamlet this month and
a comedy afterwards.
The .Griffiths sisters, thanks (say some persons)
to the influence of Lady Carington, Lady Jersey
and others (also, of course, thanks to their own
charm and talent) are doing exceedingly well,
having made £l3O by one afternoon’s recital. They
never wish to return to the land of their birth ex-
cept to see some warmth and sunshine once more.
Miss Beatrice Griffiths—who, a late cable says, has
succeeded greatly in the Patti provincial tour—got
so frozen driving once to a concert, that she could
hardly play when she got there.
Fashions will soon be settling themselves for the
summer, and, on the whole, there are no very
startling changes. The apron-skirt isn’t going to
rule over anything but ball dresses, because it’s
such an easy matter that. popularity will ruin it
with modish people. Hair-dressing wavers a bit
just now, and sort of totters on its throne.
The very few make knobs and pull them down
on their necks for evening wear, and push
them up a trifle higher for daylight, but the
rest of the world keeps its hair on the top of its
head religiously. Crimping-irons have their work
decreased by half. An odd wave here and there
follows the mad sea of billows that used to roll
over every dressed head. Next month the knob
on the neck will be indispensable. Race frocks
are going in heavily for the polonaise and frilly
underskirts, and white lace, over white, still flut-
ters from the top of the ladder of smartness.
Turned-up hats tremble a little. They may stay
in, but there’s just a chance that they will succumb
to the dressy little shop-girl, who chops up her
best sailor and stitches the brim on to the crown,
and heaps a yard or two of velvet over the scissors-
cut, and puts a bird in front of the velvet, and a
bow and a branch in front of the bird.
Poor little shop-girls—little bundles of headache
and anaemia, and hot, black boots, and mysterious
complaints ! Talk of cookery and suffrage, and
fresh air, and education of the masses—-in my
opinion all these burning questions fade and die
in the light of that sickening, barbarous, brutal
shop-rule that won’t let its women sit down when
they want to. Two years off the end of this
highly-varnished nineteenth century a shop-girl
has to kneel under the counter to give her limbs
rest, while a friend takes her place on the under-
standing that she does the same for the friend
later on. The Factories and Shops Act says that
proprietors will be prosecuted who prevent their
assistants from sitting down, or don’t provide
seats ; but there are wheels within wheels, and the
girls know how adroitly the “ sack ” can be given.
And what is the proportion of seats to girls, and
what is the meaning of the word seat ? One shop-
girl told me that she would rather stand and be
tired than take mock rest on an inch of
wood built up so close to the counter that
the backache exceeded the ache of feet and legs.
And there’s a final coat of gloss to all
this exquisite cruelty. When no one is waiting
to be served, and all the boxes are back in their
places, and there’s nothing in the world to prevent
all the girls at one counter snatching a little rest,
the management steps in with a humorous rule,
‘ ‘ Assistants must always appear to be doing some-
thing.” They must fumble about with a box of
ribbons or a basket of remnants, and open drawers
and shut them, and touch this article and that
article, and play to be just straightening up after
a huge rush of customers. This is supposed to be
good for trade. It is also supposed to delude the
public. Will the public kindly take notice that it
is being deluded, and stand up for its rights like a
woman ?
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ After repeated promises, winding up with that
confirmatory telegram from Sydney, Lady Brassey
did not manage to turn up at the Austin
Mansion, at Toorak, on Saturday afternoon (Oct.
22nd), to open the Time and Talents fete. Cer-
tainly the way-worn pilgrim would barely have
had time to change her frock prior to keeping the
appointment, and, as it was, her substitutes, His
Ex. and Aide Nevill, were half an hour late, de-
laying business in consequence. The Ancient
Mariner, looking pale and shaky, apolo-
gised quite affably for the absentee, while
‘ Lord Richard ’ was all smiles and compla-
cency. In the absence of Lady 8., Janet
Lady Clarke came in for the presentation
bouquet, took the vice-regal visitors under her wing
to afternoon tea, and later on helped them to invest
their small spendings to the best advantage.
Daughter Mary was in grey with plenty of white
chiffon, and plumed hat to match. Most of the
other girls wore muslin, white for preference, with
several flounces on their skirts and yards of lace
ribbon and chiffon all over. Not a creature but
affected a low-down coiffure, the figure ‘B’ as
popular a hair-design as any. With their white
pique or serge kilts, tiny boys of Toorakia wore
white Claude Duval coats and three-cornered
washing-hats.
“A pink-silk jupon was hawked all over the
grounds at the fete in search of a buyer. It was
Lady Rupert Clarke and Mrs. Geo. Chirnside, who
set the example of trading blouses and other high-
priced plumage at their Bon Marche stall at the
recent ‘ Ministering Angels ’ fair. One of their
customers, the Duke of Manchester, forked out
six guineas for a dressy bodice.
“An incongruous figure at the frilly ‘ Time and
Talents ’ fair was a lady-cyclist in a hotch-potch
garment of black and grey patches, sewn together
at sixes and sevens. Wheelwomen are apt to be
dress faddists. Some are hygienic cranks; then
there’s that lean brunette, who provides a spot of
color in traffic crushes on city crossings by her
flat-chested orange silk blouse.
. “A blood-curdling account of the rush to get
into the members’ tea-room at Caulfield (on Cup
Day) comes to hand from a crumpled victim. The
Cup catastrophe had such an awful effect upon
their nerves that the female holders of tea-tickets
made for the room at full gallop, but when they
§ot to the door they were asked to produce mem-
ers’ tickets as well, and some of them hadn’t
brought the necessary ‘brief,’ and others couldn’t
get to their pockets on account of the shoving, so
they began to faint with one accord, whereupon a
cruel stout gentleman snorted at them, and what
with tears, groans and supplications, it was a
‘ horrible scandal ’—more horrible than the fatal
accident on the course.
“ Self-approval comes very easy to the conscience
of a bishop’s wife. Lately, when Melb. Women’s
Hospital committee was debating as to which nice
young man it should select, from four applicants
for the billet of assistant resident surgeon, it fell
to Mrs. Goe’s lot to give a casting vote. The
estimable matron seized this opportunity to feel
very proud of herself. Of two candidates who bad
polled an equal number of admiration votes, one
young man, said Mrs. G., was a personal friend of
her own, nevertheless she couldn’t ignore the
other fellow’s ‘ better recommendations, ’ so she
voted for him. Whereupon, all the other com-
mittee ladies murmured ‘ What a noble sense of
duty! ’ Seeing that Mrs. Goe couldn’t give an
honest casting vote without commenting on her
sacrifice of personal prejudices, I would like to
know which young man she voted for in the first
instance.
“The tall, athletic-looking young man who is
accompanying Mr. and Mrs. Sidney Webb in their
tour through Australia is Mr. Charles Trevelyan,
eldest son of Sir George Trevelyan and therefore a
grand-nephew of Macaulay. The range and
quality of his conversation seem to indicate that
he inherits the literary instincts of his family.
His grandfather, Sir Charles Trevelyan, wrote a
‘Life of Fox’ and other works, and his father
is the author of the ‘ Life of Macaulay ’ and of a
fine little book on the Indian Mutiny. Mr.
Trevelyan, like his father, is going in for a
political career on the Radical side, and he evi-
dently has a good grip of big questions.
“David Syme gave a big dinner party on Wednes-
day night, in honor of Mr. and Mrs. Sidney
Webb, of whose economic works the old autocrat
has a iarge admiration. But it was somewhat of
a joke for Henry Wrixon on the previous
evening to have given a dinner in honor of these
vigorous Socialists in the sacred precincts of Yic.
Leg. Council Chamber. No wonder the Council
agreed to the second reading of the ‘ one-man-one-
vote ’ Bill on the same evening without a division !
Is society safe when a pair of dreadful Socialists
can be allowed to dine in the very halls of Fat-
manocracy ? But then, it was from Sidney Webb
that Wrixon got a vast amount of the information
for his dreary book on Socialism. The forty-eight
Rich Landlords cast side glances at Webb and his
wife as they sat in the gallery before the dinner
hour—Mrs. Webb in a black satin skirt and a blue
silk bodice; and, no doubt, marvelled how on
earth a woman who has written Socialist books
could look so charming and refined. Fatman
clearly gave up the Webbs as ‘inexplicable
enigmas ’ —to use the phrase of one of their number
on a recent occasion.
“Irene Fletcher, the clever young.Yic. pianist
just back from Germany, wore a floppy, white-silk
frock, & la Elsie Hall and AdelaYerne, at her first
recital (Oct. 17). Otherwise, tall, willowy Miss
Fletcher, though slighty round-shouldered and
with hair overprone to tumble into her eyes, is
more graceful of platform deportment than either
of the damsels aforesaid, less timorous than Elsie,
not so slap-bang as t’other. Once on a piano-stool,
however, positions are reversed. The new girl,
crouched over the keyboard with pencil wrists and
big, capable hands hard at work, is more impres-
sive than ornamental. Not that her performance
either quite equals that of her two predecessors.
At the same time, Madame Clara Schumann,
Miss Fletcher’s teacher, vouched for her pupil’s
‘ soul, ’ which valuable asset, taken in conjunction
with cultivated memory and execution, should
help the owner in time to repay the self-sacrificing
efforts of her devoted mother and sister.
“At the Bijou, with Yic. operatic amateurs,
Miss Millie Tree Chapman was a bright and tune-
ful, if not extra-beautiful, Bohemian Girl. Her
gipsy costume was more becoming on the whole
than the pink satin and plumes of her Court
toilette; but Thaddeus Neilson looked such a
Simple Simon in his stage-finery that Arline was
quite dressy, enough to shine by comparison. The
Polish hero in question wore one of those babyish,
black knicker-suits trimmed with blue ribbons,
and an ill-advised bandana handkerchief tied
round his naturally over-substantial waist. In
the scene where Lord Thaddeus and friend Devils-
hoof come to coax Arline away from Pa’s palace
back to the greenwood-tree, the latter falls asleep
on a sofa, and during the billing and cooing lies
placidly scratching himself in his dreams. If the
young lady had noticed his proceedings, every
scratch should have been a warning to stay where
she was and let picturesque squalor go hang.

by her weepful garb, the alleged wooer un-
doubtedly seeks the mourner’s society, though,
with a grown-up daughter to confuse the trail,
onlookers are apt to go off on a wrong tack.

before; and from M.L. it is cabled that Louis

pockets.

Victor Daley writes : “I have been
glancing through an advance copy of Ogilvie’s
volume, ‘ Fair Girls and Gray Horses.’ In my
opinion it will be the most charming book of Aus-
tralian verse that has yet appeared. For Ogilvie
can not only write ringing rhymes that make the
blood spin through the veins ; he can also sing
dainty and delicate songs which remind me at
times of the cavalier songs written between a
battle and a banquet. There is much of the
cavalier and, as becomes his name, not a little of
the moss-trooper in Ogilvie. He writes with a
galloping heart. But, above all, there is the
passionate bloom of youth in his verses. He looks
out upon the world around him and finds it fair
and young, and makes it so for his readers by the
magic of his rhyme. Sadness is not his metier.
If I were asked to name the source of his literary
inspiration I would say—the old Scotch ballads.
He is essentially lyrical in his manner. His songs
need no musical setting. They provide their own
music.”

Mat. Davies is said, to be in treaty for a
mansion equal to his historic Bracknell.
Daniel Cooper preceded Saul Samuel as
Acting N.S.W. Agent-General, and, after 18
years, succeeds him in the same capacity.
The magnificent Majah Neild is evidently
booked as representative of N.S.W. at Paris Ex-
hibition. Premier Reid won’t deny it, and that
may explain several things.
“ Captain ” Hancock, who has managed
S.A. Moonta mines for 34 years, lately resigned.
During his term the co. has paid 5| millions in
wages, and total profits were 1| millions. Hancock
has outlived all the original directors, all overseers
and foremen, but one, and nearly all the men
originally employed.

The estimate that one-third of the mhabi-
tants of Norfolk Island have Nobbs as some
portion of the i r names is only a slight exaggera-
tion. Dr. Nobbs, a physician and clergyman,
volunteered to serve the islanders in the early days,
and devoted himself so strenuously to their wei-
fare, spiritual and temporal, that the people no
only gave his name to their children, but in some
cases to favorite animals and birds.
Brisbane has its Norton-Meagher vendetta.
Bobby Byrne, of Figaro, and Walter Bentley, of
Saturday Night, are libelling each other with a
ferocity which would turn Potts and Slurk green
with envy.
Australia has three foreign-born Premiers
—Dickson, Reid, and Braddon —and three Aus-
tralian-born—Kingston, Forrest, and Turner.
Dickson, the youngest Premier, but the oldest
man of the six, save one,, was born in Plymouth
G 6 years ago.

ness, was called to N.S.W. Bar in IFSO, three
years before the Chief-Justice and eight years
before Justices Owen and G. B. Simpson, his
nearest colleagues.
James Dickson, Queensland’s new Premier,
is a holy, bald, dull auctioneer, and also collects
rents. James Munro, ex-Premier of Victoria,
was a holy, dull, rather bald man who collected
rents. Come to think of it, what a lot of dull,
holy men collect rents !

from the D.T. within the past three years have
gone back to that journal.

“out.”
It is scarcely to the credit of Maoriland
that no adequate memoir of the gallant Maori-
fighter, M „or Von Tempsky, has ever been pub-
lished. His contemporaries are fast passing away,
and soon his name will be forgotten. Some time
ago it was reported that his eldest son was work-

Died in Adelaide, a few days back, Mrs.
Hill, grandmother of one of the most aggressive
of the Adelaide Ho pital honorary staff under the
Kingston-Tucker rule. Right down to this year
the old lady used to chat interestingly over her
personal recollections of such way-back incidents
as the crowning of the fourth George.
The Irish-born William Bowen, one of
the first Victorians who crossed the Murray to
open up the N.W. country of N.S.W., passed
away near Dubbo a week or so ago. He was a
successful pioneer, and his Bowen Park estate is a
model of its kind. He died at 71, but till his
last days the acquisition of land was his ruling
passion.
When young Loch was at Melb. Grammar
School the ordinary common schoolboys were
snobbishly instructed (in addition to the ukase
against swearing) to call the Governor’s son
“Master Douglas ” when they spoke to him. To
their credit it is that the boys did nothing of the
sort, and even applied to him the term “ candles ”
—suggested by Ins locks. . '
The late William Collard Smith,
Vic. politician, had historic feet, and once
when he sent a pair of boots back to the
maker as being too small, the artist com-
plained that they would be on his hands.
“ There ain’t another man in Australia
could make any use of them boots. Might
do for show-boots in the winder; but even
then I’d have to explain who I made ’em
for, and that they was too small for you,
Colonel. It’d be a good advertisement.”
“ That’ll do ! ” shouted Smith. “ There’s
the money, and let me see you cut up
those boots before I leave the shop.”
Hori Grey’s opponent in his prin-
cipal election contests in Auckland was
softgoods-man James M'Cosh Clark, a
man possessing far more than the aver-
age calico-Jimmy intelligence. Clark,
who had been three times Mayor of the
city (elected by the burgesses), had set
his heart on ousting Grey from the
Auckland City East seat, but was de-
feated again and again. On one occas-
sion, when it was not convenient for Grey
to contest the seat he expressed his “you-
he-danmedness ” by nominating against
Clark an utterly-unknown man, a gold-
fields pumping - engine driver named
Speight. Speight threw himself into the
contest attired in a rusty frock-coat and
patched pants, and though assailed by
all the swagger influence and sneered at
by the press “ as dirty water from the
Thames,” he handsomely licked his op-
ponent. Clark died recently, his ambi-
tion to reach Parliament ungratified.

are Augustus Loftus, formerly of N.S. Wales, and

plication.

A Maoriland caricaturist once represented
Hori Grey as wearing a shocking bad hat. As a
matter of fact, the “old man eloquent ” invari-
ably wore a chapeau of uncompromisingly antique
shape, but it was ever faultless as to nap. Judge
Fenton, an inveterate punster, pointed the picture
out to Grey, and suggested that it was a compli-
ment to his “ fur-tile imagination.”

nibals ” no connection whatever with England or
things British, beyond a few days’ stay at Singa-
pore—was so suddenly seized with devotion to
Queen Vic. as to paint her colossal picture on a
rock, and expatiate to the wondering savages upon
her power and glory.

requiem mass for the Empress of Austria, at St.

The latest move of Premier Reid in getting
himself appointed a Q.C., has not raised his
popularity amongst the members of either branch
of the legal profession. Indeed, some barristers
profess to see in Mr. Reid’s action a thinly-dis-
guised attempt to bring the hitherto honorable
office of Q.C. into disrepute. For the N.S.W.
Premier is certainly not regarded as an eminent
lawyer.

In the Vic. Libel Bill discussion Trenwith,
with evident relish, revived the following story of
his unpopular days—
He remembered an incident that occurred to himself
when he was standing for Villiers and Heytesbury some
time ago. A certain newspaper asked in its leading
columns —“ Who is Mr. Trenwith? Is this Trenwith the
drunkard? Is this Trenwith the wife-beater? Is this
Trenwith the pot-house brawler ? ” And having assumed
by innuendo that Trenwith was a drunkard, a wife-
beater, and a pot-house brawler, it proceeded to comment
upon it, and to ask whether that was the sort of man who
should be sent to represent the people in Parliament.
The paper alluded to was the now defunct Melb.
Daily Telegraph, the then custodian of what
passed for the Nonconformist conscience of Vic-
toria.
The death the other day, at Chatswood
(N.S.W.), of the widow of John Bowie Wilson
revives memories of the turbulent N.S.W. Lands
Minister of 35 years ago. Bowie Wilson was
Minister for Lands in three N.S.W. Administra-
tions, commencing Ministerial life with James
Martin, and quitting it when that forceful
personage got out of Parkes’ road and took
the first seat on the Bench. Wilson was
a variously, and deeply - read man, with a
creditable record from a couple of Scotch
Universities, but was singularly impression-
able by spiritual, and particularly spiritualistic,
influences. His father was a parson, and no doubt
he drew from him his passion for discussing affairs
of the other world, said discussions generally
leaving in doubt what opinions he really enter-
tained. He was equally vigorous on questions of
social reform, but with much more practical and
lasting results.

The late Hori Grey’s invariable courtesy
to the poor was one great secret of the love which
everywhere enveloped the person of the great
Pro-consul. Once, in Wellington, his house was
invaded by a humble, weatherbeaten widow from
the bush who desired to know if Grey could find a
situation for her daughter. The old man was out
of the Ministry then, with no hope of a return to
power, and the lady wasn’t even a constituent—
besides, women had no votes in those days. Yet
he received her personally and heard her tale out,
and offered her refreshmen 1 s ; and then he paid
for a cab to take her to her lodgings, and assisted
her into it, standing bareheaded on the pave-
ment, as the average politician would do only to
the Governor’s wife, or the wife of his most influ-
ential publican-constituent. And after that he
hunted up the desired billet for the weather-
beaten dame’s daughter. Grey never snubbed
the person who was down, though to the person
who was up he could be even more unpleasant
at times than the average man.
Hori Grey’s departure from Maoriland
about five years ago was most undemonstrative.
The Gothic was lying in Wellington harbor on
the occasion of her first visit, and a few days
before her departure for London Grey tottered on
board leaning on the arm of a friend, to whom he
expressed his thankfulness, as they left, that he
had lived to see so fine a vessel placed in the local
trade. Two days later it was whispered that he
intended going to England in her, but this was
denied ; and as no one could exactly sound the
deep old man, no steps were taken to give him a
send-off. On the day the steamer lay in the
offing preparatory to picking up the mails and last
passengers by the two o’clock tender, a shipping
reporter on the same staff as the writer strolled
with him on to the wharf, and there, all by him-
self, walked the “great pro-consul.” Taking his
seat on the further side of the bridge he remained
hidden from the eyes of the few people down to
see the small boat leave with the mails, and the
last view of him was as he leant on his stick
apparently buried in thought and oblivious to all
around him. There were no cheers, no speeches,
nothing to indicate that the greatest man Maori-
land has known was leaving its shores for ever.
The Rougemont business revives memories
of Capt. Jackson Barry, the Maoriland J.
Verne, who 20 years ago published a book of
adventures, of which the making of sea voyages on
whale-back was not the most difficult of digestion.
A little gnome-like personage and as truculent as a
Comanche. In his best days woe betide the wight
who betrayed any hesitancy in accepting his most
airy flight of fancy, or, indeed, who crossed him in
any way. On one occasion, when he was Mayor
of Cromwell, in Otago district, he visited Dunedin
and overstayed his leave. On his return he found
a vote of censure against him on the minutes of
the council. “ Who moved this vote ?” he asked,
with an angry snort. “ I did,” said a councillor.
“ Well, take that ? ” roared His Worship, smiting
the mover a “ sockdolloger ” on the chin and send-
ing him to grass. “ Who seconded it ? ” was his
Worship’s next query, but no one responded—too
much respect was now felt for the captain’s handy
left. “Well, then,” said Barry, “as no one
seems to have seconded this motion of censure, it
is informal and I now expunge it from the minutes.
Next business, please?” By the way, it appears
that Captain Jackson Barry finds a difficulty in
grasping some of the Rougemont items. He says
“ A man can ride a whale—l did. But d d if
you can make me believe a man can ride a turtle.”

Mr. T. Streater writes : “ I have received so
much benefit from Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa I feel
that I must thank you for it. I am a baker by
trade, which, as you know, is very hard work, but

night I go home and have a cup of your Cocoa,
and feel quite fresh again, and then I go and
enjoy myself. But before I took to Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, I used to mess about indoors as tired as
could be.”
YkCocoa will become a household word amongst
us. Prepared from substances of admitted dietetic
value, Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa is at once digestive
and refreshing, nutritious and invigorating. This
is the secret of its phenomenal success. It seems,
so far as we can gather, to agree with everybody,
and everybody is agreed in giving it a good name.
Apparently it is the accepted beverage of a people
who have recognised and appreciated its remark-
able health-giving properties. And certainly in
this age of rush and worry, wear and tear, when
the energies of mind and body are severely taxed,
it is an ideal food. 1 .
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.

Aboriglnalities.
A strange case of disappearance occurred
on the Tasmania N. W. coast a few years ago.
A child of about three years, after being dressed
by his mother, ran out into the enclosed yard at
the back of the house. Two or three minutes
afterwards the boy was missed, and, although the
house and grounds were turned upside-down, he
was as completely gone as though he had been
borne upwards to the clouds. The whole country
was systematically searched by scores of bushmen
and the police, but in vain—the child had “gone.”
The sequel is just as strange. Long afterwards
some men were clearing a paddock a few chains
from the house, and beneath a quantity of bark
and rubbish, which had fallen from a large, ringed
tree into the hollow at its roots, they found the
skeleton of the little wanderer. In this paddock
the search had been hottest ; and it was, more-
over, proved that it was impossible for the child
to have gone beyond the yard in the time. There
is only one solution of the mystery. Some high-
strung females declared that the child had been
carried off by an angel. Perhaps, but the angel
was an—eagle.
* * * *
Note that when the dead body of the
thirst-killed prospector is found in Westralia—and
such corpses have been turning up pretty regularly
once a month at least during the winter —it is
almost always stated that the victim died full-
dressed and with his boots on. The latest cases are
those of Anderson and Power, found in Roe-
burne district. Ninety-five per cent, of the writers
on bush perils have hitherto pictured a maniac
tearing his clothes off when the end came near;
and far-Western N.S.W. and Northern S.A. ex-
perience seemed to pretty fully warrant it. Whence
the difference ?

“ Inverell ” : The old-maid species is nob
so responsible for cat-prevalencj’ as the supersti-
tion which allots the cat-slayer a long term of ill-
luck. In this district, scarcely anybody would
kill a cat right-off, though they don’t mind so
injuring the creature that it dies a slow, torturous
THE ACME OF VANITY.
HE : “Do you really think Cholly is so very vain? ”
SHE: “Vain! Why, I saw him using the undertaker’s window for a mirror as he passed it.”
death, or placing batches of kittens in hollow
stunips to starve and sizzle under a hot sun. A
common practice is to keep the cats till about half-
grown, then bag ’em up and loose them miles
away, regardless of the fact that the tame cat gone
wild is even a greater enemy to fowl-roosts than
the “ native cat.” Sometimes these fate-dodgers
return from such expeditions to discover a small
regiment of strange cats in possession of their own
hearthstones, someone having been along in the
interim and done unto them as they were doing
unto others. Have heard many —women, of course
—defend the stump atrocity—“ It’s so cruel to
drown the poor little things ; I couldn’t do it! ”

“Tom A.”: Be women-workers. Once
knew an old couple on a selection in one of the
most God-forsaken places I ever saw. They lived
principally on goat’s milk and flesh ; and the day
I fell across them they were ploughing in the
“ cultivation ” paddock, on the side of a barren,
sandy ridge. The woman, habited in one single,
loose garment reaching from neck to midleg, and
with her unkempt gray hair straggling from under
one of her old man’s cast-off tiles, was leading the
miserable moke in the furrow, while the old man,
nearly blind, was steering. They were very hope-
ful, and anticipated grand results, but are. now (if
alive) in one of Sydney’s institutions, and the
selection, I suppose, is “ Crown Lands ” again..

“ W.K.A.” : Be “ Steele Rudd’s ” remarks
(10/9/’9B) that there is a species of kangaroo found
near Fingal (Tas.) which is as destitute of a tail
as man himself. This ’roo, nathless, is sure-
footed, swift, and A 1 over the sticks ; and I am
prepared to lay heavy odds against “5.R.,” or
any other sprinter, running him down in dis-
tances from 50yds. to five miles in open country.
Should any Bulletin reader be sceptical, I refer
him to the caretaker of Launceston (Tas.)
Museum, where may be seen a specimen.

Dear Bulletin. —Be bottle-tree (8.,
24/9/’9B). It is true that the pithy interior of the
bottle-tree is edible and will fatten stock, but that
it contains water is a mere fabrication. Some
bushman has been “ ringing a pitch ” into
“ E.S.S.” On this station we had recently to cut
down a number of these trees, some of large size,
but not one yielded a drop of water. The old
hands in this district ridicule the idea of such a
thing.—D.L.H. [There are several kinds of
bottle-trees (Sterculia). The Queensland variety
often contains water, or mucilaginous juice, and
ots of it.]
♦ * * *
“ Borak ” : In answer to “E.S.S.” The
bottle-tree is very serviceable in dry seasons.
Have seen wool scoured by water from one ; but
it will scour sheep best. Also, is not generally
known that the last great Brisbane flood was
caused chiefly by the bursting of several large
bottle-trees. Cannot give the diameter of the
trees, but was out in a dingy in one once duck-
shooting.
* * * *
Quite common on the W.A. gold-fields for
a diggers’ camp to have one or two gins living
in it, and, lately, a N. W. pastoralist brought down
to town with him three lubras, taking apart-
ments for them and himself with the utmost sang
froid. The beneficent Forrest Govt, has cut
down the diurnal support allowance for aged and
infirm niggers to threepence—just enough to buy
half-a-pound of flour in the wayback country.

“Tomahawk”: Be hamstrung niggers.
On a Northern river is a half-caste, who was ham-
strung in infancy because he wasn’t born black
enough for the “ old man’s ” fancy. Moves along
on hands with legs doubled under him. Can ride
(hauling himself up horse’s fore-leg into the sad-
dle with assistance of stirrup-leather). Can chip
corn and cane, and climb trees with anybody.

“R.G.L.”: A N.S.W. farmer who has
been long ploughing with same pair of bullocks,
has been in habit of unyoking them at lower end
of paddock when the missus cooees for dinner.
One windy day he did not hear the cooee ; but the
bullocks did, and stopped at the usual place. He
has since often tried, but never succeeded in getting
another round out of them after the cooee has
been heard. Also I know a cow that won’t give
down her milk if her owner’s horse is fed first.
Jealousy ?

Thus A. Meston, S.Q. Aborigines-Pro-
tector, re supply of blacks’ blankets :
Your correspondent (15/10/’9B) forgets that Queensland
is a territory of magnificent distances. The 150 Boulia
blankets, which arrived Aug. 20, were despatched 21st
Jan. The blankets for Birdsville were at one time sent
ma North Australia, and were once eighteen months on
journey. The Boulia blankets for next winter were sent
away 9th Sept, (ult.), in time to arrive at Boulia for April
or May, 1899. In the coming days of aerial navigation,
the Govt, storekeeper will doubtless be able to sail over
the west and drop bales of blankets into all the myall
camps on Ist May, but till then he must be more-or-less
regulated by floods and droughts, and so forth, to say
nothing of erratio tea msters and wayward pack-horses.
* • * *
“ Rougemont, Junior ” : One day a
picaninny, a few months old, was playing on the
grass near our camp when suddenly a native bear
was seen to rush out of the woods and seize the
child. The shrieks of the mother alarmed the
camp, and a rescue was attempted, but the bear
placed the child in her pouch, and made off at
such speed that pursuit was hopeless, and we gave
up the picaninny as lost. About a month later,
however, a chief of another tribe killed a native
bear in the woods after a desperate struggle, and
on approaching the huge carcase was astounded
to find a fat black baby in the pouch. The news
eventually reached our tribe, and the child was
restored to its mother none the worse for the
attentions of its strange foster parent. The
above is accredited by nine scientific societies of
Great Britain and two real live dukes.
* * * *
“S.S.”: Have been told of a peculiar
mare-and-cow friendship. The two animals were
the property of a Gippsland settler, and were
reared together, paddocked together, and in winter
stabled in the same shed. They made no show of
friendship that struck the settler, but when the
cow was sold to a farmer about five miles off, the
mare became strangely uneasy, and wandered
about the paddock and the house for a day and a
night whimpering queerly. On the following
morning she had jumped the fence and dis-
appeared. She turned up at the house of the
farmer who had bought the cow, and meanwhile
the latter animal, after carrying on in a most out-
rageous manner, had broken loose, and bolted into
the bush. Cow and mare returned in company to
the home of the original owner during the after-
noon. Subsequent efforts to keep them apart
failed, and the settler got his cow back at half-
price. He is looking for another purchaser.

“R.W.” : Yes, most out-back publicans
“ do their own killing,” to their neighbors’ dis-
comfiture, but it works out all right in the end.
“I had dinner, at Hogan’s to-day,” said one of
my acquaintances, “and I’ll swear I ate my own
meat.” “I hope the change agreed with you,”
remarked his hearer.
* * * »
Way back in the 60’s some bullock-drivers,
arriving with tired cattle at the mile or so of steep
hill on the road from Bruthen to Omeo, unyoked
their teams and went to the pub. at Tambo cross-
ing. When they came back to their drays in
the evening they found a case of gin out of the
loading open on the ground, with all the bottles
but two neck-broken and empty, and had to pull
the roasted boozer out of the fire, just in time to
see him die. His swag gave no clue to his identity,
so he was buried on the spot, and his logged-in
grave remained a gruesome reminder for years
before a bush-fire removed all trace of it.

“Lynx”: Be “Steele Rudd’s” par.
(8., 8/10/’9B), the laughing jack is not a fraud !
Know personally of several instances of snakes
being swooped upon and killed by jackasses. In
one case last summer, where two men were clear-
ing, jack, without warning, swooped down and
nailed a 2ft. long snake close beside them. Know
of another instance where two jacks swooped down
simultaneously on large snake, and, in the end,
finished him. My experience is that jack prefers
to fly with his snake to a height of from 30 to 40ft.,
and then drop him, obviously for disabling pur-
poses. “ S.R.’s ” idea of placing jack and a snake
together in a cage, or 24ft. ring, has nothing in it.
J ack is too shy for circus tricks.

“ 8.D.5.”: More dingo. Was camped one
evening on Koolburra Creek, Cape York Peninsula
(N.Q.). Time, middle of wet season. Creek run-
ning strong—nearly a banker. Saw a kangaroo,
close pressed by two dingoes, jump on to an almost
submerged rock about 10ft. from bank. Dingoes
held hurried consultation. Evident conclusion,
“current too swift for straight swim ” ; and one
goes a little way up-stream, the other standing
guard opposite kangaroo. First takes to water, is
swept down to the rock, and, making ineffectual
snap at besieged in passing, lands lower down.
Same thing repeated several times, till dingoes,
disturbed by rifle-shot, decamp, when kangaroo
hops ashore and away.
* * * *
“Jep”: lie “ B.P.’s” account of supposed
bunyip at head of Murrumbidgee, and bunyips in
general. If he isn’t lately from England he should
know that the creature he saw has been seen in
different forms and circumstances, all over N.S.
Wales. Early settlers called it “ too much ’pos-
sum.” The Camden cookies have a traditional
bunyip—half fish, half dingo—which disports itself
in the lagoons and feeds luxuriously on podgy
calves for choice. Sensible folk call it “snake-
juice.” And, away out in the “flat country,”
boundary-riders, shepherds, and such lonely liars
have given freezing accounts of a fearsome-looking
monster seen in the lakes of that region. “ Touch
of the sun ”is its other name. Finally, let B.P.
study Bulletin’s pork-supper sketches and see if
he can’t pick his bunyip.
* * * *
“J.D.”: About trees leaning to N.E.
Never observed it, but majority of trees twist
, towards sun. Moreover, every splitter knows that
when the bark of a (say) stringy-bark or messmate
twists ever so slightly away from the north-east,
i.e., with the sun, it is well to leave that tree
severely alone; only corkscrews could be split
from it. But when the bark twists against the
sun the chances are that the grain of the wood is
quite straight. I suppose the sun draws the bark
round after it, or has a tendency to do so. If it
also draws the whole tree in a similar fashion that
would account for the “lean ” being north-east.
* * * *
“Malle” : A N.S.W. pastoralist of great
wealth ran a store, in a i fining township, in the
very long ago. Once he found himself with a
large quantity of weevilly flour on hand, and the
way he traded it off on the rival storekeeper fully
explains how he attained his present affluence.
He had a dray-load of flour coming up from
Sydney, and, when the new consignment was
about due, he loaded up the weevilly lot on his
own dray in the dead of night, and drove out to
intercept the teamster and his load. Then he
made an exchange, and got back at dawn with the
new lot, while the teamster had instructions to
drive in with the old decayed sample a day or two
later, and go through certain performances on
arrival. In the interval, the wily storekeeper
went round to his rival, lamented that he was
out of flour, and tried to buy “a little to goon
with,” and was repulsed with scorn. Then the
teamster came and surreptitiously whispered
that other storekeeper in a dark corner. He
said he had a load of flour for Blank, but he had
heard on the road that Blank was just about
insolvent, and he didn’t think it safe to deliver.
Did the other storekeeper know anything about
Blank? The other storekeeper didn’t commit
himself, but, seeing a chance to make a corner in
flour and snatch all the trade, he offered the cur-
rent market-price in cash for the entire consign-
ment. And there and then he acquired the whole

bye Tom, an’ me two horses pwhat doied on me,”
was his reply.

“0.C.”: Re the carpet-snake as a rat-
eater. Writer once put two big rats into a box
with a 7ft. carpet-snake. Next morning found
that the rats had eaten the 'snake’s head off and
laid bare about 4in. of his spine.

“ Long-Un ” : On Lake Everard station
recently some Fowler’s Bay blacks joined the
local tribe, and, when all are squatting round fire,
gin pulls out emu breastbone, places one end in
ashes, and tilts other over towards coastal Jimmy,
who, the moment he spots bone, collars his spears
and ’rang, and makes off full gallop. Next day
sent after him a black boy, who returned in a fort-
night. “Find ’em, Jimmy?” “Yes, bin find
’em alonga sandhill (pureba hill) ; ’em peg out
alright.” Over 50 miles distant!
“Bogan”: Re slanguage. In grasping your
meaning, the Australian youth will either “ brown-
joe to yer dicken graft ”or “ take a tumble.” His
home is ‘ * therubby dub, ” his occupation “ the j pint, ”
his friends “cobblers,” and gaol “ booby.” If ar-
rested, a “John” “sloughed” him. A glass of
beer is “ a pot of wollop,” and the previous night
he was “on his pink,” “juiced,” “wined,” or
“shikkered.” His horse and cart is “ the puff
and dart,” and he drives it along; “ the frog and
toad.” On a racecourse, the other day, saw a
gentleman manipulating a purse and sovereigns.
uddenly “ cobber ” walks up behind him, “ Nit 1
Hopscotch on yer paddy-whack, right hook.”
Artist disappears. Interpreted, “ Stop it. Police-
man after you, right-hand side.” Bub, after all,
it’s mostly cockney thieves’ patter.
• * * *
House-removing on the Barrier is done
with jinkers. Record travel was in shifting store,
Silverton to Broken Hill (20 miles) with 38 bul-
locks, in three weeks. Some days didn’t do over
100 yards.

“ Lob ” : Best way to get rabbits out of
burrow is—give a crayfish a piece of lighted candle
in his claw and put him tail-first into burrow.
He will keep backing away from light until reach-
ing end of burrow. Knew an experienced cray to
go four miles through burrow and have candle
alight at t’other end. The cray is called “ Cousin
Jack’s ferret,” and, like any other ferret,'if he
nabs a bunny inside he’ll stop and feast on him
unless hauled out by a piece of string tied round
his neck.
Alba Studios, photo.
Mr . and Mrs. ROBERT BROUGH .
“J.H.8.”: To “Yokel’s” question
(8., 24/9/’9B) “ Are pigs strychnine-proof ? ” Yes !
The nut from which strychnine is prepared poisons
humans ; also dogs, rats, and many other quadru-
peds, but can be eaten by hogs and goats with
impunity. It is also non-poisonous to many
species of the S. American toucan. [This doesn’t
square with the fact that the toucan is poisonable
by “ woorali ” and the pig by sumpitan-dart
poison—both obtained from strychniferous plants.]
“H.S.R.”: Are black
spoonbills common in any
part of Australia? I have
only seen one all black, near
Texas, Dumaresq River, Q.
Arethe black-and-white ibis?
We have the black ones by
the thousand, but I have
never seen above six white
ones together, and always on
rivers or water. The black
ones are everywhere. How
does the platypus suckle
young ? How often do mar-
supials breed in the year ?
I think at least three times,
for I have hardly ever seen
females at the productive age
without joeys either in the
pouch or the uterus. I have
got as many as three joeys in
the pouch of the scrub wal-
laby, two in the wallaroo’s,
only one in the grey kanga-
roo’s. Have seen nine young
attached to small native-cat.
Have seen old - man grey
kangaroo with pouch. Have
seen scrub wallaby with only
one arm, born that way.
Have found bullet embedded
in spine of old-man wallaroo
who was fat and hearty, and
must have got it years before.

“C.H.” : Can vouch for the truth of this
case of pre-natal influence. A young woman,
some weeks prior to the birth of her infant,
received a nasty blow in the eye from her husband
whilst the latter was in liquor. The child was
born minus an eye ; and from just above where the
eyebrow ought to have been (for there was neither
eye, brow, lids, nor, in fact, the sign of any orifice)
to the cheek-bone, and extending partly across the
nose, was a smooth, dark-purple mark, correspond-
ing exactly with that on the woman’s face. I saw
both mother and child, and could, therefore, com-
pare satisfactorily.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
Seudo : Sydney Harbor is, as it were, a public
park. A water-park instead of a land-park. No one is
allowed to offend the public eye by disfiguring the Cen-
tennial Park with brutal poster-advts. Then why should
anybody be allowed to brutalise the Harbor with floating
whisky, tobacco or pill advts? Waipiro :In sending
extract-verse in MS. form you should have expressly said
it was extract. The fact of its being written, not printed,
and on a page bearing your signature, led us to believe
you were tendering it as original Z. : Lynching-in-
etfigy is an offence against the law. And it would be

THE CUP-A DANGEROUS OUTSIDER.


A DAY OF HUMILIATION.
AQUARIUS: “ Don’t shoot ! I’ll come down!”


Query. Will the new-old Q. Premier fol-
low the example of his predecessor and oppose the
attempted New Guinea land steal ? Echo answers,
“ ’E ain’t built that way.”
Perth (W.A.) Council advertises for ten-
ders for “ the right to search the Council’s rubbish
heap.” Another sign of hard times in the land of
the setting sun and the stormy wild cat.
Sounds apocryphal, but isn’t so. A Cairns
man, working at a centrifugal, wore an apron
which caught somehow on the revolving shaft.
Every scrap of clothing was torn off the man into
the veriest shreds ; and the man himself—never
budged and never got a scratch.
A N.S.W. country alderman is fond of
scraps of Latin, &o. Questioning the genuineness
of a certain disinfectant to be used by the council,
he remarked : “If ya read th’ advertisement, yull
see th’ gen-uin stuff has got a face smile of th’
manufactrer’s signachure, which this lot ain’t
got.” During late lamented election same genius
orated: “I’d do ennythink ta frustrate the tic-
tacs of those demi-dogs, the so-called Labor-
party.”
Walking on the sea-cliff, while Cape
Governor, Grey’s foot struck an obstacle. It
proved to be a skull, with a piece of golden hair
Sticking to it. This was the burial place of a
cargo of female convicts, for Australia, drowned
through wreckage.

The Eleventh Report of the Directors of the Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited,
To be presented to the Shareholders at the Eleventh Ordinary General Meeting, to be held at the Bank, 126 Elizabeth-
street, at Noon, on Monday, 31st October, 1898.
REPORT.
The directors beg to submit to the shareholders their eleventh report, with a balance-sheet and statement of
profit and loss, for the half-year ended 30th September, duly audited.

note circulation, the net profit amounted to £4163 17s. 3d., which the directors propose to apportion as follows, viz. :

_ Melbourne, 20th October, 1898.
To the Shareholders of the Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited.
_ Ladies and Gentlemen,—We beg: to report that we have audited the accounts of the Colonial Bank of Australasia
Limited for the half-year ended 30th September, 1898, and that, so far as we are in a position to form an opinion, the
balance-sheet and accounts have been drawn up in accordance with the provisions of the “ Companies Acts,” and
present a correct view of the state of the company’s affairs, and that all our requisitions in regard to the shareholders’
as well as the private balance-sheet have been duly complied with.
We are, ladies and gentlemen, yours faithfully.
W. BOULLY, A.1.1.A.
FRED. H. WILSON, F.I.A.Y.

“ Timi ” : I was intimately acquainted with
William Sylvester Pulford (the ex-Vic. police cadet
alluded to in a recent Bulletin) during his Maori-
land career. He was business manager of Auck-
land Observer, Charles Otho Montrose being
then editor. Both men had been under fire,
Montrose as a soldier and war correspondent dur-
ing the Waikato war, and Pulford while bush-
ranger-chasing, and it was the fun of the world to
hear “ Monty ” relate a tale of derring-do, which
Pulford would most artistically cap. One time a
trick-shootist struck the town, and gave public
exhibitions of his skill. Pulford challenged Mon-
trose to mount the stage and display his nerve
while the shootist removed an apple from his head.
Montrose, nothing loth, submitted himself, and
the apple was deftly divided by a bullet. Then
Pulford insisted upon emulating the feat of his
friend, but, expanding his chest just as the trigger
was pulled, he imperceptibly increased his stature,
and the bullet furrowed his skull. Poor Pulford
never quite recovered from the injury, and even-
tually died under distressing circumstances. He
was the father of “ Willie ” Pulford, once well-
known as a scenic artist to strolling theatrical
co.’s.
“The struggle for a crust.” “Cyril” to
The Bulletin:—
Re recent par. on the text: “ Country editor wanted ;
salary, £2 a week.” Nearly 100 “ atonies ” replied, all
eager to lead bucolic thought, with the help of stereo,
supplements, to better things than pumpkins. Which
roll-up of the ready writers is but a circumstance com-
pared to another muster about a month back. An advt. ap-
peared in S.M. Herald requiring services of gentlemanly,
companionable man for a driving tour through Riverina.
Apply 2.30 p.m., , street, North Shore.” Such
was the rush for , street, that the cable-tram
guards thought a land-sale was on, sure. And as mobs of
“gentlemanly,” &c., went up the hill by tram or on foot,
according to finance, other obviously disappointed droves
were passed coming down in a long procession. When
the writer’s brigade arrived at , street, about 2.25
p.m., the advertiser declined to be interviewed, but
Bhouted through the keyhole he had been suited at 6.30
a,m. Several rude remarks were passed by the applicants,
who, seeing another detachment advancing in skirmishing
order, retired cursing. Over 200 men applied for that job;
taking fares, boat and tram, at 10d., and allowing half
walked, over £4 was dragged out of their pockets,
thanks to the laziness of advertiser, who might as well
have arranged for written application. But the “ side ” of
the person eager to become a white nigger-driver is
colossal. “ Wanted, a gentleman of good address, familiar
with suburbs, to represent business firm.” Then fol-
lowed address—a very far suburb ; and the far end of that
suburb, too. What was the fate of the many who called
in answer to this, I do not know; but one man,
whose previous excellent position was only lost through
prolonged ill-health, states “ the business firm ” was first
represented by a bare-footed woman, and subsequently
by a frowsy older man. The meeting-place was a cross
between a pig-pen, duck-pond, and fowl-yard; and the
job consisted of hawking round, on foot, a dozen or so
live ducks and cocks and hens; no salary—commission
ljd. to 2d. a bird. The fowl-run being too muddy to
enter, the interview was conducted and applicant
sampled at a range of about 121 f. ; also, the male partner
of the business firm sampled the effect of a large handful
of gravel travelling on a true trajectory; point of impact,
his pachydermatous cheek.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Extract from letter of a W.A. legislator to
an importunate Town Council:—“There is no-
thing left in the Treasury but a large hole, and
that is getting larger every day.”
Seven Ms.L.A. lately expelled from Q.
Parliament for over-excitement, as alleged, con-
stitute the phalanx of -inveterate opponents of the
Parliamentary spirituous refreshment-bar.
Henry Copeland, whose Parliamentary
jemmy opened the doors of Sydney Zoo and Art
Gallery on Sundays, refuses to stay his hand till
he works the pub.-doors ajar on the same day.
A Leg. Assembly return reports that 38
(coal-laden) vessels sailing from the port of New-
castle, N.S.W., were lost in the last five years—
and 261 lives. The loss of vessels and life from
Sydney in the same period was nothing like so
heavy, though Sydney has many times the traffic
of Newcastle.
Premier Reid publicly declares that he will
not accept the Agency-General. Let’s see. So
did Premier Cowper, and he got there ; Premier
Forster, and he got there ; and Premier Alexander
Stuart, and, in a sense, he got there. Stuart,
indeed, was appointed in 1879, but resigned in
1880 without actually taking office.
Three months ago Premier Braddon gave
as his reason for not appointing an Agent-General
for his province, the imminence of Australian
Federation. Last week he appointed Fysh for
the full term of three years. Has Federation
receded in the Braddon eye, or was the “gentle-
man ” Premier merely talking politically ?
Thus Leahy, a prominent Q. legislator, of
Hibernian extraction : “ We are not followers of
Mr. Dickson, but followers of Mr. Philp, who is a
follower of Mr. Dickson.” This is really a fine
distinction ! If a cipher follows a cipher that fol-
lows another cipher, which cipher is boss, and
what do they all amount to in sum-total ?
“ Yic.” : How remarkable to find Deakin
leading the way for religious education in State-
schools ! This must be a clever politician’s indi-
cation of the current of public opinion—or else a
blind politician’s mistake. When a Democracy
takes the power nobody is to prescribe what it
will do. I believe Australia will come round to
a State church.
Edward Terry, M.L.A., leader of the
little Independent party in N.S.W. Assembly,
started his maiden speech by remarking that he
was a neutral Independent. Having said so much
he proceeded to rub it into Reid—
“ I’m in a peculiar position,”
Said the wealthy man from Ryde—
“l’m a neutral Independent
On the Opposition side.”
The funny little Freetrade Democratic
Association of Yic. has found a real purpose in
life. H. R. Reid, the brother of “No-Yes,”
addressing an attenuated meeting of the Faithful,
a few nights ago, besought them to keep their
association on its feeble legs “ until the time came
for them to urge their Freetrade policy on the
new Commonwealth of Australia.” Meanwhile,
they are maintaining their Collins-street office on
a small income and a forlorn hope.
Re Mrs. Sidney Webb’s remark to Kater,
M.L.C., that “the conservatism of thb colonies is
not the conservatism of England 'to-day but of
the England of 1534 ” ! it may be apropos to point
out that Arthur Griffith, M.L. A., has not, so far,
been able to get any prominent member of N.S.W.
Leg. Council to take charge of his “Bill to
abolish attachment of wages.” Yet this Bill
merely seeks to bring local law into line with that
in Britain, where a similar Act was passed years
ago.
With another change of Premier in Leper-
land, it may be interesting to note that since the
Northern province was granted Responsible
Government—3B years ago—it has had 13 prime-
ministers—Herbert, Macalister, Mackenzie, Lilley,
Palmer, Thorn, Douglas, MTlwraith, Griffith,
Morehead, Nelson, Byrnes, and Dickson. Nation-
alities : 3 Australian, 4 Scotch, 1 Irish, 4 English,
1 Welsh. The most brilliant of the crowd was
Griffith ; the dullest, Thorn. Lilley was the most
progressive ; Nelson the most reactionary. Griffith
held sway the longest—seven years and four
months ; Byrnes was Premier for only 168 days.
Dickson may hang on for something over 168
hours.
Another conference of the rail way
managers of N.S.W., Queensland, Victoria, and
S.A. has been held at. Melbourne, and last week
it closed up in disgust with no results. The four
distracted departments couldn’t decide on a uni-
form rail way-gauge, because they couldn’t agree
on a fair division of the cost of alteration. There
was some talk of giving up the policy of carrying
goods from the border districts at a heavy loss in
order to attract traffic, but each one reckoned that
the other province was trying to get ahead of his
province, and each man reckoned that the other
fellow’s proposal was loaded. The Vic. and S.A.
men reckoned (in their own interests) that traffic
should be allowed to go to the nearest port, and
the N.S.W. and Queensland men considered (in
their own interests) that each province should get
all its trade for its own port. And until the
Federal Government grabs the situation with a
strong hand, and abolishes provincial boundaries,
and runs all the railways on plain commercial
principles, they will go on just like that.
Re disarmament. Armies provide billets
for the aristocracy and employment for millions of
men, and, though the people em-
ployed might just as well be idle if
peace is possible, many of them will
resist the destruction of their means
of livelihood if they have to fight
for it. The standing armies of the
world, as the Czar seems to see, are
a decayed tooth that needs extrac-
tion, but, if he thinks a suggestion
will hurry the patient off to have
it out regardless of the pain, he may
be a first-class emperor, but he’s a
mighty poor dentist.
Someone should submit a Bill
for the suppression of that heavy
bore of N.S.W. Assembly, Broken
Hill Sleath. Sleath is possessed
of the strange delusion that he is a
born humorist, and spends much
of his time interjecting inane re-
marks, presumably intended to be
funny, and laughing hideously and
immoderately at them himself.
Labor-member Ferguson, who also
interjects frequently, does possess
humor, but Sleath is about as comic
as a heap of wood.
Another burden added to the
load carried by N.S.W. country members :
Hawking their constituents’ “ pomes ” around to
newspaper-offices.
When a Yic. M.L.A., in recent debate on
Libel-Law Amendment Bill, asserted that the
Age’s manufacture of oratorical absurdities for
the Delatite member was calculated to bring him
into contempt in his constituency, Graves
promptly interjected that his constituents con-
sidered that paper too thundering a liar to take
any notice of what it said. Another member, in
same debate, observed that “ things had come to
such a pass that if a public man fell out with the
two morning papers in Melbourne they could
practically ruin his public career by writing him
down and falsifying his statements. The dis-
agreement between the reports of the two papers
on the same subject had become a perfect
scandal.”
The agitation for the separation of the
Eastern goldfields of Westralia and annexation by
South Australia is a fizzle; though the constitu-
tional provision by which a portion cut off is
saddled by way of debt with only the amount
spent within it will help separation movements
when the time is ripe. At present Coolgardie
sniffs at the agitation, Kanowna is inclined the
same way, and only Kalgoorlie is at all warm.
“ South Australia is a poverty-stricken province,
already overburdened with territory, with a
revenue and population no greater than ours, and
with a per capita debt of 4264 as against 4224 of
this colony.” Upon that argument, repeated with
a hundred variations, those who pooh-pooh the
enthusiasm stand solid.
The cable-man, who generally gets things
upside-down,- has been circulating a report in
England that the Maoriland Old Age Pension
scheme provides an income for every old man
who likes to claim it—in fact, that it pro-
vides a pension for every old man on earth if he
chooses to come and live in Maoriland. Seven
ancient gaffers have already arrived from England
with the avowed intention of living on .the M.L.
Govt, for the rest of their days ; they started out
so promptly that they reached the spot before the
Pension Bill was even passed. And now that
they find a 15 years’ residence-qualification is
needful, several infuriated ancients have given
notice that they want heavy damages, and will
apply to the British Govt, to bombard Wellington
if necessary.
A SURE SIGN OF OVER-FEEDING.
"You must be starving that ’orse. Look how ’is bones is stickin' out.”
“ That’s because he’s overfed! ’E's so chock full of feed that’s there’s no room for
’is bones, and they’re getting pushed out!”
The suggested making of Port Darwin a
second Australasian naval station would greatly
increase Britain’s command of the down-under
maritime situation, and would be a good thing
for Australia generally, as tending to wake up a
very lost and forlorn corner of this continent.
The Salisbury (N.S.W.) land exchanges,
which were at the bottom of the Carruthers-
Norton-Lonsdale criminal-libel proceedings, have
not yet been effected, and negotiations will, it is
said, end without any exchange whatever. The
why of this will make an interesting Parliamen-
tary return.
LAW BREAKING AND RECORD
BREAKING.
MAGISTRATE (to bloomered cycliste ): “ You have
been found guilty of running over and injur-
ing this old man.” To the court orderly,
“ What’s her record?”
PRISONER (sobbing): “ Two-forty-five unpaced,
your worship."
Premier John Forrest’s excuse for refusing
the goldfields adequate representation in W.A.
Parliament—in proportion to population they have
about one-eighth the representation of the other
parts of the province snows great poverty of in-
vention. He says the last Parliament made a re-
distribution of seats, and he can’t overturn the
arrangements again so early. If the last Parlia-
ment had made a fair distribution of seats there
might be something in this, but all it did was to
give the goldfields about one-sixth of the Parlia-
mentary representation they were fairly entitled
to. Forrest’s attempt to calm the Separationists
by reminding them of that bygone outrage is
merely pouring curses on the troubled waters.
A telegram from the dusty West expresses
surprise that Wentworth (on the Murray, adja-
cent to Sheol, close to where the three provinces
meet, where the wind shrieks on the plains in
winter and the summer temperature, reaches 115
in the shade) hasn’t been more often mentioned
as the Federal capital. One good reason for plant-
ing the capital out thereis that it would give the
Federal Government a grand chance to read up
the irrigation problem. The shortest way to a
good comprehensive irrigation policy is to deposit
the Government in the. dry wilderness and let it
see the thing for itself. Out there it couldn’t
help learning something about this question—one
of the most important that Australia has to deal
with.

A Labor
member pro-
tested in S.A.
Assembly the
other day be-
cause some
other M.P.
had slipped in
before him
with a motion
in favor of
making travel-
ling allowances uniform in all grades arid
departments of the Public Service. He hotly
declared that there should be honor “ even among
members of Parliament.” His statement is that
many of the most highly-paid S.A. civil-servants
travel by railway on a free pass, carry sandwiches
and a bottle of milk with them, and sleep on board
the train—and yet never fail to claim 10s. a day
travelling expenses.
_‘‘ He (the Hon. W. Downie Stewart)
frightened us with the threat that capital would
fly out of the country. How often have we not
heard this during the last year ? How often have
we not had pictured to us the gaunt picture of
frightened capital striding with furious stride sea-
ward, with its money-bags under its. arm..
Twomey, M.H.R. (Maoriland).
The latest addition to the ever-changing
yet continuous Q. Ministry is G. W. Gray—a
small, harmless, semi-articulate Scotsman who
married a rich R.C. lady and —got converted.
The Govt, clearly intend Gray as a bait for the
R.C. vote —his elevation is not understandable
otherwise. By this move Dickson has shown a
lamentable ignorance of human nature the
“ ’vert ” is really looked upon with as much
suspicion as the apostate.
The Westralian Govt.’s latest retrench-
ment idea is the abolition of all public schools
where there are less than 35 or 40 children (if
this is carried out it means the closure of about
one-third the schools in W.A.), and the refusal to
open new ones even when buildings are offered
free. This cuts two ways :It is found that educa-
tion opens people’s eyes too much, and a retro-
grade movement is desirable to preserve the
ascendancy of the Forrest crowd. Also, it saves
money. In answer to a letter of remonstrance
from Goongarrie, in which it was hinted that
retrenchment might take some other form less
disastrous to the community, the mouthpiece of
the Education Department wrote : “ The Minister
agrees with you that it is desirable that economy
was (sic) observed in other directions, but he has
to deal unfortunately (sic) with facts. ’ Which
burst of grammar from a high educational source
suggests that W.A. can’t spare any of its schools.
For ten years past the subsidy paid by
Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria, and W.A. for the
government of Papua has exceeded the outlay,
and when the subsidy agreement expired this
year there was a surplus of =630,000 to be returned
to these provinces. Gov. Bill M‘Gregor gently
persuaded them to hand over this sum before he
sprang the news of the great syndicate-grab upon
them. M‘Gregor, by the way, is represented as
saying that he never had any idea that the N.S. W.
and Vic. Premiers didn’t know all about the stu-
pendous land-deal which practically shuts out
Australian settlement from Papua for 30 or 40
years or so. Mac. recently met these Premiers in
conference on Papuan affairs. He didn’t mention
the great syndicate ; neither did they, because
they never heard of it. And this dead silence on
everybody’s part, including his own, about the
most important Papuan subject of the day seems
to have struck M'Gregor as quite natural. The
fact that M‘G. said nothing rather tends, it would
seem, to prove that he hadn’t the least desire to
conceal anything.
J. A. Andrews : X am surprised to find
The Bulletin, which I used to imagine was
nothing if not Australian, insisting that the
Federal capital must be in a cold climate. Such a
situation may be admirable for the imported
people, who would only be in their own element;
but haven’t we had rather an overdose of im-
ported politicians and civil servants ? What about
us Australians, the majority of whom have not
been born and brought up on Mount Kosciusko,
and are only at home in the friendly rays of our
glorious sun and the genial freshness and warmth
of our life-giving breezes ? How could we attend
properly to the affairs of our country as legislators
or civil servants, or do our business with the
Government as members of the public, in a state
of morbid frigidity or feverish waste of hydro-
carbon, with our toes frozen, our shoulders drawn,
our limbs benumbed, our bodies shivering, and our
brains hypnotised with the desperate craving for
blankets and hot whisky ? Let the Australian
capital offer a fair average sample of the Austra-
lian climate; or it will afflict the land with a
deadly chill of un-Australian-ness for ever.
Auckland (M.lj.) people are clamoring for
their long-postponed Trunk railway.' The North
Island would have got its railway long ago but for
the fight over the rival routes. Some want the
railway to go through the centre of the island,
while the majority favor a West Coast route.
This would be more to Auckland’s advantage, as
it would tap Taranaki for its benefit. Unfortu-
nately, an independent committee, years ago,
decided in favor of the Central route, which
Wellingtonians strongly favor. They have the
further pull on Auckland that construction, in-
cluding a ,4230,000'viaduct, has been begun at their
. end. Auckland vehemently asserts that the
Central route passes through 40 miles of desert —
which is true; Wellington replies that it taps a
magnificent totara forest—which is also true.
Auckland says that the Stratford route opens up
a lot of good land—which is true ; Wellington ex-
plains that aforesaid good land is so broken as to
make railway construction too expensive—which
is also true. The Bulletin’s local correspondent
favors the Taranaki route (which happens to pass
right through his farm). Auckland is in deadly
earnest on the matter. Its tradesmen are using
invoices headed “ Remember the Taranaki route.”
Meanwhile, Govt., which depends chiefly on
South Island support, sits tight and does nothing;
and, so long as Auckland and Wellington are pull-
ing different ways, it is not likely to do much.
