Doctor Bevan, the Melb. divine, de-
scribes the objects of the order of “ the Daughters
of the Court”—a semi-pious hen-convention—as
“ the assistance of Art in all its forms, and the
encouragement of people never to speak ill of one
another.” Two very long-felt wants in godly
circles.
Rougernont, whose picturesque narrative
is the subject of so much controversy, has been at-
tacked by Louis Becke in The Daily Chronicle.
Becke spent many years in the island trade, and
got enough story matter out of the islands to make
himself famous as a writer. So he is undoubtedly
well-equipped to make things warm for Rouge-
mont. Becke roasts Rougemont’s turtle stories,
especially the one about Rougernont sitting astride
a turtle’s back and steering the marine gee-gee by
thrusting a toe into its eye. If you stuck your toe
into a turtle’s eye, says Becke, the turtle would
sink vertically. The best thing now for the ex-
islander to do is to each prove his case by experi-
menting on the Thames, before a crowd at so
much a head. Becke would certainly look as
picturesque on a turtle as Rougernont ever did,
and if the Rougernont toe can serve as a bridle,
there appears no reason why the Becke toe should
not. And, if either Rougernont or Becke desire
any training for the experiment he had better
do it on Sydney Lager Bier. It is the best tonic
going.*
The recent death of a singularly alert
young Sydney bankruptcy official, was attributed
to overwork in connection with the Horton insol-
vency case, he having had to take voluminous evi-
dence in shorthand and type-write it in addition
to regular office-work. This, following the sad
case of Parry Long, the Registrar-General, seems
to point to the necessity of some regulation by the
Public Service Board against the overworking of
willing public servants who can be ill spared.
Any Bulletin reader in need of a long-
lost brother, or an unexpected estate, is advised
to consult J. W. Holdsworth, the well-known
Melbourne inquiry agent, who has lately had
much success in unravelling intricate claims of
next-of-kin, and tracing missing and mislaid
persons.*
All kinds of rumors have been attached
to the return from England of the canny
and deliberate S.M. Herald editor Cur-
now, and The Bulletin is waiting to see the
E air fax organ slide gradually back to the abnor-
mally-cautious traditions of its unemotional
past. During the reign of acting-editor Gullett
the Herald has persistently plugged the stolid
Brunker with a playful left, and has lodged its
good right hand in the most vital portion of Pre-
mier Reid’s anatomy—the place where the wind
comes from. And, although the yarn that editor
Curnow was suddenly recalled to end the Herald’s
unwonted burst of patriotism is apparently un-
true, it will need long and careful nursing, and an
unbroken diet of Herald'milk-and-water, to re-
store Reid and his holy lieutenant to their normal
health. It is understood that with Mr. Curnow’s
resumption of Herald editorial control Mr. Gul-
lett will visit England for a substantial holiday,
to which he and Mrs. Gullett have long looked
forward.

WHAT THE DOC TORS SAY
Woolloston-house, Castlemaine,
November 30th, 1897.
My dear Mr. Malign,—
I have used vour ointment. Ligrnolliene with distinct
success in my own case, but with greater as an emollient
unguent for the face of a young lady suffering from
“Acne,” very common during puberty, and you should
push it in that direction. Kindly send me another box
of it. —Yours truly, GEO. D. WOOLEY, M.D.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY:—
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record pro-
gress with regard to my old enemy “ Eczema Capitas,”
from which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treat-
ment for some years under the prescriptions of Pro-
fessor M'Oall Anderson and other eminent derma-
tologists, with only partial and temporary relief.
When I met you I commenced somewhat sceptically
your ointment Ligrnolliene, and after about two months’
treatment I may honestly say my enemy is defeated,
and lam perfectly well. I will only add that since I
commenced the treatment of myself I have prescribed it
to others, and am still doing so with brilliant results.
Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain vours sin-
cerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.
Discovered by the Proprietor,
William Henry Malyon,
MANUFACTURING CHEMIST, Ballarat.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Running a weary eye over the Melbourne Cup
card this year one is struck by the weakness of the 3-year-
old forces. Last year Aurum and Aiuberite, likewise
lleet Admiral, were thought possible winners of the
Derby and Cup double, but nobody to-day can be very
sweet on any of the young horses for the two-mile handicap
Bohadil, a legitimately hot favorite for the Derby was
scratched for the Cup when the weights appeared. If
Bobadil wins the blue ribbon—as he probably will, though
all the same, he isn’t worth backing at the price-Cocos
and Gardite will not look dangerous next Tuesday. And
if Cocos takes the Derby it will merely prove that Bobadil
can’t stay, and Cardite is a commoner. The writer
reckons that none of the fancied 3-year-olds are Al—that
the Derby result will be Bobbie 1, Cocos 2, Cardite 3—and
that Cardite should be equal to Cocos over the Cup dis-
tance, wherever they may finish. Woodlark or the dark
Symmetry may beat them both.
By the way, The Provost, judging from his
running at Caulfield, the other Saturday, will have more
than an ordinary chance to win one of the hurdle races
at Flemington. Make a note of The Provost.
Lightly-handicapped Cup horses are Massinissa
Woodlark, Dreamland, and Plutus. Massinissa didn’t finish
well for the Caulfield Cup. It seemed that he wasn’t a
sticker, and after the race the gelding had a dead-beaten
appearance. A non-stayer can even win Caulfield Cup
as Massinissa might have won it with better luck
and yet fall to pieces at the end of the Flemington
two. miles. Dreamland is now given a better chance
than ever before. He is said to be a better horse, too
But he has failed in four successive Melb. Cups, carrying-
stable-money each time, wherefore the writer loves him
not. The writer’s two-and-sixpence has too often been
misplaced on Mentor’s half-brother.
Take Rosebery, Wait-a-Bit, Catspaw and
Clarion as middle-weights, ranging from 7st 61b
to Bst. 51b. Of these Wait-a-Bit has the finest
shov L ‘ on paper.” If he were a straggler he
would be a ‘moral,” “cert,” “dead-bird,” &c He
hasn t ever struggled into first place thusfar-not when
good horses were bustling him. Rosebery is fast but
> S u ’ and may not be a genuine 2-miler when “ fit ”
1 erhaps it wasn’t merely superfluous flesh that made
Rosebery drop back so suddenly in Caulfield Cup. The
big horse may be only another Hopscotch. Clarion has a
chance on his “ form ” in weight-for-age races, because
he meets his conquerors on much better terms. No man
knoweth, however, the exact comparative value of
Clarion’s running in the many races which he hasn’t yvon.
lhe writer reckons that this respectable performer
will get among the first eight for the Cup. Likewise
Catspaw. Everything depends on which end of the
eight places they arrive at.
® ne °f the top-weights—Amberite, Battalion,
Merloolas, The Grafter, and the Chief—should win the
Melbourne Stakes on Saturday. Presuming that the
state of the course doesn’t change in the meantime,, the
stakes winner and runner-up may have a big say in the
Cup The writer reckons that Amberite will beat
Grafter on a hard course, and Grafter will beat
Amberite if the going is soft, as it was last year. A
strong case can be made out for Merloolas, and though he
has had less “ preparation ” at Flemington than some of
the others, he is possibly all the better for the indulgence.
Many connoisseurs prefer an underdone gelding to one
» at ,- as een well-backed and basted on the track.
According to some of his public performances Merloolas
should beat all the other top-weights. But The Grafter
pretensions to do likewise. Ditto Amberite.
Ditto The Chief. There is more or less reason why any-
one of them should turn up trumps on the day.' The
writer likes Amberite best of the lot, on the strength of
nis proved ability to do big things in handicaps when he
has had all the worst of the weights.
Reducing the guess to six names, the writer
takes Amberite, Catspaw, Clarion, Merloolas, The
Grafter, and The Chief, and places them accord-
th®. wea ther. For a dry day—Amberite,
Merloolas, Clarion, The Chief. For a muddy day—The
Grafter, Catspaw—well, if there were several other
sorts of days he would mention all the horses in the
race, ana perchance strike the winner somewhere.
Believers in coincidences may note that prior to
Gaul us winning Melb. Cup he was the last horse to leave
d o ne ,£ tO . tkafc engagement. This year’s Cup candi-
date Toxteth, is the last to leave for the Vie. capital,
>' k e Gaums he also won Rosehill Oct. Handicap
prior to his departure. Gaulus won his race at Rosehill
by any number of lengths ; so did Toxteth.
«•* * * *
‘ Victor ”: A wary backer of any horse for a
big event generally wishes to be satisfied on the following
points ere he planks down his dollars-Are owner, trainer
and jock right ? And is the horse a good ’un ? For Melb.
Cup, the Carbine horse, Carbinier, seems to about fill the
ticket, for not only are the above conditions fulfilled, but
this genuine candidate is the best-bred horse in the race,
'or three yearold 8 DOt been raCed t 0 death eithel ' as a two
• * * *
Astonishing how the horse-reporters worship
success. They are all agreed that the Caulfield Cup
accident made no difference to the result. Hymettus
must have won under any circumstances, they think but
y th an tSay i t u e seven horses that toDpled over,
and the several other horses that lost ground through the
scrimmage, should not have provided the winner if the
luck had been equally distributed.
* * * *
It was the circular shape of the course that ac-
counted for Aiemene falling in Moonee Valley Cup, any-
how The Gozo mare ran up to the Locksmith at the
tmal turn, and in trying to rush round she lost her footing.

* * * *
Jockeys, seemingly, look for foul riding as a
matter of course, where there is a lot of Ikev Mo’s money
at stake. Said young Foulsham, rider of the Caulfield
Cup favorite to an Argus reporter-” When the barrier
rose I got well away from where I was in the middle of the
field, and seeing Australian with Carson on him I im-
mediately steered for him, as I knew that he would not
interfere with me in any way, whereas I did not know
what some of the others might do to me.” Considering
that every jockey who rides a bona-fide trier is instructed
to avoid bumps and keep out of danger as much as
possible, it follows that Foulsham was dreading “ some
others ’ who were not on the winning job at all, and had
as he thought, probably taken instructions from some-
body desirous of spoiling the favorite’s chance.

On Saturday Randwick had a better day than
was expected as regards the paddock, but the deserted
leger-reserve showed that the crowd has not got over its
prejudice to Randwick as a speculating medium, and the
odds quoted against the winners in Monday's papers
must have reconciled them to their absence from the
scene. Infanta, 7to 1, won the Hurdle Race; Oban, 10 to
1, the Stockbridge Handicap ; Beaulieu, 5 to 1, the Tyro
Stakes ; Keystone, 10 to 1, the Salisbury Stakes ; and
Blanche Florence, 14 to 1, the Welter Handicap. Beau-
lieu, the Tyro winner, is a likely colt by Mr. Long’s old
champion, Grand Flaneur, and his win imposes a penalty
of 51b. in Manbyrnong Plate.

Some kinds of handicapping are hard to under-
stand— e.g., the light imposts awarded the smart pony
Brown Mantle, who has won three times successfully at
Kensington. Her first win was with 6st. 71b., and for'this
success her weight for her next race was made 6st 111 b
an increase of 41b. only. On her third appearance she
was asked to carry 7st. 41b., and again easily beat her
neld.

fame, is cabled. Though of the same family, he is not to

* * * *
Re W. R. Wilson’s suggestion that acceptance
fees for the Cups should be raised to £5O, the voice of
Ikey Mo is heard protesting—“ Why put impediments in
the way of the struggling owner?” and “What’s the good
of trying to reduce the size of the field as long as there is
Tatt.’s sweep-money to induce owners to start duffers on
the off-chance?” . The answers to Ikey’s vociferous ques-
tions are, firstly—the turf ‘doesn’t want struggling
owners; they are the curse of horse-racing in Australia.
The less needy the owner, the more easily he can afford
to be honest. Secondly, the objection to Tatt.’s sweep-
money arrangements is “rot” for the most part. If a
horse had no visible chance, its owner wouldn’t pay £5O
hard cash to start it merely because a ticket-holder had
laid him “something to nothing’’ against the animal for
win or place. Moreover, the public will soon discontinue
the foolishness of making arrangements of ariv sort with
owners. Thirdly, the biggest fields for Caulfield and
Melb. Cup races were in the old days, when sweep induce-
ments didn’t exist. 41 horses started at Caulfield in 1885.
There were 36 runners for Melb. Cup the same year, and
39 for the famous race which Carbine won. Tatt.’s sweep
had nothing to do with the size of the field in that case.
A good many of the horses were started on the off-chance
of their interfering with the great “ dead bird.”
» * * *
The owner of Grafter expressed his annoyance
last week because the Melb. bookies forced his horse into
the position of first favorite for Melb. Cup, a position nob
warranted, according to Mr. Forrester, by the amount of
stable money betted on it. Mr. Forrester, in his own
interests, should keep his feeling of irritation to himself;
it tends to create the impression that the Grafter is the
right pea in the stable, and will have a tendency rather to
shorten than to lengthen the odds.

As here prophesied, the Plover, Sam. Hordern’s
22-footer, showed herself a heeler in a light air last Satur-
day, when she won the Johnstone’s Bay Handicap from
scratch, beating the 18-footer Stella 11., 7 minutes, by a
few feet. The ex-champion Eflfie, off scratch, finished
third.

The Golden Gate Club, Sydney, brought off
another set-to, on Saturday night, between the Kaffir and
Tom Christie, when the difficulty of finding equally-
matched men was again experienced. The Kaffir knocked
his man to a standstill in about half a round.
* * * *
There was nothing startling in Sydney cricket
last Saturday. Leichhardt, last, season’s weak team
playing against Paddington, began well, the two first men
(Diamond and Hornidge) putting together 49; then a rot
set in. The remainder scored from 6to 0, and the innings
closed for 93. Paddington then lost 3 wickets for 148
Alec Mackenzie top with 60. Burwood compiled 157 as
against East Sydney’s 6 wickets for 45. Redfern played
a miserable innings against Glebe of 38 runs, top score 9 •
coming out with a bowling average of 7 for
21 runs. Glebe responded with 1 wicket for 110. Waver-
ey playing North Sydney, made a poor show, Iredale
leading off with 6 ; 5 wickets fell for 21 runs.
These are the charming introductory-
verses to Ogilvie’s book, which The Bulletin
will publish on Ist Nov. :—,
For keen feeling of his position, the re-
cently death-sentenced Archer (for Strathmerton,
Vic. murder) is remarkable amongst alleged cri-
minals. At the police-court his misery was piti-
ful, and during his trial at Benalla he neither ate
nor slept. But the cruellest touch of all was when
brought back to Melb. gaol after sentence.
Archer had had three epileptic fits on the way
down, and had with difficulty braced himself up
to put on the prison-clothes, when a brass band at
the irades Hall opposite the gaol rang out with
Home, oweet Home.” The prisoner immedi-
ately collapsed, and cried like a child for several
minutes.
The big M'Sharry arbitration case—like
the almost as big Robb case—is a monumental
warning against the folly of trying to get too much
out of a good thing. The parties to the ancient
quarrel were the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners
on the one side, and M'Sharry and Maerossan—
the famous Q. politician, John Murtagh Maerossan
long deceased—on the other. M'Sharry, with
Maci ossan as aid and backer, executed a railway
contract, and was not satisfied with the payments.
He claimed some .£130,000 additional, arid after
an arbitration suit which commenced in 1895, and
took 323 days in hearing, he gets ,£l3,ooo—and
"'S °osts are stated at anything from £50,000 to
£60,000. If the Q. N. Bank really backed
M bharry, as alleged, there will be no balm in
Cilead for the shareholders this year, or next or
the year after that. The Bulletin, however has
a great deal of sympathy with M'Sharry. The
issues were not so complicated that the case could
not have been decided in a tenth «f the time, at
a tenth of the cost; and the way in which days
and fees were wasted is a scandalous satire on
arbitration methods. Taw has a bad name ; but
the settlement of the case at law would have been
far cheaper.
Ernest Howard, an Adelaide professional
shorthander, is trying to arrange with the Pit-
mans for a world’s speed competition, in which he
believes he will have a say. Howard finds piano-
playing first-rate exercise for speed-developing.

address for 4/6 in stamps.
AMBERITE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve not been sleeping on the track
To tout the nags at early dawn ;
I don't advise you what to back ;
But, all the same, I mean to pawn
My Sunday suit next Monday night
And risk the stuff on Amberite.
Nine-stone-eleven sounds a lot;
They haven’t “ chucked him in ” the race—
I must admit he hasn’t got.
A soft thing on in any case.
But though his ’andioap ain’t light,
I’ll take my chance with Amberite.
’Cause why? The nag’s a cut above
So many that he'll have to beat;
The “ bit o’ class ” which turfmen love
Is stamped on him from head to feet
He looks the “gentleman ” all right,—
Old Carbine fathered Amberite.
He’s just the sort of boy that takes
Some doin’ in a big event;
He travels best when bonnie stakes
Are urging on his deep intent.
Shakespearian, my language, quite,
When speaking of an Amberite.
Some say he’s had uncommon luck,
Luck comes to them who’ve got the 'eart:
And here’s the point by which I’m struck,
His times were mostly pretty smart.
There’s speed, and stamina, and fight
In lucky ones like Amberite.
Two Derbies, and the Caulfield win—
A Champion in decent time !
His form on paper’s not too thin ;
And, anyway, he fits the rhyme.
A dozen verses one may write
To end, you see, with Amberite.
And when he came in Number Two
At Randwick for the Sydney Cup
The veteran that “ put him through"
Had 18 pounds less burden up.
He beat The Grafter out of sight
At level weights, did Amberite.
The Grafter’s looking great, I’m told ;
Already he is all the rage.
Well, my horse, as a three-year-old,
Could smother him at weight-for-age.
The chance for Clarion looks bright—
Still I shall stick to Amberite.
Will Massinissa stay the course ?
I doubt it; but I like The Chief.
Old Dreamland lacks internal force,
And Wait-a-Bit’s a dandy “ thief” ;
But nothing harsh or impolite
Was ever said of Amberite.
There’s Rosebery, he’ll make some pace
And Ikey Mo will yell his name. ’
Young Cocos, too, might get a place.
I wonder, is Merloolas lame?
I give them all a show in spite
Of my regard for Amberite.
If Reka (wounded in the smash
Which gave the coroner a job)
Can toe the mark, I’ll have a dash
On him for ten or twenty “ bob.”
His colors, too, are black and white.
Reminding one of Amberite.
The smash ! The mention of it sends
A chill through one’s prophetic soul.
The horse that starts wit h many friends
May never reach the winning goal.
A shocking accident may blight
The hopes I pin to Amberite.
Oh, never fear ; the course is wide,
And duffers will be dropping out
Ere comes the rush for berths inside ;
And though Grim Death be thereabout
He’ll need to ride with main and might
To overtake good Amberite.
I see the “hoops” a-flashing round
The final turn in rippin’ style ;
He’s going strong, and gaining ground—
The Grafter wins ! Well, I should smile '
He’ll finish in the foremost flight,
But not in front of Amberite.
I’ve not been sleeping on the track
To tout the nags at dawn of day;
I don’t advise you what to back
(’Tis far the better game to lay)—
But midst the din of punters’ skite
I sing my song of Amberite.
Melb., Oct. 21.
EDMUND FISHER.
FAIR GIRLS AND GRAY HORSES!
Pair Girls and Gray Horses! A toast for you
Who never went wide of a fence or a kiss :
While horses are horses and eyes are blue
There is never a toast in the world like this 1
To all Fair Girls ! For the sake of one
Whose bright blue eyes were awhile ray star,
Whose hair had the rich red gold of the sun
When his kisses fall where the leaf lips are !
To all I'air Girls ! how the red wine fleams
To the glass’s rim as it gleamed that night
In the jewelled hand of my Dame of Dreams—
O, jewelled fingers so soft and white !
To all Fair Girls ! Turn your glasses down.
Here s “ Blissful bridals and long to live ! ”
And if I am slighting your eyes of brown,
O, Gipsies Born of the Night, forgive 1
To all Gray Horses ! Fill up again
For the sake of a gray horse dear to me;
For a foam-fed bit and a snatching rein
And a reaching galloper fast and free !
To all Gra3' horses ! For one steed’s sake
Who has carried me many a journey tall
In the dawn-mists dim when the magpies wake
In the starry haze when the night-dews fall l’
To all Gray horses ! Now drink you deep,
t( For red wine ruins no rider’s nerves :
Light w ork and a long, long after-sleep ! ’*
As each gray horse in the world deserves.
Fair Girls and Gray horses ! To each his way
But golden and gray are the loves to hold :
And it gold tresses must turn to gray
Gray horses need never be turned into gold.

ASSISTANTS Engaged for the Country.
Another coincidence. Both Arthur Orton
and Rougetnont, the champion liars of the last 30
years, have their stage on this tired island. Also,
both took their wares to John Bull and co., and
both seem to have got the fabric from outside
accidental suggestions. There is, however, this
distinction: Orton lied with circumstance and
u * or y ears scores of shrewd men, whereas
the Munchausen exploits of the Swiss had a
gamey flavor from the start.
A curious note sent to The Bulletin
by “C.S.”
*'7* ,llen O'd the other day with the Pythagorean
method (as per an ancient work on auguries) of depicting
toe character and prognosticating the future of a person.
After “trying it on the dog” in the shape of George
Dean, the poisoner, whereby the result panned out
marriage—calamity,” Sulieman Amien, the Sydney
chutney-boss, was experimented with—with the startling
answer, “A long voyage—a cure (for)—ambition.” This
gentleman, ’twill be remembered, foolishly stood for Cook
Division at the general election, and polled eight votes.
Next jammed “ Yes-No” Reid’s name into the prophetic
alembic. Subsequent distillation gave this curiously*
apropos condensation : “ Strength—blindness— error—
affliction.” Supply the needful prepositions and conjunc-
tions, and what could be more accurate? [More convinc-
ing had the oracle said something about grease.—Ed. B.]
Jack Want’s little lot sums up into: “Persecution —
praises (of)—liberty—(and subsequent)catastrophe.” [No-
thing about Japan, or fishing-excursions ! —Ed. B.] Ex-
Minister Garrard will possibly be pleased to hear that, in
a Pythagorean sense, he is just a mixture of “ Struggles
imperfections—diminution and grief.” [Nothing about
Gas ! —Ed. B.] Ex-Minister Sydney Smith is to be an
“adept (in) taciturnity,” which, as he is relegated to
absence from the talking shop, seems to meet the case.
One more example—Albert John Gould : “ Silence—
merciless—errors and catastrophe.” Suffield and Single-
ton, please note. As the above are no fakes, this system
is hereby recommended to aspiring politicians, &e.,
anxious to see themselves as others see them, and also to
know their own fate. John Norton, by the way, works
out: “Combats—struggles— patriotism—misfortune.”

Dear Bulletin.-- I can hardly let pass
such a slur on late Fred Weld’s memory as is con-
tained in “Yang Anyut’s ” story (B. 8/10/’9B). No
one who had travelled in Gov. Weld’s company
could believe that he would drink “ ayer klapa ”
at all, unless largely diluted with whisky, and any
journey of his would have come to an abrupt con-
clusion as soon as the “ s’tengah ” ingredients had
run out. When one M was D.O. at an out-
side station in a certain protected state Weld
(travelling through the country) intended to stay
a night at M——-’s bungalow, and to the latter
came the A.D.C. in advance to inquire into state
of his whisky-and-soda stock, as the gubernatorial
supply had given out. Said M , “Very sorry, !
but I have none. My supplies haven’t come to I
hand.” By Jove, then,” said the A.D.C., “ you’ll ]
have to tell the old man yourself. I daren’t, as he
has been without a ‘ s’tengah ’ for at least two
hours.” When the Gov. arrived, after the pre-
liminary greetings, said M , “Exceedingly
sorry, your Ex., but you are aware that in these
outside districts supplies do not always come to
hand in time. lam therefore unable to offer your
Ex. anything to drink but green cocoanut-water.”
His Ex., after speechless glare of some seconds,
“ Green eocoanut-water be d d, sir ! ” and,
turning to the A.D.C., “Order up those horses
again ; we’ll go on to Blank’s place if it takes us
all night.” And so departed without even an
adieu.— Man in the Lalang.

With reference to certain Boyle Roche-

question are chestnuts; (2) he didn’t use them ;
and (3) the attribution to him of such flowers of
speech was an entire fabrication by Byrnes’ base
myrmidons in the reporters’ gallery. “ The Dela-
tite member,” another correspondent writes, “has
never been forgiven by the Age for his outspoken
attack, on the floor of the House, upon the power
of King David.”
Newnes, of the Lie World Magazine,
now admits that Rougomont is Grien. Newnes
is green too. He adds that the romancer is a liar
unequalled since Defoe—though the crude stu-
pidity of his yarns would get him driven from the
swagmen’s hut with mutton-bones. And as com-
paring him with Defoe ! —it is Defoe’s style , not
his facts or fictions, which has preserved “Robin-
son Crusoe” and the “Journal of the Plague.”

In the Supreme Court
op Queensland.
Matrimonial Jurisdiction.

Mr. Justice Cooper.
Between WILLIAM THOMAS PHELPS,
Plaintiff; and FLORENCE PHELPS,
Defendant; and CHARLES GORDON
McKEOWN, Co-Defendant.
rpO CHARLES GORDON McKEOWN. Take notice
that this action was commenced against you by the
Plaintiff issuing a Supreme Court writ dated the fifteenth
day of July, 1898, and by the filing of his petition for a
dissolution of his marriage with the Defendant, on the
ground of adultery with you. And take notice that the
Court has ordered that, service of the writ, and petition
herein, upon you, shall be effected by the publication of
a notice of the abovenamed action, and by the insertion
of the same once in the Queensland Government Gazette,

Pastor Gribble—if it is the old original
Pastor Gribble whose name still appears in
the papers — must know as much about
the condition and' treatment of the Austra-
lian aboriginals as most people. He has had
many years missionary work amongst them
and has made himself rather unpopular more
than once by his outspoken denunciation of
the white men’s treatment of the blacks. A
good many years back he undertook a mission
to the Gascoyne (W.A.) blacks; after a few
weeks’ work, seeing that they were not being
treated humanely, he visited Perth, and,
having tried in vain to bring about aruenquiry
into the matter, he delivered a lecture detailing
the whole situation. This—as some of the leading
residents of W.A. were in the Gascoyne—raised a
great outcry, and the West Australian, then
Perth’s only paper, severely attacked Mr. Gribble,
who took action for libel—of course unsuccessfully.
For he was, as the West Australian said, “ not
fighting the paper, but the whole colony.” (The
paper’s expenses were subscribed for it mainly by
Gascoyne residents). Truly, as an American
humorist says, “We do not care what is said
against us, so long as it is not true.”

Concerning Melbourne Anarchist Club
Nobody—including its own advocates—has ever taken
the Great Cause seriously. An attempt to do so was
made about eight years ago, when the Anarchists’Society
used to meet in the Alexandra Theatre buildings. The
society never got beyond six active or visible mem-
bers, and at the end consisted of but two, who solemnly
met each Sunday night and read each other long philo-
sophical papers. But the growing desire of each member
to be the reader and not the listener eventually led to an
estrangement, and, after the sole survivor had convened
himself and carried a resolution unanimously expelling
his absent comrade, he was forced to join the Demo-
cratic Club to learn that the cause of revolution
was advanced more successfully by 8-round glove
fights than by the advocacy of bombs and barricades.
The last reunion of the Anarchists took place a couple of
years back, when three turned up at Young and Jackson’s
Hotel to join in the celebration of the Fall of the Bas-
tille. There were altogether about 20 present, and the
speeches which everybody wanted to make and nobody
wanted to listen to were concluded by the singing of 24
original verses of the “Marseillaise.” At the fifth verse
the common or garden democrats gave out; at the
tenth, the Trades Hall representatives cried go ; at the
twelfth, the Socialists girded up their bats and fled ; but
the devoted band of three Anarchists disdained to take
their lOsec. at any time and kept on to the bitter
end. Two were Yarra-bank orators, one old, one young,
and the third was a man of no apparent age—a man with
l° n £ _ yellow hair and a long yellow string-like beard
hanging straight, like a cataract, over all but his eyes and
nose and the gap through which the song escaped. His
eyes were turned to the ceiling, his hands trembled with
excitement, and he wore on his face that sublime serenity
which is seldom seen outside small religious sects. It
was a great night, and when the Yarra-bankers cleared
out, the yellow-haired anarchist yelled “Vive 1’ Anar-
chic !” da capo and da capo until the barman chucked
him out. Bomb-throwers are made of sterner stuff than
these.

“One Way of Love.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ George, I want to speak to you.”
He looked up and saw his wife standing in the
doorway. Her face was set, her eyes bright. He
rose and drew a chair forward for her.
“Let me stand,” she said, “I can talk better
so.”
He looked at her anxiously. “What is it,
Helen ? Is anything wrong 2 ”
“Everything, I am afraid! I have come to
you for help and advice. I want to put the whole
matter in your hands.” She was trembling with
excitement, and as she came forward rested her
hand on the table for support.
“ We must begin at the beginning,” she said,
controlling her voice with difficulty. “ And the
beginning is, that we are not in love with each
other, and have not been for the last five years.”
George Greer looked at her for a moment, then
away out of the open window.
“The next fact is that you love Edith Stafford,
and that she loves you.”
He started, but said nothing.
“ I have known it all along; and, George, believe
me, it has hurt me most terribly—not the loss of
your love ” She laughed drearily. “ But the
fact of your sacrifice ; it seemed so unavailing,
so ”
“ Is it of this only that you have come to speak
to me 2 ”
“ No, not of this only ; though many, many
times I have wanted to speak of it to you, for I
am your wife, George; and also, apart from that I,
have some human sympathies.” .
“ I am sorry that you know this ; it must surely
make things harder in every way. What has at
last led you to speak of it to me ? ” It was char-
acteristic of the man that he made no attempt to
deny or argue the point.
“My own affairs. In this last year I have been
very unhappy. Do not blame yourself, the fault
lies with me if anywhere; but I think fate and
circumstance have been too hard for us.” She
paused a moment, then went on quickly : “ I have
found most consolation and happiness in Carl
Ramer’s company. ” She looked at her husband,
but he only ma'de a gesture for her to continue.
‘I am in love with him, and he with me. We
have realised the truth utterly; what is to be
done? ”
For a moment Greer made no answer ; then he
replied thickly: “You must send him away.”
“I must send him away!” she repeated in a
dull tone.
“ There is nothing else to be done ? ”
“No, there is nothing else.”
For some moments there was complete silence ;
they stood facing one another, looking in a hope-
less way into each other’s eyes.
“ There is no other way ? ” she asked at length.
“ Can nothing be done ? ”
He shook his head. “ You can do nothing but
send him away from you.”
She turned as if to go out of the room. ■■ . _
“Helen, I cannot ask your forgiveness for ,my
part in this. I should have foreseen it, but lam
very sorry, dear ? ” He felt how hopelessly inade-
quate his words were, but knew she would under-
stand all he wanted to express.
She came back towards him. The long, wakeful
night had done more for Carl’s cause than the
most eloquent pleading. She knew she scarcely
dared face the future without him. She felt
utterly helpless, and had come, on an impulse that
she half distrusted, for help to her husband.
“ George, is it worth while wrecking four lives for
. a shadow called Respectability ? Is it worth while
to break four hearts for a col'd something called
Principle ? Must we live a lie for the rest of the
time, so that the world be not offended ? Listen ;
I know what you and Edith suffer. I know what
it is to break your heart and cover the wound. I
know the long days, and longer nights, the wild
crying of one’s nature for some happiness. A cry,
which, in our case, the world says must be stifled.
What ! —may it judge for us, when in judging it
breaks our hearts 2 George, must I send him
away 2”
He was trembling, and answered almost in a
whisper. “ What course could you suggest,
Helen?”
“ That Igo away with him. You need never
see nor hear of us again, and, George—you would
be free! ”
He drew a hard breath through his set teeth.
“It must be right—it must , I say."’ Tears were
streaming down her cheeks, and her voice shook.
“ I have coine to you, because we care for each
other a little still, and ( because you are stronger
than I, and far, far better.”
He made an impatient dissent.
“Ear better,” she repeated; “and I’ll leave
you to decide. I will do what you tell me ; if
you say lam to stay here with you I will do it.
I owe you that.” '
“ You owe me nothing. You must judge for
yourself.” . , ,
“ But I cannot. I only know what 1 want, that
I want it passionately ; but I am strong enough
to bear things, I suppose ?” ■ • . .
“No love on this earth, or beyond it, should
rule us completely.”
She looked up, and could have laughed wit-
right but for the pain she saw in his eyes. Do
you really love when you argue like that 2 Does
it mean everything to you 2 ’’
“ We cannot discuss that.”
She walked slowly towards the mantelpiece, find
laid her arms upon it, resting her chin on her
folded hands. She did not speak for some time,-
then she glanced round at him.
“ The world is very weary; and in search of new
sensation, when it sends a wife to consult her hus-
band as to the feasibility of her elopement. ”
He looked at her sternly. Such speeches jarred
upon him. He could not understand a good woman
uttering them: ■
“I come and ask for your permission. You
refuse it. I obey you. Send the man away, and
we will live unhappily ever after.”
“ You make things no easier to bear by treating
them lightly.”
“Don’t I? I often think I do. It seems to
shift a burden a little ; but I don’t want to appear
flippant to you, for you do not understand. But,
George, duties are very terrible sometimes, they
don’t always fit one’s shoulders squarely ; they rub
and fret, and wear wounds that never heal.”
“ Helen, a thing is either right or wrong ; one
could not deliberately choose the wrong.”
“lam afraid I could; but, apart from that,
who is to judge? Who is to say that a strong,
fierce, overwhelming love is not a better guide
than a worn, world-tutored conscience ?
I cannot argue about it. ’
George, and you love her ! Did she put her
arms round your neck, and lay her cheek against
yours? Were her lips wet with streaming tears
as you kissed her good-bye ”
“ For God’s sake, Helen ! ”
“ Weren’t you even tempted? Didn’t you put
her from you—comforting her broken heart with
the cold cheer ? It is right ? ”
“ Helen ”
She saw he was suffering, saw the sweat gather
on his brow. She came towards him, and laid her
hand on his arm. “ George, defy the world, and
custom, and prejudice Give me my freedom and
take yours. I do not only plead for myself, but
for her. X know what a woman can feel, X ah,
George, think ” ——
“ Helen, no amount of thinking could alter it.
Do you think I have not thought ? ”
“ But you have thought only of cold right and
wrong; and you do not really know which is
right, and which wrong, and you will not think of
her, of her suffering and tears, of the sadness and
loneliness of her life, of how her arms go out for
you, and you are not there ”
“ I will not listen. You are melodramatic.”
She saw he was with greatest difficulty keeping
command of himself.
“Ami—at least I know what a woman can be
made to suffer through a man’s terrible con-
science. Is it so very valuable, this right and
wrong, that it is worth a woman’s broken heart?”
“ Listen to me. I advise you—l certainly can-
not, and will not, command you. You have heard
what I think. Nothing could alter my views, and
even if you took your freedom, I would not take
mine.”
She looked at him wonderingly. “Poor little
Edith,” she said, in a half whisper. It was no use
arguing any further. He was certain he was
right, and in her heart she believed he was.
“There is nothing more to be said, then?”
She felt numb and weary, and wondered vaguely
if the life she must lead would he even possible.
“ Helen ! he spoke hesitatingly, and as if the
effort was great. “ I blame myself for all this,
I would give my life, were it any use. I thank you
for coming to me. I knew my wife was an honor-
able woman, and I am proud of your strength,
and of your trust in,me. You are strong enough
to go though with life as it is. Do. not be led
away. Let us help each other if we can. We
will leave Australia. I say nothing of Ramer’s
career, which would be ruined if nor of our
own relations. I only look at bare right and
wrong, and, as there is a God above, I knew it
would be wrong. Brave it out: we have a strange
and sad secret, to hide from the world, but I do
not fear for you. I do liot offer you 1 sympathy,
but ,I will help you if I can.”
He paused ; the sentences had been broken, dis-
connected. He held out his hand, and his voice
gave for a moment. “ Helen, my poor girl !” he
said.
She took his hand and looked into his eyes ; her
expression was cold and grave. “ I will do my
best, as you have done,” she answered.

Ferguson, M.L.A. (N.S. W.): “ The time
a man wants a drink most is just before going to
church .on Sunday. The hon. member, Mr.
Moles worth, is in the habit of going to church
without being fortified, and it is killing him by
inches.”
S.A. Assembly decided, after a hard
struggle, not to include butchers among the people
who are to close at the statutory hour. But, after
all, it isn’t the hour at which the butcher-man
closes which is of most importance to the public.
The great essential is that the dog shouldn’t be
shut in with the meat when he does close.

Purnamoota, the fore-runner of Broken
Hill, has existed only on Govt, maps for the last
half-dozen years. Plans still showed it a thorough-
going township, with all the usual trimmings of
common and pound and cemetery and so on. But
now even these have been swept away by a
few unrecognisable Gazette notices; and a
selector has taken the whole lot up in the vain
hope that a few goats and cows will be able to
chew enough mulga-leaves and old prospectors’
ancient boots to keep themselves and him alive.
So Purnamoota, which Speaker Abbott, then
Mines Minister, tried to get changed to ’Moota
because he feared that people would confuse the
original native name with Parramatta, fades
further off the maps.
A kleptomaniac of : 30 years standing has
been arrested at Oama'ru (M.L.), and the law has
jmoceeded, as usual, to minister to his diseased
intellect by putting him in gaol. The following
collection was found on his premises : 40 hedge-
knives, 2 garden hose, 1 wire strainer, 11 gorse-
cutters, 11 crowbars, 1 pair leggings, 6 scythe
stones, 1 door mat, 6 balls twine, 1 horse rug, 2
choppers, 14 adzes, 14 brushes, 3 tape measures,
3 rules, 12 shirts, 1 tent, 2 surveyor’s chains, 21
picks, 8 tomahawks, 38 spades, 20 shovels, 35
garden forks, 31 hoes, 17 rakes, 26 hammers, 18
saws, 1 spokeshave, 15 axes, 2 squares, 1 plane, 5
trowels, 1 dish, 7 door-handles, 2 chisels, 400 books.
He never sold anything, but lived alone in a cot-
tage with his accumulated property.


The New York Legislature lately held a
special sitting to pass an Act enabling citizens
absent on war-duty to record their votes for New
York in whatever part of America or the West
Indies they might happen to be. This was done
without difficulty, yet Vic. Assembly hesitates to
pass its Absent Voters’ Bill, which accords similar
privilege to sailors and shearers, and professes not
to see how the thing can be done. No will, no
way.

“Six boxes cured me,” said Mr. Carl Mont M‘Croy, of 204 George-
street, Sydney, to a reporter, “and that was after I had been
attended by doctors in vain, and tried several different sorts of
medicines. My heart trouble was, I suppose, the most serious. I
used to be seized with violent spasms in the left breast, which nearly
doubled me up in agony. After these fits I would be panting and
quite exhausted, and my heart would palpitate frightfully for more
than half-an-hour. In addition to this, I had a lot of trouble with
my kidneys. .There was a continual, dull, leaden, dragging pain in
the lower portion of my back, which was especially bad after I had
been lying in bed. Nothing would make my kidneys act properly.
My heart and my kidneys got worse and worse, notwithstanding
the most careful attention of my doctors. For five months I lay
on a bed of sickness. I had fallen away to a shadow, and my bones
nearly came through my skin. When 'I felt there was no hope left
for me, I read a story of a remarkable cure by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. I asked for them, and with some trouble I managed to get
them. It was after taking a few pills that I had for the first time
for months a really refreshing sleep. I procured some more pills,
and at the end of a fortnight I could see 1 was making rapid head-
way towards recovery. The distressing symptoms of my kidney
complaint began to abate, and my heart almost ceased to trouble
me. Day by day I got better, and after taking six boxes I was
completely cured. My recovery was very rapid, for in ten weeks’
time I was as well as ever I was, and have never had a single
symptom of heart or kidney disease since.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are sold only in a white package, upon
which is printed in red ink the full name, “Dr. Williams’Pink
Pills for Pale People.” Do not accept pills, even if colored pink,

How Influenza Weakens.
STRENGTH, VIGOR, AND BEAUTY GIVEN TO A
SUEEERING GIRL.
The after-effects of influenza made Miss Jamieson, of Autumn-
street, Ashby, Geelong, Victoria, exceedingly weak and wretched
Below she tells a startling narrative.
“Some few years ago,” she told a reporter, “ I suffered from a
very severe attack of influenza. I could never get rid of its after-
effects until I tried Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
Although generally experiencing robust health, when at last I was
able to leave my room I was almost of ghostly proportions. But in
March of last year I commenced to experience great inconvenience
from pains in my right side. I consulted a medical man, who said
that it was merely the after-effects of influenza, and I was not to
worry myself. But, as the pains continued, I went to Melbourne
to consult a specialist. He informed me it was partial paralysis
He put me through treatment that left me as I was, and I returned
home fairly worn out. Whether the worry or the heroic treatment
I had undergone was responsible or not I will not venture an opinion
upon, but a week afterwards erysipelas set in on my face, and for
months I suffered the greatest agony. In January a friend (a Miss
Evans, of Eaglehawk, near Bendigo) came down to see me, and one
day she brought me a box of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People. When visiting Melbourne I took them, and four days
after the pain commenced to go away. I continued taking them
for some time, and, in all, I used eight boxes, discontinuing them
in August. From then until now I have not had the least symptom
of a return of either complaints—complaints that I had not been
free from for four years. lam 22 years of age. Scores know my
sufferings, and the means of my recovery I am only too pleased to
supply.”
Obtainable from all chemists and dealers, or from the Dr
Williams Medicine Co., Sydney, N.S.W., who will forward six
boxes for 15s. 3d,, or one box for 2s. 9d., post free.

Babel Town.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(“ Dwellers in citydom—children of Babel.”— Richter.)
When the breath of misty morning wakes the
fever in your blood,
Have ye felt the Babel frenzy in your brain?
Have ye felt her fury lift you like a feather down
the flood
And her subtle virus coursing through your vein ?
Have ye heard it coming down
Ev’ry street and alley brown,
The rout and roar of crazy, murky, mazy Babel
town ?
Hid ye say the fight was flagging and the times
were growing tame
Because we’d left behind the bow and spear?
Is a man the less a Hunter if he follow Human
game
Than the man who drank the life-blood of the
deer ?
Hark ! the chase is coming down
Every street and alley brown,
To the cursed roar eternal of infernal Babel Town.
And I hear the city murmur in a sombre under-
tone :
Ye must battle in my by-ways for your bread ;
Ye shall grind yourselves to powder, thew and
sinew, blood and bone,
And the Dust shall fill my nostrils from your
Dead;
Ye shall tremble at my frown,
Ye shall greet me in my Crown,
V e shall love me and no other—Queen and
Mother—Babel Town ! ”
Did ye mark the mob a-dancing in the merry
Babel-street,
While the gutters ran between with ripple red ?
Did ye see the fierce Bacchante with her flying
hair and feet;
Did ye see the craven smite the hoary head ?
Did ye hear the thunder drown
Ev’ry cry for mercy down,
When her ranks without a tether came together—
, Babel Town.
Have ye heard the homes a-singing on the city’s
outer rim ?
( di, the gentle isles that dot a stormy sea !)
Have ye heard her temples echo with the peal of
holy hymn
And her halls unholy chanting in their glee ?
Have ye seen the Night come down
rn-n Dn the dusty ways and brown
lill a respite brief and eerie broods on weary
Babel Town ?
O, the magic of the mother ! Could we linger if
she lead us ?
Lo, her fingers move our heart-strings when they
play !
Her voice above all other ! Could we falter if
she need us ?
In her passion she is Potter—we are Clay ;
Things of clay—she draws us down,
Prince and pauper, clod and clown,
lo the merry madd’ning medley down in deadly
Babel Town !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
On Our Selection.—A Scheme of
Dad’s.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dad used to say that Shingle Hut was the finest
selection on Darling Downs; but we never could
see anything fine about it—except the weather in
drought time, or Dad’s old saddle mare. She was
very fine. The house was built in a gully so that
the bailiffs (I suppose) or the blacks—who were
mostly dead —couldn’t locate it. An old wire-
fence, slanting all directions, staggered past the
front door. At the rear, its feet almost in the back
door, sloped a barren ridge, formerly a squatter’s
sheep-yard. For the rest there were sky, wallaby-
scrub, gum-trees, and some acres of cultivation.
But Dad must have seen something in it, or he
wouldn’t, after he had knocked off work' of an
evening, have stood feasting his eyes on the
wooded waste. In all his wanderings^—and Dad
had been almost everywhere—swimming flooded
creeks and rivers, humping his swag from one end
of Australia to the other—at all games going
except bank-managing and bush ranging--he had
seen no place timbered like Shingle Hut.
“Why,” he used to say, “it’s a fortune in it-
self. Hold on till the country gets populated, and
firewood is scarce ; there’ll be money in it then-
mark my words.”
Poor Dad ! I wonder how long he expected to
live ?
At the back of Shingle Hut was a tract : of
Government land—mostly mountains—marked on
the map, the Great Dividing Range. Splendid
country, Dad considered it— beautiful country—
and part of a grand scheme he had in his head; A
wild place. Giant hills and lofty peaks ; mighty
gorges that seemed asleep, so silent were they..
Bush it was indeed—weird, ghostly, magnificent
bush !
* * * *
The day had been hot. Inside, the mosquitoes
were bad ; and, after supper, Dad and Dave were
outside, lying on some bags. They had been
grubbing that day, and were tired. The night
was nearly dark. Dad lay upon his back, watching
the stars ; Dave upon his stomach, his head rest-
ing on his arms. Both silent. One of the draught-
horses cropped the couch-grass round about them.
Now and again a flying-fox circled noiselessly
overhead, and “ Mopoke !—Mopoke ! ” came dis-
mally from the ridge and from out the lonely-
looking gully. A star fell, lighting up a portion
of the sky, but Dad remarked it not. In a while
he said:—
“How old a,re you, Dave?” Dave made a
mental calculation before answering.
“ S’pose I must be 18 now . . . Why?”
A silence.
“I’ve been thinking of that land at the back—
if we had that I believe we could make money.”
“ Yairs—if we had.”
Another silence.
“ Well, I mean to have it, and that before very
long. ”
Dave raised his head, and looked towards Dad.
“ There’s four of you old enough now to take up
land, and where could you get better country than
that out there for cattle? Why ” (turning on his
side and facing Dave) “ with a thousand acres of
that stocked with cattle and this kept under culti-
vation we’d make money—we’d be rich in a very
few years.”
Dave raised himself on his elbow.
“ Yairs—with cattle,” he said.
“Just so—(Dad sat up with enthusiasm) but to
get the land is the first thing, and that’s easy
enough only (lowering his voice) it’ll have to be
done quietly and without letting everyone ’round
know we’re going inforit.” (“Oh ! yairs, o’course, ”
from Dave). “ Then (and Dad lifted his voice and
leaned over) run a couple of wires round it, put
every cow we’ve here on it straight away ; get
another one or two when the barley’s sold, and let
them breed.”
“ ’Bout how many’d that be t’ start ’n ? ”
“Well, eight good cows at the least—plenty,
too. It’s simply wonderful how cattle breed if
they’re let alone. Look at Murphy, for instance.
Started on that place with two young heifers —
those two old red cows that you see knocking
about now. They’re the mothers of all his cattle.
Anderson just the same. . . . Why, God bless my
soul ! we would have a better start than any one
of them ever had—by a long way.”
Dave sat up. He began to share Dad’s enthu-
siasm.
“ Once get it stocked, and all that is to be done
then is simply to look after the fence, ride about
among the cattle every day, see they’re right,
brand the calves and every year muster the mob,
draft out the fat bullocks, whip them into town
and get our seven and eight pounds a head for
them.”
“That’d suit me down to the ground, ridin’
about after cattle,” Dave said.
“ Yes, get our seven and eight pounds, maybe
nine or ten pounds a-piece. And could ever we
do that pottering about on this place?” Dad
leaned over further and pressed Dave’s knee with
his hand.
“ Mind you” (in a very confidential tone) “ I’m
not at all satisfied the way we’re dragging along
here. It’s utter nonsense, and, to speak the truth ”
(lowering his voice again) “ I’ve been sick of the
whole damn thing long ago. ”
A minute or two passed.
“It wouldn’t matter,” Dad continued, “ if there
was no way of doing better ; but there is. The
thing only requires to be done, and why not do it ?”
He paused for an answer.
“Well,” Dave said, “let us commence it
straight off—t’morrer. It’s the life that’d suit
me.”
“Of course it would . . . and there’s money in
it ... no mistake about it ! ”
A few minutes passed. Then they went inside,
and Dad took mother into his confidence, and
they sat up half the night discussing the scheme.
* * * *
Twelve months later. The storekeeper was at
the house wanting to see Dad. Dad wasn’t at
home. He never was when the storekeeper came
—he generally contrived to be away, up the pad-
dock somewhere or amongst the corn—if any was
growing. The storekeeper waited an hour or so,
but Dad didn’t turn up. When he was gone,
though, Dad walked in and asked mother what
he had said. Mother was seated on the sofa,
troubled-looking.
“He must be paid by next week,” she said,
bursting into tears, “or the place ’ll be sold over
our heads.”
Dad stood with his back to the fire-place, his
hands locked behind him, watching the flies
swarming on the table.
Dave came in. He understood the situation at
a glance. The scene was not new to him. He
sat down, leant forward, picked a straw off the
floor and twisted it rouml and round his finger,
reflecting.
Little Bill put his head on mother’s lap and
asked for a piece of bread. ... He asked a second
time.
“ There is no bread, child,” she said.
“ But me wants some, mamma.”
Dad went outside and Dave followed. They
sat on their heels, their backs to the barn, thought-
fully studying the earth.
“ It’s the same thing Dad said, reproachfully
from one year’s end to the other ! . . . alwuz
a biH !
“Thought last year we’d be over all this by
now ! ” from Dave.
“So we could . . . can now ... It only wants
that land to be taken up ; and, as I’ve said often
and often, these cows taken ”
Dad caught sight of the storekeeper coming
back, and ran into the barn.
* • • *
Another six months later. Dinner about ready.
“ Take up a thousand acres,” Dad was saying ;
“ take it up ”
He was interrupted by a visitor.
“ Are you Mister Ross ? ” Dad said he was.
“ Well, er—l’ve a fi. fa. against y’.”
Dad didn’t understand.
The Sheriff’s officer drew a document from his
inside breast-pocket and proceeded to read :
“To Mister James Williams, my bailiff. Greet-
ing : By virtue of Her Majesty’s writ of fieri
facias, to me directed, I command you that of the
goods and chattels, money, bank-note or notes or
other property of Murtagh Colin Joseph Duncan
M‘Gregor Ross, of Shingle Hut, in my bailiwick,
you cause to be made the sum of =£4o 10s., with
interest thereon,” &c.
Dad understood.
Then the bailiff’s man rounded up the cows and
the horses, and Dad and the lot of us leant
against the fence and in sadness watched Polly
and old Poley and the rest for the last time pass
out the slip-rails.
“That puts an end to the land business ! ” Dave
said gloomily. •
But Dad never spoke.
